201 of 2888 DOCUMENTS


Leon v. Ohio Bd. of Psychology


No. 91-229


Supreme Court of Ohio


63 Ohio St. 3d 683; 1992-Ohio-105; 590 N.E.2d 1223; 1992 Ohio LEXIS 992


January 22, 1992, Submitted  
May 27, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Court of Appeals for Cuyahoga County, No. 57256.

Appellant, Julian P. Leon, was granted licensure in psychology by appellee, Ohio Board of Psychology ("board"), in 1973. On June 6, 1988, the board issued a "Notice of Opportunity for Hearing" to appellant with respect to allegations raised by a former female client, hereinafter referred to as "Client X," concerning appellant's violation of R.C. Chapter 4732 and the Ohio rules governing the professional conduct of psychologists set forth in the Ohio Administrative Code. Subsequently, a hearing before the board was conducted on July 29 and 30, 1988 for the purposes of exploring the allegations raised by Client X.

After receiving testimony and numerous exhibits, the board made findings of facts and conclusions of law which resulted in an order dated August 18, 1988, revoking appellant's license to practice psychology. In its report, the board made the following findings of fact:

"Mr. Leon had a therapeutic relationship with Client X in July, 1984 through November of 1985. Prior to the initiation of the therapeutic relationship with Client X, Mr. Leon served as her teacher and personal growth group leader.

 [***2] "Beginning about June, 1983 until about July, 1984, Client X was involved in the Gestalt Institute of Cleveland's year-long Post-Graduate Training program. Prior to the year-long program, Mr. Leon served as Client X's teacher in one or more workshops.

"During the year-long training program, Mr. Leon was involved with Client X as a personal growth group leader and as a teacher.

"In March of 1986, Mr. Leon entered into a non-professional social relationship with Client X. Said relationship began with telephone calls, lunches and other social engagements, proceeding to a sexual relationship by June of 1986.

"Mr. Leon knew, based on his own experience with other clients, that the therapeutic relationship extends for a period of time after cessation of formal appointments and that a therapist's influence does not end on the date of the last formal appointment.

"Mr. Leon failed to recognize the influence which he had over students; failed to recognize the impact of his role as a therapist; failed to identify the condition of Client X during treatment and at the time the personal relationship began; failed to recognize the subsequent distress of Client X and did not identify clearly [***3] what 'ex-client' status was."

Upon these findings of fact, the board concluded that appellant was negligent in his practice of psychology contrary to R.C. 4732.17(E), for failing to avoid a dual relationship with a client which would impair his professional judgment. The board further found that contrary to R.C. 4732.17(G), appellant violated several rules of professional conduct, including having a sexual relationship with an "immediate ex-client" in violation of Ohio Adm.Code 4732-17-01(A)(2)(d). The board also found that appellant violated Ohio Adm. Code 4732-17-10(A)(2)(c) by exploiting the trust or dependency of a client, and that his actions amounted to a failure to recognize the boundaries of his competence in violation of Ohio Adm.Code 4732-17-01(B)(1).

Upon appeal, the court of common pleas reversed and vacated the board's order revoking appellant's license, as not being supported by reliable, probative and substantial evidence. The trial court stated that the relationship between appellant and Client X "* * * was one that was initiated and pursued to a great degree by Client X herself." The court further found that "* * * a significant amount of time had elapsed since [***4] the final professional encounter between Appellant and Client X and the beginning of their social relationship." The trial court concluded that appellant had violated neither the statutes nor the rules governing the professional conduct of psychologists.

Upon further appeal, the court of appeals reversed and remanded the cause for reinstatement of the board's revocation order. The appellate court reasoned that the trial court merely substituted its judgment for that of the board and abused its discretion in reversing the board's revocation order. Upon a review of the entire record, the court of appeals held that there was substantial, reliable and probative evidence to support the board's conclusion that Client X was an immediate ex-client and that appellant engaged in a dual relationship in violation of Ohio Adm.Code 4732-17-01(A)(2)(d).

The cause is now before this court pursuant to the allowance of a motion to certify the record.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant psychologist sought review of a decision from the Court of Appeals for Cuyahoga County (Ohio) which reversed a trial court's decision reversing and vacating appellee licensing board's decision to revoke the psychologist's professional license for having a sexual relationship with an immediate ex-client.


OVERVIEW: The psychologist had a sexual relationship with a client seven months after her treatment terminated. The licensing board found that the psychologist violated Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4732.17(E), (G) and Ohio Admin. Code § 4732-17-01(A)(2)(d) (amended 1990), and revoked the psychologist's license to practice. The trial court reversed the licensing board's decision. The appellate court reinstated the licensing board's revocation. The court affirmed the appellate court's decision. First, the use of the term "immediate ex-client" in § 4732-17-01(A)(2)(d) was not vague, and its definition was determined on a case-by-case basis. After reviewing the evidence, the licensing board reasonably found that the seven months between the termination of the patient's therapy and the start of the relationship made the patient an "immediate ex-client," with whom the psychologist was prohibited from having sexual relations. Second, the appellate court applied the correct standard of review to the trial court's decision, giving due deference to the licensing board's weighing of the evidence.


OUTCOME: The appellate court's decision which reversed the trial court's decision reversing and vacating the licensing board's decision to revoke the psychologist's professional license was affirmed. First, the regulation that prohibited the psychologist from having a sexual relationship with his "immediate ex-client" was not vague for lacking temporal specificity. Second, the appellate court properly gave due deference to the licensing board's findings.

 

202 of 2888 DOCUMENTS


Pater v. Pater


No. 90-2447


Supreme Court of Ohio


63 Ohio St. 3d 393; 588 N.E.2d 794; 1992 Ohio LEXIS 651


December 10, 1991, Submitted  
April 15, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Court of Appeals for Hamilton County, No. C-890553.

On January 19, 1988, Robert A. Pater, plaintiff-appellee, filed an action to divorce Jennifer M. Pater, defendant-appellant, in the Domestic Relations Division of the Court of Common Pleas of Hamilton County. Plaintiff's complaint included a request for custody of the couple's son, Bobby Pater. Bobby was born on July 9, 1985 and was three years old when the court awarded custody.

The court held the custody hearing on August 8 and August 17, 1988. There was no dispute that both parties were conscientious and loving parents. Jennifer had quit her job to care for Bobby when he was born and had recently resumed working as a dental technician for approximately twenty hours a week. She testified that her sister lived nearby and would be able to watch Bobby while she (Jennifer) was at work. Robert Pater worked full-time and testified that his mother and sister-in-law would be available to watch Bobby while he (Robert) was at work.

The record demonstrates that the major issue in this case was the effect that Jennifer's religious practices would have on Bobby if the court granted custody to her.  [***2] Jennifer had recently converted to the Jehovah's Witnesses religion, while her husband remained a member of the Roman Catholic church. Early in the hearing, the judge stated that he "would not make a decision based purely on the religeous [sic] aspects of this case." On the second day of the hearing, the judge stated that the issue was whether the mother would indoctrinate her child in her religion, and was "that going to in some way adversely affect the moral, mental, and physical health and judgement [sic] of this child adversely, so as not to be in the child's best interests." The hearing proceeded as follows.

Several family members, neighbors, and friends testified that Robert was an affectionate and competent father. The remainder of plaintiff's case concerned Jennifer's religious beliefs. Plaintiff's counsel called Jennifer as a hostile witness, and questioned her at great length about whether Jehovah's Witnesses celebrate holidays, associate with non-members, participate in extracurricular activities and social organizations, salute the flag, and sing the national anthem.

Counsel also called two expert witnesses to prove that the child would be harmed if he [***3] were raised as a Jehovah's Witness. Dr. Gerald Bergman, Ph.D., a former Jehovah's Witness, testified about religious doctrine and his experiences growing up as a Jehovah's Witness. He also concluded, on the basis of his dissertation, that mental illness occurs more frequently among the Jehovah's Witnesses than it does in the general population. Dr. Cynthia Denber, a clinical psychologist, who had not interviewed Bobby, stated that extracurricular activities and exposure to different cultural and religious beliefs are beneficial to a child's development. She stated that conflict between the parents' religious beliefs can upset a child. Robert testified that on one occasion Bobby said that "church is bad," then cried and said that he was confused.

In addition to the extensive discussion of Jennifer's religion in her own and Dr. Bergman's testimony, other witnesses made repeated references to the fact that she no longer celebrated holidays or attended the Catholic church. During closing argument, plaintiff's counsel frequently read from Jehovah's Witnesses' publications and interjected his own interpretation of the text.

Defense counsel called several of Jennifer's relatives and [***4] associates to testify on her behalf. In addition to testifying about Jennifer's close relationship with Bobby, these witnesses rebutted plaintiff's case by testifying in detail about the Jehovah's Witnesses. Donald Swartley, an elder at the Kingdom Hall where Jennifer attended services, testified exclusively about the Witnesses' beliefs and was questioned briefly by the court about those beliefs on two separate occasions. Steve Lambers, the son of a Roman Catholic father and a Jehovah's Witness mother, testified about his experiences growing up with parents of different religions. On cross-examination, plaintiff's counsel asked each Jehovah's Witness who testified whether he believed that all information in the Witnesses' publications was absolutely true.

The trial court awarded custody to Robert on September 26, 1988. Jennifer filed a Motion for Findings of Fact and Conclusions of Law on October 7, 1988. On November 7, 1988, Robert moved to strike that motion because it was not filed timely. The court never took any action on either motion.

The court heard Jennifer's request for visitation on December 8, 1988. At that hearing, the judge told Jennifer that he would not grant [***5] visitation rights if she was going to teach the child her religion or take the child to Kingdom Hall. After being assured that she would follow the court's instructions, the judge set a visitation schedule which included the restriction that "defendant shall not teach or expose the child to the Jehovah[']s Witnesses' beliefs in any form."

The court of appeals affirmed the domestic court's custody and visitation orders and found no abuse of discretion by the trial court because "the child would be less likely to receive proper medical attention, 1 obtain a college education, or participate in social activities" if Jennifer were to be granted custody.


1 The testimony regarding the Jehovah's Witnesses' belief that blood transfusions are prohibited was excluded at the custody hearing as irrelevant due to its speculative nature. There was no other testimony that would support this finding.

The cause is now before this court pursuant to the allowance of a motion to certify the record.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed and cause remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant mother sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals for Hamilton County (Ohio), which granted custody of their child to plaintiff father. Defendant alleged that the decision was based on her religious preferences.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff and defendant were members of the Roman Catholic church. Defendant converted to the Jehovah's Witness religion. They divorced and both parents sought custody of their minor child. The court reversed and remanded the trial court's decision. The court held that a domestic relations court could consider the religious practices of the parents in order to protect the best interests of a child, but could not evaluate the merits of religious doctrine or define the contents of that doctrine. The court held that there was no evidence that defendant's religion would harm the child. The court held that there was no evidence that the restrictions that would prohibit the child from exhibiting national pride, participating in extracurricular activities, or celebrating holidays would harm the child and that the fact that the child would be different was not a showing of harm. The court held that the lower court could not restrict defendant from teaching her child about her religion.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the lower court's decision because the court considered evidence of defendant's religion, there was not evidence that the religion would harm the child, and the court impermissibly prohibited defendant from teaching her religion to her child.

 

203 of 2888 DOCUMENTS


State ex rel. Rear Door Bookstore v. Tenth Dist. Court of Appeals


Nos. 90-1941, 91-995


Supreme Court of Ohio


63 Ohio St. 3d 354; 588 N.E.2d 116; 1992 Ohio LEXIS 616


February 26, 1992, Submitted  
April 8, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: In Mandamus.

Appeal from the Court of Appeals for Franklin County, No. 90AP-986.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporation sought review of the decision of the Franklin County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio) which found that their place of business a public nuisance, as that term is defined in Ohio Rev. Code § 3767.01. The trial court had also ordered the closure of the corporation's bookstore for a period of one year from the date of the entry and ordered that the personal property used in conducting the nuisance be removed.


OVERVIEW: A corporation's adult bookstore was found to be a "public nuisance," as that term was defined in Ohio Rev. Stat. § 3767.01. The corporation contended that there were numerous trial errors including failure to dismiss the complaint on the grounds that the terms "lewdness" and "assignation" appearing in the state nuisance statute were unconstitutionally vague and overbroad, in violation of the due process clauses of the federal and Ohio constitutions. The court stated that its analysis began with the assumption that legislative enactments were entitled to all reasonable presumptions, interpretations, and rules of construction consistent with the constitutionality of the statute. The corporation argued that the dictionary definitions of "lewdness" were nothing more than a string of synonyms that added little to establishing a clear meaning for the term. The court disagreed and affirmed the judgment because the statute went beyond moral legislation, which in and of itself was a permissible area of regulation, and was directed at the health of patrons of an establishment open to the general adult public.


OUTCOME: Appellants' assignments of error were overruled and the judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

 

204 of 2888 DOCUMENTS


Cincinnati v. Bawtenheimer


No. 90-2315


Supreme Court of Ohio


63 Ohio St. 3d 260; 586 N.E.2d 1065; 1992 Ohio LEXIS 396


December 11, 1991, Submitted  
March 18, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Court of Appeals for Hamilton County, No. C-890609.

Plaintiff-appellant, city of Cincinnati, appeals from the judgment of the First District Court of Appeals which upheld a Hamilton County Municipal Court order dismissing charges against defendant-appellee, Ralph Bawtenheimer, that he had failed to produce his tax records or federal income tax returns after being subpoenaed pursuant to a Cincinnati ordinance.

The facts giving rise to this appeal are that appellee received a subpoena duces tecum from the Cincinnati Tax Commissioner requiring him to appear at the city's Tax Department with books, records and federal income tax returns for the period 1983 through 1987. The subpoena was issued in order to determine if appellee owed income tax to the city for this period.

On or about December 20, 1988, appellee appeared at the Tax Department but failed to produce the requested books, records and tax returns, refused to make any reference to the existence of such records, and sought immunity concerning these items. Appellee's refusal was expressly based upon his claimed Fifth Amendment right against self-incrimination. Appellee was apparently denied the [***2] benefit of immunity and he thereupon left the Tax Department without releasing the requested information. Appellee was subsequently charged, in a complaint, with refusing to "permit the Tax Commissioner or any duly authorized agent or employee to examine the books, records, papers and federal income tax returns of such person." The criminal charge was brought pursuant to Section 311-99 of the Cincinnati Municipal Code, which in pertinent part mandates:

"No person shall:

"* * *

"(e) Refuse to permit the tax commissioner or any duly authorized agent or employee to examine the books, records, papers and federal income tax returns of such person or such person's employer; or

"* * *

"(h) Fail to comply with the provisions of this chapter or any order or subpoena of the tax commissioner[.]

"* * *

"Whoever violates any provision set forth above shall be guilty of a misdemeanor of the first degree and may be assessed such penalties, including fines and/or imprisonment, as the same are set forth in Sections 2929.21 and 2929.31 of the Ohio Revised Code, whichever may be applicable."

The matter came before the trial court upon appellee's motion to dismiss claiming that the relevant [***3] portions of the Cincinnati Municipal Code were unconstitutional, in that they violate appellee's rights as protected by the Fifth Amendment to the United States Constitution. Specifically, according to appellee, the ordinance would require him to produce records which are testimonial in nature, and require him to give testimony regarding such books and records as well as his federal tax returns.

The trial court, after oral presentation of the matter and briefing, found in favor of appellee and dismissed the charge. The trial court, relying on United States v. Cates (D.Md.1988), 686 F.Supp. 1185, held that the city could not compel Bawtenheimer to produce books, records and federal tax returns in that it would violate his Fifth Amendment right against self-incrimination. Accordingly, the trial court found that, as applied in this case, Section 311-99(e) of the Cincinnati Municipal Code is unconstitutional.

The Court of Appeals for Hamilton County, also relying upon Cates, supra, and upon Lefkowitz v. Turley (1973), 414 U.S. 70, 94 S.Ct. 316, 38 L.Ed.2d 274; and Mathis v. United States (1968), 391 U.S. 1, 88 S.Ct. 1503, 20 L.Ed.2d 381, affirmed the trial court's [***4] dismissal of the charge.

This cause is now before the court pursuant to the allowance of a motion to certify the record.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of an order from the First District Court of Appeals (Ohio), which upheld a trial court order dismissing charges against appellee taxpayer based on appellee's failure to produce tax returns after being subpoenaed pursuant to a local ordinance.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city appealed from the judgment which upheld a trial court order dismissing charges against appellee taxpayer. The charges alleged that appellee failed to produce his tax records or federal income tax returns after being subpoenaed pursuant to a local ordinance. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that the trial court could have lawfully found, as it did, that although appellee was involved in a civil proceeding, the information obtained by forcing him to produce certain tax records would more than likely have resulted in some type of criminal filing against him for not reporting and paying his income tax. The court found that if appellant in the case sub judice had granted transactional immunity to appellee, it could have availed itself of the tax materials requested and the taxpayer would not have been under a threat of criminal charges as a consequence of providing such materials.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed an order dismissing appellant city's charges against appellee taxpayer for failure to produce tax records. The court held that the information obtained by forcing appellee to produce the tax records would more than likely have resulted in a criminal filing against appellee.

 

205 of 2888 DOCUMENTS


Fairview General Hosp. v. Fletcher


No. 91-315


Supreme Court of Ohio


63 Ohio St. 3d 146; 586 N.E.2d 80; 1992 Ohio LEXIS 274


January 22, 1992, Submitted  
March 11, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied April 22, 1992, Reported at 1992 Ohio LEXIS 687.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Court of Appeals for Franklin County, No. 90AP-726.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed and cause remanded with instructions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Director of the Ohio Department of Health challenged a judgment of the Court of Appeals for Franklin County (Ohio), which affirmed the grant of summary judgment to appellee hospital and declared that the certificate of need law was inapplicable to appellee's request for redesignation of the level of its neonatal intensive care unit. It also ruled that appellant exceeded his authority when he applied the law to appellee's request.


OVERVIEW: Appellee hospital requested a redesignation of the level of its neonatal intensive care unit and appellant Director of the Ohio Department of Health determined that the change was a "reviewable activity" to which the certificate of need (CON) statutes applied. Appellant subsequently denied appellee's application. Appellee filed an administrative appeal which it voluntarily dismissed before a judgment was issued. The instant action was filed and the trial court granted summary judgment to appellee, declaring the CON laws inapplicable. The appellate court affirmed and the court reversed. The court found that declaratory relief was unnecessary to the preservation of appellee's rights because the CON statutes provided appellee with an adequate legal remedy and that appellee presented no constitutional issues. Accordingly, appellee should have exhausted its administrative remedies prior to filing this action. Appellant did not exceed his authority because the determination of whether a particular activity was reviewable was within his statutory duties. The court held that appellee's complaint should have been dismissed and its request for summary judgment denied.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the grant of summary judgment to appellee hospital and remanded for dismissal of appellee's complaint for failure to exhaust administrative remedies. Because the administrative remedies adequately preserved appellee's rights and there were no constitutional issues, declaratory relief was inappropriate. Appellant Director of the Ohio Department of Health had authority to determine whether a particular activity was reviewable.

 

206 of 2888 DOCUMENTS


State ex rel. Taft v. Court of Common Pleas


No. 91-1240


Supreme Court of Ohio


63 Ohio St. 3d 190; 586 N.E.2d 114; 1992 Ohio LEXIS 279


November 12, 1991, Submitted  
March 11, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: In Prohibition.

On June 19, 1991, relators, the Secretary of State and Ohio Elections Commission, filed a complaint in this court to prohibit respondents, Judge Dana A. Deshler and the Court of Common Pleas of Franklin County, from proceeding in a declaratory judgment action to determine whether intervening respondent (a plaintiff in the declaratory judgment action) Ohioans for Fair Representation, Inc. ("OFRI") is a political action committee subject to the prohibitions of R.C. 3599.03 and other criminal statutes applicable to political action committees. We denied relators' motion for an alternative writ and granted OFRI's motion to intervene. However, before respondents' answer was due, respondent Judge Deshler issued his decision in the case on July 11, 1991. Relators then filed a motion to supplement their complaint to include the fact that the case was decided. Respondents and OFRI oppose this motion because, they state, such matters are irrelevant.

Respondents have also moved to strike additional evidence submitted by relators, which pertains to the proceedings before the Ohio Elections Commission ("commission"). (The parties have otherwise stipulated [***2] an agreed statement of facts.) Respondents maintain that the additional evidence is irrelevant to the key issue -- whether respondent judge had jurisdiction to hear the underlying declaratory judgment action -- and that the exhibits are not certified as required by Evid.R. 902.

Relators and respondents have both filed motions for judgment on the pleadings. Relators contend that respondents have no jurisdiction because special statutory proceedings commit issues involving these election statutes to their respective jurisdictions. Respondents contend that respondent judge has entered his decision in the underlying declaratory judgment action, and, therefore, prohibition being prospective, the writ should not issue.

The parties have also submitted briefs on the merits.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relators, secretary of state and state elections commission (secretary and commission), filed a complaint to prohibit respondents, judge and common pleas court, from proceeding in a declaratory judgment action to determine whether intervening respondent organization was a political action committee subject to certain statutes. The secretary and commission then sought to file an supplemental complaint, which the organization opposed.


OVERVIEW: The organization filed a declaratory judgment action seeking a determination that it was a political action committee. The secretary and commission sought a writ of prohibition preventing the judge and common pleas court from making that determination, asserting that the commission had exclusive jurisdiction on the issue. After the trial court granted the organization leave to intervene, but before time for the organization to file pleadings, the underlying proceeding was decided. The secretary and commission then sought to supplement their pleadings to include the fact that the underlying action was decided. The court granted the motion to supplement the complaint as just and reasonable, as required by Ohio R. Civ. P. 15(E). The secretary and commission's motion for judgment on the pleadings was denied because they had no special statutory jurisdiction which precluded the judge from hearing the issues raised in the declaratory judgment action. The judge and common pleas court's motion for judgment was denied because the matter had already been decided. The court found that there was no proof that the judge's assumption of power was clearly unauthorized by law.


OUTCOME: The writ of prohibition was denied.

 

207 of 2888 DOCUMENTS


State v. Evans


No. 90-1452


Supreme Court of Ohio


63 Ohio St. 3d 231; 586 N.E.2d 1042; 1992 Ohio LEXIS 271


November 20, 1991, Submitted  
March 11, 1992, Decided 
March 11, 1992, Released *


* Reporter's Note: This cause was decided on December 31, 1991, but was released to the public on March 11, 1992, subsequent to the retirement of Judge Strausbaugh, who participated in the decision.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Rehearing Denied April 22, 1992, Reported at 1992 Ohio LEXIS 683. As Amended. * Reporter's Note: This cause was decided on December 31, 1991, but was released to the public on March 11, 1992, subsequent to the retirement of Judge Strausbaugh, who participated in the decision.
Rehearing denied by State v. Evans, 63 Ohio St. 3d 1450, 589 N.E.2d 393, 590 N.E.2d 750, 1992 Ohio LEXIS 683 (1992)
Writ of certiorari denied Evans v. Ohio, 506 U.S. 886, 113 S. Ct. 246, 121 L. Ed. 2d 179, 1992 U.S. LEXIS 6041 (1992)
Subsequent appeal at Evans v. Mitchell, 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 19416 (6th Cir.) (6th Cir. Ohio, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Court of Appeals for Cuyahoga County, No. 54883.

On the evening of March 25, 1987, appellant, Derrick Evans, and his friends, brothers Wayne Frazier and Michael Frazier, appeared at the apartment of Joann Richards and Marcellus Williams, located at 49th and Central in Cleveland, Ohio. Williams's nineteen-year-old son, Derek Speights, who was visiting his father at the time and who earlier that evening had had a conversation with appellant and the Fraziers, opened the apartment door and let the three men into the apartment. Also present in the apartment that evening were  [***2] Richards's seven-year-old son, Albert Richards, and Richards's infant son.

Shortly after the arrival of appellant and the Fraziers at the Richards's apartment, Williams, Joann Richards and Speights were repeatedly stabbed. Speights survived the attack, but Williams and Joann Richards died from the wounds they received.

On April 6, 1987, a Cuyahoga County Grand Jury issued a six-count indictment naming appellant, Wayne Frazier and Michael Frazier as the perpetrators of the stabbings. The first two counts in the indictment charged appellant and the Fraziers with the aggravated murders of Joann Richards and Marcellus Williams, respectively, each in violation of R.C. 2903.01. With respect to appellant, the counts each carried three death-penalty specifications: (1) the offense was part of a course of conduct in which the offender purposely killed Joann Richards and Marcellus Williams, and attempted to kill Derek Speights -- the "mass murder" specification, R.C. 2929.04(A)(5); (2) the offense was committed while the offender was committing or attempting to commit or fleeing immediately after committing or attempting to commit aggravated robbery and aggravated burglary, and either the offender  [***3] was the principal offender in the commission of the aggravated murder or, if not the principal offender, committed the aggravated murder with prior calculation and design -- the "felony-murder" specification, R.C. 2929.04(A)(7); and (3) prior to the offense, the offender was convicted of the crime of murder, of which an essential element was the purposeful killing or purposeful attempt to kill another -- the "prior-murder" specification, R.C. 2929.04(A)(5). Also with respect to appellant, each aggravated murder count contained an aggravated-felony specification and a gun specification.

Count three of the indictment charged appellant and the Fraziers with aggravated burglary of the Richards residence in violation of R.C. 2911.11, and count four charged appellant and the Fraziers with the attempted murder of Derek Speights in violation of R.C. 2903.02 and 2923.02. Both counts three and four contained the same aggravated felony and gun specifications as in counts one and two.

Count five of the indictment charged appellant and the Fraziers with aggravated robbery of Marcellus Williams, and count six charged appellant and the Fraziers with aggravated robbery of Joann Richards, both in violation  [***4] of R.C. 2911.01. Each count also contained the same aggravated felony and gun specifications as in counts one, two, three and four.

Appellant entered a plea of not guilty to each of the counts in the indictment. He proceeded to trial on all the charges with the exception of the prior-murder and aggravated felony specifications, which he elected to have determined by the trial judge.

On September 28, 1987, jury selection commenced, and on the following day a jury of twelve jurors and four alternate jurors was empaneled and sworn. On September 30, 1987, counsel gave their opening statements, and the prosecution began the presentation of its case.

The prosecution's chief witness, Derek M. Speights, testified that on March 25, 1987 at about 10:00 p.m., appellant and the Fraziers pulled up in an orange Chevette shortly after Speights left a store across the street from his father's home where he had gone to purchase a beer. He identified Wayne Frazier as the driver of the car, Michael Frazier as a passenger in the back seat and appellant as the front seat passenger. Speights stated that they asked him if his father was home, to which he responded affirmatively. Speights then was told  [***5] to tell his father they would be back to see him.

Speights related that approximately one-half hour after his encounter with appellant and the Fraziers, the three came to the apartment and knocked on the door. Speights let them into the apartment and they all sat down, with Wayne Frazier sitting on the couch next to Joann Richards and Michael Frazier and the appellant sitting together on a love seat.

According to the testimony, after about five minutes of general conversation, the appellant suddenly jumped up with a gun, ran over to Williams and put the gun to Williams's head. Appellant asked for some money and told "nobody to move." Speights stated that at that same moment, Wayne Frazier ran over to Joann Richards with a knife, and Michael Frazier knocked him (Derek) to the floor. While Speights was down, he could see what appeared to him to be Wayne Frazier stabbing Richards. Speights also observed the appellant knock Williams down, tie him up with a telephone cord, and stab him on both sides.

At some point during the incident, Michael Frazier tied Speights's hands behind his back with a towel. Michael Frazier then stabbed Speights twenty-one times before he, Speights, was hit  [***6] in the head with a gun. After Speights was stabbed, he saw Wayne Frazier run into one of the bedrooms and grab a television set. Speights also observed appellant take money from Williams's pockets.

Speights further testified as to various orders appellant gave to Wayne and Michael Frazier during the entire incident, including an order to kill Speights if he moved, and an order to Wayne Frazier to grab Richards. In his opinion, it was "like they planned it because the way it happened, as soon as he [appellant] moved, they [the Fraziers] all moved after him."

After the trio left the apartment, Speights tried to get up, but could not walk. He testified that he then crawled over to his father and kicked him to see if he was alive, but his father did not respond. Speights then crawled to the stairway and rolled down the steps, where a tenant who lived downstairs found him. An ambulance was summoned, and after two days of hospitalization for his injuries, Speights gave police the names of the perpetrators.

After the court held a hearing to determine whether seven-year-old Albert Richards was competent to testify, the court found him qualified, and permitted the jury to hear his testimony.  [***7] Albert testified that, after being put to bed at about 9:00 p.m., he was awakened by banging noises. He got out of bed and saw Wayne Frazier, whom he referred to as "Twin," stabbing his mother on the couch. He also testified that he saw the appellant, known to him as "Da Da," run out of the apartment with a television.

Albert further testified that he observed Wayne Frazier kick his father, Williams, "in the eye," and that he did not look for Speights because he (Albert) hid under the bed until the police came. After telling the police officers who arrived on the scene not to shoot him, that he was "a kid," he came out from under the bed and was picked up by one of the officers, and was taken downstairs. Subsequently, Albert gave the police a statement describing the incident that evening.

On March 26, 1987, after speaking with Albert, the police apprehended Wayne Frazier at about 4:00 a.m. After interviewing Wayne, the police learned of two locations where various items relating to the crime were located. At one location, the police found items belonging to the victims including personal papers and Williams's prescription bottle, a buck knife (state's exhibit 16), and a television.  [***8] At a second location, the police found pieces of burnt clothing and a bottle of cologne that had been used to ignite the clothing. A second knife, found by uniformed officers at another location, was identified to police by both Wayne Frazier and Albert Richards. The knife, state's exhibit 17, was confirmed through the testimony of Michael Frazier as belonging to him.

In addition to identifying the knife, Michael Frazier testified as to his version of the events of March 25, 1987. He related that he, his brother Wayne, and appellant visited the home of Marcellus Williams at around 11:00 or 12:00 p.m. According to Michael, appellant jumped up with a gun, stated, "Everybody get on the floor," and told Wayne to tie up Williams with a telephone cord. Then, Michael testified that Williams was asked for money, to which Williams responded that he did not have any, but indicated that there was some cocaine in an ashtray in the bedroom.

Michael stated that he went to the bedroom, got the cocaine from the ashtray and, upon his return, he saw Joann Richards jump up and try to leap out the window. Wayne grabbed her and, according to Michael, told her nobody was going to get hurt, but that  [***9] appellant then yelled, "Shut up. Shut up, bitch," ran to her and stabbed her, and while stabbing Joann, stabbed Wayne in the hand.

Michael also testified that appellant was the only one who stabbed anybody, and that appellant had used his (Michael's) knife, state's exhibit 17, to stab each of the victims.

In addition to the testimony of Speights, Albert Richards, Michael Frazier, and members of the police department involved in the investigation of the crime and the apprehension of Wayne Frazier, the prosecution presented the testimony of Dr. John A. Daniels, a pathologist with the Cuyahoga County Coroner's Office, and Linda Luke, a forensic serologist with the Trace Evidence Department of the same office. Daniels testified that he had performed autopsies upon the bodies of Joann Richards and Williams. His investigation revealed that Joann Richards had received thirty-four stab wounds, of which at least one to the base of the neck was potentially fatal. The autopsy of Williams revealed that he had received nine stab wounds to his body, of which one transected the thoracic aorta, the main trunk of the arterial system, and was the only wound that was lethal. According to Daniels,  [***10] a person who receives a significant injury to that aorta will hemorrhage to death relatively quickly, as in Williams's case.

Daniels concluded that both victims died as a result of multiple stab wounds, and that the deeper stab wounds were more consistent with the use of state's exhibit 17 than with the smaller knife, state's exhibit 16. He also concluded that both deaths were homicides.

Luke, the forensic serologist, testified that an examination of state's exhibit 17 revealed the presence of blood that was consistent with Speights's blood grouping and enzyme type. Her examination of the television recovered the day after the stabbing and tennis shoes obtained during the booking of Wayne Frazier also revealed the presence of blood that was consistent with that of Williams.

The prosecution also presented the testimony of Officer Michael Exline with respect to the arrest of appellant in Columbus, Ohio, on May 29, 1987. Patrolman Exline, of the Columbus Police Department, testified that he sought to question appellant in connection with an investigation of a purse snatching. When police confronted appellant in the basement of a boarding house, underneath the stairway, appellant threatened  [***11] to inject himself with battery acid if they tried to arrest him. Exline was under the impression that appellant's name was Kelly, the alias appellant was using in Columbus, but appellant informed him that his name was Derrick Evans, and that there were warrants for him from Cleveland. According to Exline, appellant also volunteered that "he didn't kill those people but he knew who did, but he didn't want to rat on them."

After appellant gave his Social Security number to the officers, Exline ascertained that there was indeed a set of active warrants out for appellant. Exline's supervisor directed the officers to pull appellant from under the stairway, but appellant stated he had AIDS, and threatened to bite them. In order to remove appellant from the basement, the officers sprayed mace on him and, after taking him upstairs, they arrested him.

On October 2, 1987, the prosecution closed its case, and appellant elected not to present any evidence. After the presentation of closing arguments, the court charged the jury, and it began its deliberations in sequestration.

On the following day, the jury foreman announced in court that the jury had reached a verdict. The court read the jury's  [***12] verdict finding appellant guilty of all the above counts and specifications, except the felony-murder specifications. As to those specifications, the jury found that appellant was not the principal offender, and that he did not commit the murders with prior calculation and design.

The court then heard argument and received evidence on the aggravated felony and prior-murder specifications and found the appellant guilty of those specifications. Following a mitigation hearing, the jury recommended that the court sentence the defendant to death for the two murders. The trial court, after independently weighing any mitigating factors against the aggravating circumstances, found that the aggravating circumstances outweighed any mitigating factors beyond a reasonable doubt and imposed a sentence of death upon the appellant for each aggravated-murder count.

The appellant appealed, and the court of appeals affirmed the convictions except the aggravated burglary conviction and the gun specification convictions. After independently weighing the aggravating circumstances against any mitigating factors and performing a proportionality review of the death sentences, the court affirmed the sentences  [***13] of death.

The cause is now before this court upon an appeal as of right.
State v. Evans, 1990 Ohio App. LEXIS 1772 (Ohio Ct. App., Cuyahoga County, May 10, 1990)



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The jury found defendant guilty of two counts of aggravated murder, aggravated robbery, and the mass-murder death penalty specification. He was found not guilty of the felony-murder specification. The trial court found him guilty of the prior-murder specifications and sentenced him to death. The Court of Appeals for Cuyahoga County (Ohio) affirmed the convictions and sentence. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court determined that the prosecutor's closing argument during the mitigation phase was improper because the prosecutor told the jury that life imprisonment carried the possibility of defendant's return to freedom. However, the court decided that the death sentence did not need to be vacated because the trial court's admonition to the jury was sufficient to cure any prejudice. The court found no merit in defendant's remaining challenges to the prosecutor's conduct. Defendant contended that he was not death-eligible because the jury did not find him guilt of the felony-murder specification because the jury found that he was not the principal offender and that he did not commit the murder with prior calculation and design. The court determined that imposition of the death penalty was permitted based solely on the guilty finding on the prior-murder specification. Defendant asserted that the prior-murder specification was invalid because he was a juvenile when he committed the prior murder. The court replied that defendant was tried as an adult and was competent when he pleaded guilty. The court rejected the rest of the litany of defendant's propositions.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions and death sentence were affirmed.

In re Miller


No. 91-154


Supreme Court of Ohio


63 Ohio St. 3d 99; 585 N.E.2d 396; 1992 Ohio LEXIS 226


December 4, 1991, Submitted  
February 26, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Court of Appeals for Richland County, No. CA-2739.

On November 18, 1989, appellant, Kenneth Miller, was transported by three police officers from his home in Mansfield, Ohio, to Mansfield General Hospital, where he was involuntarily committed to the psychiatric ward. The officers had been summoned by appellant's family after appellant refused repeated requests to commit himself voluntarily. No hearing was held prior to the hospitalization, and no temporary order of detention was issued.

Civil commitment proceedings were initiated on November 22, 1989 by social worker Jody Allton, who executed an affidavit of mental illness, pursuant to R.C. 5122.11, which was filed with the Richland County Probate Court. Filed along with the affidavit was a certificate of examination, executed by appellant's long-time personal psychiatrist, Dr. Felipe N. Fernandez.

The probate court scheduled appellant's hearing for November 24, 1989, and continued the hearing date to November 28, 1989. At the November 28 hearing the referee denied appellant's motion to dismiss, which was based on alleged due-process violations in the course of appellant's involuntary confinement.  [***2] The hearing was continued until December 14, 1989 to allow appellant to obtain an independent psychiatric evaluation.

At the December 14 hearing the referee heard testimony from, inter alios, members of appellant's family, two of the officers who transported appellant to the hospital, appellant's own expert, and appellant's treating psychiatrist, Dr. Fernandez. The referee allowed Dr. Fernandez to testify despite appellant's objection that the physician-patient privilege, R.C. 2317.02(B), had not been waived. Based upon the testimony and evidence presented, the referee found that appellant was a mentally ill person subject to hospitalization pursuant to R.C. 5122.01(B)(4), and recommended that appellant be committed for a period not to exceed ninety days.

The probate court, adopting the referee's recommendation, filed a Judgment Entry of Commitment on December 14, 1989. Appellant was then returned to the hospital and on December 27, 1989 agreed to accept voluntary commitment. Appellant was released from the hospital on January 9, 1990.

The court of appeals affirmed the judgment of the probate court, ruling on the case even though the state of Ohio argued that the matter [***3] was moot. The state contended that appellant's acceptance of voluntary commitment, and the probate court's subsequent dismissal of the civil commitment case, made further proceedings unnecessary. However, the court of appeals reasoned that the case was not moot because the probate court's finding that appellant was mentally ill was an adjudication of status, which carried with it a stigma that could have profound effects on appellant's life, the stigma continuing to follow appellant even though his case had been dismissed.

The cause is now before this court pursuant to the allowance of a motion to certify the record.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court of Appeals for Richland County (Ohio) affirmed a judgment entry of commitment of the probate court on the basis that appellant, an individual alleged to be mentally ill (patient), was mentally ill. The patient appealed pursuant to the allowance of a motion to certify the record.


OVERVIEW: After his involuntary commitment, the patient voluntarily committed himself and was released thereafter. The patient contended that his due process rights were violated in the course of his involuntary commitment. The court agreed and reversed the judgment. The court found that the patient was not afforded due process because: (1) the police officer who transported the patient to a hospital gave no written statement of the patient's condition to ensure at least a minimal level of probable cause; (2) the patient was not informed of his various rights at the commencement of his commitment, such as the right to contact an attorney, as required by Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 5122.05(C); (3) the affidavit filed to commence the judicial proceedings for the commitment was not prepared by the proper person, as designated by Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 5122.05(K); and (4) the affidavit offered to the court by the patient's treating psychiatrist was conclusory and thus deficient under Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 5122.11 to invoke the jurisdiction of the probate court. The court also found that the patient did not waive the right to prevent his treating psychiatrist from testifying at the commitment hearing.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals.

Shirokey v. Marth


No. 90-1760


Supreme Court of Ohio


63 Ohio St. 3d 113; 585 N.E.2d 407; 1992 Ohio LEXIS 229


October 16, 1991, Submitted  
February 26, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied April 15, 1992, Reported at 1992 Ohio LEXIS 642. As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Court of Appeals for Cuyahoga County, Nos. 57267 and 57412.

Joseph Shirokey, plaintiff-appellee, is a classified civil service firefighter with the city of Cleveland Heights, Ohio. On June 23, 1984, Shirokey took a written examination for the position of Fire Lieutenant and finished sixth out of eighteen exam applicants. After additional points were awarded for seniority, efficiency and residency, Shirokey moved up to fourth on the eligibility list, which was certified by the city's civil service commission on July 13, 1984. The parties have indicated that, after an initial appointment from the list, Shirokey moved to third on the list.

In September 1984, the city's Fire Chief, Robert Marth, notified the City Manager, Richard V. Robinson, the appointing authority, that a vacancy existed in the Division of Fire for the position of Lieutenant. On September 29, 1984, the civil service commission certified Shirokey along with two other firefighters, then standing highest on the eligibility list, to Robinson, in accordance with Section 10(i), Rule IV of the Cleveland Heights [***2] Civil Service Commission (the "one of three" rule). However, a firefighter other than Shirokey was appointed 1 to fill the existing vacancy after being recommended by Marth. Again, on October 1, 1984, there existed a vacancy in the rank of Lieutenant, and on October 2, 1984, a request for a certification of names was made by Robinson in accordance with the civil service commission rule. Consequently, Shirokey, along with two other firefighters then standing highest on the eligibility list, was certified to Robinson, and on October 3, 1984 a firefighter other than Shirokey was appointed to fill the vacancy. The civil service commission records show no further written requests by Robinson to certify Shirokey for a third time. The "one of three" rule prohibited certification of a name more than twice except on the written request of the appointing authority. Therefore, Shirokey was never again certified for promotion. Subsequently, pursuant to Section 3, Rule V of the Cleveland Heights Civil Service Commission, the eligibility list established on July 13, 1984 expired on July 13, 1985, after several additional appointments were made from the list.


1 Section 10(i), Rule IV of the Cleveland Heights Civil Service Commission requires the following procedure to fill vacancies:

"When an examination for promotion in the Divisions of Police and Fire has been completed and upon receipt of notice from the Appointing Authority that a vacancy exists in a particular rank, the Commission shall certify to such Appointing Authority, the three names graded highest on the appropriate eligible list. Such Appointing Authority shall thereupon appoint one of the three so certified to fill the office. In case there be more than one vacancy to be filled, additional names shall be certified, so that at least twice more eligibles than positions to be filled shall be certified. If there are less than three names on an eligible list because less than three candidates were eligible for, or did not compete for [sic], or did not pass [sic] an examination for promotion, then the Commission shall certify the names or name comprising the respective list for appointment, and the Appointing Authority shall appoint one of the two candidates, or the candidate on the list, to fill any such vacancy existing. No name shall be certified more than twice to the same Appointing Authority for promotion from an eligibility list, except on the written request of such Authority."

 [***3] On August 1, 1985, Shirokey filed a complaint against various officials of the city in their individual and official capacities, including Chief Marth, Robert Downey (City Manager), Marcia Nolan (Assistant City Manager) and Donald Barclay (City Law Director). On August 2, 1985, Shirokey amended his complaint to include the city as a defendant. Shirokey pled, inter alia, that he had been improperly passed over on the civil service promotion eligibility list for the position of Fire Lieutenant and that Marth had committed three separate acts of defamation. The allegations also included claims for breach of contract, intentional and negligent infliction of emotional distress, and violations of Shirokey's constitutional due process rights pursuant to Section 1983, Title 42, U.S.Code.

On January 3, 1986, the defendants moved for summary judgment. Subsequently, Shirokey filed a second amended complaint adding as defendants three members of the city's civil service commission and the commission's legal counsel. The trial court denied the city's motion on May 28, 1986. Shirokey then filed a motion for partial summary judgment on June 28, 1988 covering only his Section 1983 claims.  [***4] He claimed that the "one of three" rule had not been properly adopted, and that state law therefore applied. He claimed that under R.C. 124.46 and 124.48 he was entitled to promotion. In the motion Shirokey requested a permanent injunction to promote him with back pay and seniority in the higher rank, as well as payment of his attorney fees. Thereafter, the defendants opposed Shirokey's motion for summary judgment and filed a cross-motion for summary judgment on all claims against them. 2


2 During the course of this action Shirokey voluntarily dismissed all defendants other than the city and all counts except for the Section 1983 claims.

On October 28, 1988, the trial court denied the defendants' cross-motion for summary judgment, but granted Shirokey's partial motion for summary judgment. The court concluded that Shirokey was entitled to be appointed to the rank of Lieutenant (with seniority) as of October 29, 1984. Therefore, he was awarded compensatory damages in the amount of $ 15,309.18 plus $ 6,687.62 (later reduced to $ 3,513.09) in interest. Moreover, the court awarded Shirokey his reasonable litigation expenses in the amount of $ 2,233.88 and attorney fees of [***5] $ 41,500. The court further held that punitive damages were inappropriate.

The court of appeals affirmed the trial court and held that Shirokey had a legitimate claim of entitlement to be promoted to the rank of Fire Lieutenant because of his position on the promotional list. Consequently, the court held, he had been denied his property interest under color of state law.

This cause is now before the court pursuant to the allowance of a motion to certify the record.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city appealed the decision of the Court of Appeals for Cuyahoga County (Ohio), which affirmed the grant of plaintiff firefighter's motion for partial summary judgment by the trial court, pursuant to 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, for denial of due process under color of state law.


OVERVIEW: The firefighter was not eligible for promotion because he was passed over twice, the limit permitted by city civil service rules. The firefighter filed a complaint against the city officials and the city for improperly denying him a promotion, breach of contract, defamation, infliction of emotional distress, and for violation of his due process rights pursuant to 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. The firefighter filed a motion for partial summary judgment on the § 1983 claim and the city filed a motion for summary judgment on all the claims. The trial court denied the city's motion and granted the firefighter's motion. The appellate court affirmed the decision of the trial court, finding that the firefighter had a legitimate claim of entitlement for promotion denied him under the color of state law. The court reversed the lower courts, determining that the firefighter had not been denied protected fundamental rights and was entitled only to procedural due process protection under § 1983. The court found that the state had afforded the firefighter procedural due process because the firefighter had available statutory and common-law remedies before the courts or the civil service commission.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the lower courts, which had granted the firefighter's, motion for partial summary judgment against the city for violation of plaintiff's due process rights.

 

210 of 2888 DOCUMENTS


State v. Gill


No. 91-203


Supreme Court of Ohio


63 Ohio St. 3d 53; 584 N.E.2d 1200; 1992 Ohio LEXIS 224


December 10, 1991, Submitted  
February 19, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certified by the Court of Appeals for Summit County, No. 14668.

On May 9, 1990, appellee, Shinder K. Gill, was indicted for trafficking in food stamps, a violation of R.C. 2913.46(A). On May 30, 1990, appellee filed a motion to dismiss the indictment challenging the constitutionality of R.C. 2913.46.

The trial court overruled appellee's motion to dismiss. Subsequently, appellee entered a plea of no contest to the charge against her. Appellee was convicted and, thereafter, appealed her conviction.

The Court of Appeals for Summit County reversed the judgment of the trial court and remanded the cause with instructions to enter a dismissal of the indictment. The court of appeals determined that R.C. 2913.46(A) allowed Congress to, in effect, redefine, alter or even repeal an Ohio crime without any action by the General Assembly, and without reference to any fixed standards or policy set by the General Assembly. Therefore, held the court of appeals, the statutory scheme constitutes an unconstitutional delegation of legislative authority in violation of Section 1, Article II of the Ohio Constitution.

Finding its judgment to be in conflict with the judgment of the Court of [***2] Appeals for Cuyahoga County in State v. Bolar (1987), 39 Ohio App.3d 194, 530 N.E.2d 940, the court of appeals certified the record of the case to this court for review and final determination.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeals for Summit County (Ohio) reversing the judgment from the trial court and remanding the cause with instructions to enter a dismissal of the indictment. The court of appeals determined that Ohio Rev. Code § 2913.46(A) allowed Congress to redefine, alter or even repeal an Ohio crime without any action by the General Assembly.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted for trafficking in food stamps, a violation of § 2913.46(A). Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the indictment challenging the constitutionality of § 2913.46. The trial court overruled the motion and defendant was convicted. The court of appeals found that § 2913.46(A) constituted an unconstitutional delegation of legislative authority in violation of the Ohio Const. art. II, § 1. On further appeal, the court found that Ohio Rev. Code § 2913.46(A) did not violate the Ohio Constitution. Defendant argued that by including the language "as amended," the General Assembly intended to incorporate all amended versions of the federal law subsequent to the time that § 2913.46(A) was enacted. Therefore, defendant argued that the General Assembly delegated its legislative authority to Congress in violation of the Ohio Constitution. The court held that the General Assembly, by using the language "as amended," did not intend to adopt amendments to the federal law subsequent to the effective date of § 2913.46(A), but rather, the General Assembly simply intended to incorporate the federal food stamp law as it existed on the date § 2913.46(A) was enacted.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment from the court of appeals and reinstated the trial court's judgment that overruled defendant's motion to dismiss and convicted her of trafficking in food stamps.

 

211 of 2888 DOCUMENTS


State v. Davis


No. 90-2524


Supreme Court of Ohio


63 Ohio St. 3d 44; 584 N.E.2d 1192; 1992 Ohio LEXIS 221


December 11, 1991, Submitted  
February 19, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied March 25, 1992, Reported at 1992 Ohio LEXIS 493. Second Motion for Rehearing Denied May 26, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Ohio LEXIS 992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Court of Appeals for Butler County, No. CA89-09-123.

In 1984, appellant, Von Clark Davis, was tried before a three-judge panel for the aggravated murder of Suzette Butler. Appellant was found guilty on one count of aggravated murder for purposely, and with prior calculation and design, causing Butler's death. Appellant was also found guilty of a death-penalty specification in connection with the aggravated murder. Following a full mitigation hearing, the three-judge panel sentenced appellant to death. The court of appeals affirmed appellant's conviction and the sentence of death.

In State v. Davis (1988), 38 Ohio St.3d 361, 528 N.E.2d 925, we affirmed appellant's conviction, but a majority of this court reversed appellant's death sentence based upon errors which occurred after all available mitigating evidence had been heard in the penalty phase of appellant's trial. The Davis majority remanded the cause to the "trial court" (the three-judge panel) for a resentencing hearing "* * * at which [***2] the state may seek whatever punishment is lawful, including, but not limited to, the death sentence." Id. at 373, 528 N.E.2d at 936. 1


1 A complete statement of the facts of this case concerning appellant's conviction and original death sentence can be found in our reported opinion in Davis, supra.

On remand, in August 1989, the same three-judge panel which had originally convicted and sentenced appellant conducted the resentencing hearing. At this hearing appellant was not permitted to introduce evidence concerning appellant's adjustment to prison life since his 1984 conviction and sentence. Appellant was also not permitted to present the testimony of Dr. Roger Fisher, a psychologist, who, according to appellant, would have provided a "psychological update" of appellant covering the period since appellant's 1984 conviction and sentence.

Following the resentencing hearing, the panel, limiting its consideration to the evidence presented in mitigation at appellant's 1984 trial, resentenced appellant to death. On appeal, the court of appeals affirmed the sentence of death.

The cause is now before us upon an appeal as of right.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his death sentence following his affirming of his murder conviction by the Court of Appeals for Butler County (Ohio).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder and sentenced to death and the intermediate court affirmed. He appealed and the court affirmed. The court held that defendant was not entitled to introduce post-trial mitigation evidence at his sentencing hearing. When the sentence was reversed and remanded, evidence of defendant's activities after his trial was not admissible in the second sentencing hearing. It was not error for the three-judge sentencing panel to separate over the weekend while deliberating defendant's sentence. Unlike a jury, the court was presumed to know the law and to be incapable of influence by outside sources. The lower court correctly assessed the appropriateness of defendant's sentence by considering the factors set forth in Ohio Rev. Code Ann § 2929.05(A). The court also was required to independently review the sentence for appropriateness and found that it was neither excessive nor disproportionate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's death sentence.
