Voinovich v. Ferguson


No. 91-1882


Supreme Court of Ohio


63 Ohio St. 3d 198; 586 N.E.2d 1020; 1992 Ohio LEXIS 1698


February 10, 1992, Submitted  
February 14, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: In Declaratory Judgment.

On September 19, 1991, plaintiffs, Governor George V. Voinovich, Senate President Stanley J. Aronoff, and Secretary of State Robert A. Taft II, brought this action for declaratory judgment and injunction against defendants Auditor of State Thomas E. Ferguson, State Representative Barney Quilter, Attorney General Lee Fisher, State Senator Charles E. Henry, and State Senator Grace L. Drake, and against a class of John Doe defendants. Plaintiffs constitute a majority of the five public officials responsible for apportionment under Article XI of the Ohio Constitution. Defendants Ferguson and Quilter are the other two public officials responsible for apportionment under Article XI. Defendant Henry is the Senator of Senate District 32 of Ohio. Defendant Drake is the Senator of Senate District 22 of Ohio, which adjoins Senate District 32.

Defendants Ferguson and Quilter removed the case to federal district court, but the federal district court held that it lacked jurisdiction over the action under Article III of the United States Constitution and remanded the case to this court.

On November 6, 1991, plaintiffs moved to amend their complaint.  [***2] The amended complaint omitted the cause of action for an injunction. On November 20, we granted the motion to amend; granted plaintiffs' motion to certify the record of the case from the federal district court; and ordered the parties to file evidence and responses to the amended complaint on or before November 25, 1991 and to file briefs on or before November 27. 62 Ohio St.3d 1209, 581 N.E.2d 560. Also on November 20, defendants Ferguson and Quilter filed a notice of withdrawal of their answer and counterclaim and first and second amended answers and counterclaims filed in federal district court.

On November 25, 1991, defendants Henry and Drake filed answers and counterclaims, defendant Fisher filed a motion to dismiss and motion for oral argument, and defendants Ferguson and Quilter filed a motion to dismiss. Plaintiffs and defendants Ferguson and Quilter also filed evidence. Also on November 25, two judges of the Sixth Circuit Court of Appeals entered an order staying the remand of the case, effectively blocking further action in this court. However, on December 12, the Sixth Circuit Court of Appeals, en banc, vacated the order of November 25.

On December 16, 1991,  [***3] we ordered responses to defendants' motions to dismiss to be filed on or before December 18, ordered defendants to file any additional evidence on or before that date, and ordered the parties to file any miscellaneous motions on or before that date and to file briefs on or before December 23. 62 Ohio St.3d 1480, 581 N.E.2d 1387.

On December 20, 1991, defendant Drake filed her brief. On December 23, all other parties filed their briefs, except Henry, who filed his the next day; defendants Ferguson and Quilter requested oral argument and applied for a stay of proceedings, or alternatively, to extend the briefing schedule; and defendant Fisher renewed his request for oral argument. On December 27, plaintiffs moved to strike defendant Fisher's brief and request for oral argument, and moved to strike defendants Ferguson's and Quilter's evidence.

On January 13, 1992, this court overruled the motions to dismiss of Fisher and of Ferguson and Quilter; ordered defendants Fisher, Ferguson, and Quilter to file answers, and permitted them to file counterclaims, on or before January 27, 1992, and ordered the parties to brief, on or before January 27, whether counterclaims could survive dismissal [***4] of the plaintiffs' claim. 62 Ohio St.3d 1224, 584 N.E.2d 737.

On January 27, 1992, the parties filed briefs on the counterclaim issue, and defendants Fisher and Ferguson and Quilter filed answers without counterclaims. However, in their answers these defendants again raised the issues of jurisdiction, whether a justiciable controversy exists, and whether plaintiffs have standing to bring the action, first raised in their motions to dismiss. The case is thus submitted and the cause is before the court.



DISPOSITION: Judgment accordingly.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff public officials brought an action for declaratory judgment against defendant public officials in the Ohio Supreme Court regarding the constitutionality of Senate District 32 because the district did not meet the population requirements of Ohio Const. art. XI, § 4.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs contended that leaving Senate District 32 underpopulated was unavoidable because irreconcilable conflicts with Ohio Const. art. Xi §§ 9 and 11 prevented adding population from adjacent territory. The supreme court held that Ohio Const. art. XI, §§ 4, 9, and 11 were co-equal and that when such provisions were irreconcilable, those public officials designated to apportion the state by Ohio Const. art. XI, § 1, had the duty to choose the proper course. The court held that it would not order them to correct one constitutional violation by committing another.


OUTCOME: The court ruled that the plan was constitutional.

 

213 of 2888 DOCUMENTS


State v. Sneed


No. 89-1291


Supreme Court of Ohio


63 Ohio St. 3d 3; 584 N.E.2d 1160; 1992 Ohio LEXIS 215


October 8, 1991, Submitted  
February 12, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied March 25, 1992, Reported at 1992 Ohio LEXIS 490. As Amended.
Writ of certiorari denied Sneed v. Ohio, 507 U.S. 983, 113 S. Ct. 1577, 123 L. Ed. 2d 145, 1993 U.S. LEXIS 2152 (1993)
Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Sneed, 2000 Ohio App. LEXIS 4611 (Ohio Ct. App., Stark County, Sept. 29, 2000)
Reopening denied by State v. Sneed, 96 Ohio St. 3d 348, 2002 Ohio 4768, 774 N.E.2d 1216, 2002 Ohio LEXIS 2239 (2002)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Court of Appeals for Stark County, No. CA-6976.

The appellant, David Allen Sneed, and Chevette Denise Brown were indicted on February 1, 1985 for aggravated murder and aggravated robbery. The aggravated-murder charge included specifications that Brown and the appellant were committing or attempting to commit or fleeing immediately after committing or attempting to commit aggravated robbery, and that both had a firearm on or about their persons or under their control while committing the offenses charged within the indictment. The aggravated robbery charge also included a firearm specification.

Appellant was then tried on the indictment commencing on May 19, 1986. A jury found appellant guilty of all charges and specifications on June 11, 1986, and at the conclusion of the penalty phase, on July 27, 1986, the jury recommended that appellant receive the death penalty. The trial court adopted the jury's recommendation and imposed the death sentence.

On November 17, 1984, the victim, Herbert M. Rowan, a resident of Chicago, Illinois, arrived at a friend's house in Canton,  [***2] Ohio, to visit for the weekend. The following day, sometime between 7:00 and 8:00 p.m., Rowan left his friend's home to go drinking. Rowan went to a bar and was observed by one witness leaving around 1:30 to 1:45 a.m. on November 19.

According to the testimony of Chevette Denise Brown, a nineteen-year-old prostitute, she was standing outside Andre's Restaurant near the 300 block of DeWalt Avenue, wearing a rabbit coat, when Rowan drove by in his rented Buick automobile around 2:30 to 3:00 a.m. on November 19. Brown stopped Rowan and asked him if he "want[ed] a date." Rowan declined the offer, circled the block and again drove down the alley at the 300 block of DeWalt Avenue. Brown again propositioned Rowan, who then circled the block a second time and again approached Brown. Appellant, who was apparently in the background near Brown, wearing a trench coat, instructed Brown to ask Rowan if he would give them a ride home. Brown complied and the two entered Rowan's car. Brown opened the front passenger door and sat down in the passenger seat, appellant got in the back, directly behind the driver.

Appellant directed Brown to another destination and, en route, asked Rowan to [***3] pull over, turn the car off and to "give him some money." Rowan refused, stating that he only had enough money to get to where he was going. Appellant then pulled out a gun and pointed it at Rowan's temple and told Rowan that "this * * * [is] a robbery." Upon Rowan's refusal a second time, appellant pulled the trigger, shooting the victim. Brown related that Rowan's head immediately struck the steering wheel causing the car horn to sound. In response to the prosecutor's question concerning the likelihood of this particular shooting to cause Rowan's death, the Chief Deputy Coroner and pathologist for the Stark County Coroner's Office stated: "Generally speaking, any bullet which goes in through the skull and perforates the brain or goes into the brain itself is with rare exceptions fatal, so this could have been the fatal bullet." Furthermore, Brown stated that appellant then exited the vehicle, pulled Rowan off the steering wheel and pushed him over. Brown got into the back seat and appellant drove the car to an alley by a house.

While hidden in the alley, appellant purloined Rowan's jewelry and wallet. Next, Brown testified, appellant handed the gun to her over the front seat [***4] of the car and Brown, following appellant's orders after being threatened with the gun, shot Rowan in the back of his head. Appellant then drove to another alley where Brown helped appellant place the body into the trunk of the car.

Brown stated that after the shooting they drove the car to appellant's apartment and changed clothes. Appellant removed Rowan's boots, socks and jacket while Brown cleaned the front seat with a brush, towel and bucket of water. Afterward, the two drove in the victim's car to the house of appellant's brother, Theotis Dillard. Dillard and appellant went outside where appellant opened the trunk and disclosed the body. The three then drove back to the appellant's apartment. Brown related that Dillard and the appellant cut electrical cords from lamps, obtained two cement blocks and a plastic garment bag. Dillard in his car and the appellant and Brown in the victim's car drove to the Allen Street bridge. Brown testified that she watched as appellant and Dillard tied the two cement blocks with the electrical cords to Rowan's feet. The two then tossed Rowan's weighted body off the bridge and into the creek below. The body, however, did not become hidden [***5] beneath the water in the creek; it landed on the bank and was found the next day.

On November 19, 1984, Rowan's body was discovered on the banks of Nimishillan Creek underneath the Allen Street bridge in Canton, Ohio. The corpse was partially enveloped in a plastic garment bag bearing the name "Joe Slaughter Men's Store." A cement block was tied to the ankles by an electrical cord and a piece of speaker wire later found to have been severed from Rowan's car stereo. A second electrical cord and also speaker wire were tied around the neck. Another cement block tied with an electrical cord or speaker cord lay within two feet of the body. The body was without shoes, socks or jewelry, clothed in a cowboy shirt and jeans. Two bullet holes were in the victim's head: one toward the rear of the skull, the other beside the right eye.

In addition to the testimony indicated supra, the jury also received testimony from Dillard which corroborated that of Brown. Additionally, Dillard testified that while he and the appellant were in the process of disposing of the body, appellant admitted that he had shot the victim. Dillard was not prosecuted for his involvement with the appellant and [***6] Brown on this crime. Brown was indicted for aggravated murder and pled to the indictment in a plea bargain wherein she would not have to face the death penalty. She received a sentence of twenty years to life.

The events which gave rise to appellant's arrest are as follows. On December 6, 1984, parole officer Patrick Munford met with Roxanne Goosby, a patient in Timkin Mercy Hospital, after Goosby informed Munford about a telephone threat that appellant had made against her. Munford then arranged for appellant to come to his office the following day "to discuss some matters of alleged activity." Present with Munford at the interview was Munford's supervisor, senior parole officer David Slater. Shortly thereafter, two policemen arrived with a warrant for appellant's arrest on an assault charge filed by Goosby. After appellant was taken into custody, the parole officers requested his permission to search the premises of his apartment at 1460 Water Court. Although appellant denied that he agreed to the search of his home, Munford, Slater and one of the two policemen asserted that appellant approved the search, proclaiming that "he had nothing to hide." Upon their arrival, Munford [***7] and Slater were met at the door by Chevette Brown and obtained her permission to search the residence. During the ensuing search, a .25 caliber handgun was uncovered in the bedroom of the apartment. Soon thereafter, the Canton police learned that the handgun taken from appellant's apartment could possibly be the weapon used to kill Rowan. The suspicion was confirmed by subsequent ballistic tests.

Detective Michael O'Brien of the Canton Police Department testified that during the course of the investigation he was able to locate the victim's car. Brown's fingerprints were found in the car. Based on the evidence obtained, a search warrant was issued for the Water Court residence of appellant and Brown. In the course of the search, the police confiscated an imitation leather coat, a brown nylon jacket, a blue towel, two lamps with severed cords attached, a Zippo lighter, scrub brush and a rabbit fur coat. At the conclusion of the search, Brown was arrested on an outstanding warrant for contempt of court. After being advised of her constitutional rights, Brown confessed to her complicity with the appellant in the death of Rowan.

This cause is now before the court upon an appeal [***8] as of right.
State v. Sneed, 1989 Ohio App. LEXIS 2180 (Ohio Ct. App., Stark County, May 22, 1989)



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Court of Appeals for Stark County (Ohio), which affirmed his conviction on charges of aggravated murder under Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2903.01(B) and the imposition of the death penalty. The defendant was convicted of shooting and killing a man in the course of an armed robbery.


OVERVIEW: The court upheld the defendant's conviction and sentence. The court ruled that the trial court was justified in overruling the defendant's motion to suppress the admission of the murder weapon, which was found during a search of the defendant's apartment, because the search was conducted with the written consent of the defendant's roommate. Moreover, it was not error to refuse to give a requested instruction to the jury on accomplice credibility, because such an instruction would have been an improper comment upon the evidence. It was not an abuse of discretion for the trial court to refuse to allow defendant's counsel to use grand jury testimony to cross-examine a state's witness. Further, the court held that it was of no meaningful consequence that the jury verdict form was stated in the disjunctive, when upon the signing of a separate verdict form, each juror found that the defendant personally performed every act constituting the offense of aggravated murder, and the evidence did not reasonably suggest that the defendant was other than the principal offender. The break in the jury's sequestration did not have the effect of discharging the jury.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's decision to affirm the defendant's conviction and sentence.

Chi. Freight Car Leasing Co. v. Limbach


No. 91-2


Supreme Court of Ohio


62 Ohio St. 3d 489; 584 N.E.2d 690; 1992 Ohio LEXIS 207


November 5, 1991, Submitted  
February 12, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal and Cross-Appeal from the Board of Tax Appeals, No. 89-J-879.



DISPOSITION: Decision affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant tax commissioner and cross appellant leasing company sought review of the decision of the Board of Tax Appeals, which held that appellee leasing company was entitled to an abatement of overpaid taxes, but was not entitled to interest. The leasing company had applied for abatement pursuant to Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 5703.05(B).


OVERVIEW: The commissioner argued that she had discretion in issuing a certificate of abatement because Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 5703.05(B) used "may" in authorizing the relief, and therefore she acted reasonably in denying the abatement, and the 4-R Act provided for only prospective relief. The court held that the leasing company was entitled to an abatement of the overpaid taxes. The court held that the commissioner, under Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 5703.05(B), was permitted to abate taxes for up to five years after the overpayment while, under the refund-with-interest statutes that the leasing company alluded to, a refund of taxes was permitted for a shorter time. Failure to award interest did not deny the leasing company equal protection. The state's failure to pay interest on refunds of illegally collected taxes was not a denial of due process.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the board.

 

215 of 2888 DOCUMENTS


Williams v. Morris


No. 90-1273


Supreme Court of Ohio


62 Ohio St. 3d 463; 584 N.E.2d 671; 1992 Ohio LEXIS 137


October 15, 1991, Submitted  
February 5, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Court of Appeals for Scioto County, No. 1835.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, warden and others, challenged an order from the Court of Appeals for Scioto County (Ohio), which held appellants in contempt and ordered the release of appellee parolee subject to the special conditions of his original parole.


OVERVIEW: Appellants were ordered to release appellee on parole. Subsequently, appellants were held in contempt of that order by failing to release appellee under the same terms and conditions of his original parole and were ordered to release appellee subject to the special conditions of his original parole. On appeal, appellants contended that they were not in contempt because the original order did not require them to release appellee under the same conditions of his original parole. Appellants further contended they were authorized to impose additional conditions on the parole. The court agreed in part, holding that: (1) appellants were not in contempt of the order that merely required them to release appellee, not release him subject to the conditions of his original parole; (2) the appellate court ordered appellants to release appellee, which they did; (3) there was no basis for the finding of contempt; (4) the appellate court properly construed Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2967.15 as a special provision that restrained the parole board's otherwise near plenary authority to adopt rules; and (5) the release was required to be under the same terms and conditions of appellee's prior parole.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the appellate court insofar as it held appellants in contempt, but affirmed the decision insofar as it ordered appellee's release on parole subject to the special conditions of his original parole.

 

216 of 2888 DOCUMENTS


Quotron Systems, Inc. v. Limbach


No. 90-2497


Supreme Court of Ohio


62 Ohio St. 3d 447; 584 N.E.2d 658; 1992 Ohio LEXIS 140


October 16, 1991, Submitted  
February 5, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Board of Tax Appeals, No. 88-A-377.



DISPOSITION: Decision affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer challenged a decision of the Board of Tax Appeals (Ohio), which imposed a tax on appellant who was an out-of-state company.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court. Appellant taxpayer challenged and argued that a tax imposed upon it was a burden on interstate commerce. The commissioner had assessed the taxpayer because it had not collected the use tax. The court found that the United States Supreme Court allowed California to require a nonprofit corporation to collect California's use tax on items sold in the society's mail-order operation. The Court searched for an adequate nexus between the society and the taxing state that would permit the state to impose the use-tax collection liability on the society. The Court rejected the argument that the society's activity sought to be taxed must have a nexus with the taxing state. The court found that in the present case, a definite link existed. The taxpayer owned equipment that it delivered to subscribers in Ohio, constructed two communication concentrators in Ohio, and employed personnel in Ohio to install and maintain equipment. Ohio could require the taxpayer to collect the tax from its customers, and the taxpayer should have collected it. Having failed to do so, it was itself liable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court, which imposed a tax on appellant taxpayer who was an out-of-state company because the state could require the taxpayer to collect the tax from its customers, and the taxpayer should have collected it.

State ex rel. Columbus Southern Power Co. v. Sheward


No. 92-83


Supreme Court of Ohio


63 Ohio St. 3d 78; 585 N.E.2d 380; 1992 Ohio LEXIS 67


January 22, 1992, Submitted  
January 27, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] In Prohibition.

On April 2, 1991, relator Columbus Southern Power Company ("CSP") filed an application with the Public Utilities Commission of Ohio ("the commission") to increase its rates pursuant to R.C. 4909.18. On December 9, 1991, CSP filed notice with the commission of its intent to place in effect, on January 3, 1992, the rates proposed in its application, pursuant to R.C. 4909.42. 1 CSP subsequently agreed to postpone the effective date of the increase to January 13, 1992.


1 R.C. 4909.42 provides in part:

"If the proceeding on an application filed with the public utilities commission under section 4909.18 of the Revised Code by any public utility requesting an increase on any rate, joint rate, toll, classification, charge, or rental or requesting a change in a regulation or practice affecting the same has not been concluded and an order entered pursuant to section 4909.19 of the Revised Code at the expiration of two hundred seventy-five days from the date of filing the application, the proposed increase shall go into effect upon the filing of an undertaking by the public utility. The undertaking shall be filed with the commission and shall be payable to the state for the use and benefit of the customers affected by the proposed increase or change.

"The undertaking must be signed by two of the officers of the utility, under oath, and must contain a promise to refund any amounts collected by the utility over the rate, joint rate, toll, classification, charge, or rental, as determined in the final order of the commission. All refunds shall include interest at the rate stated in section 1343.03 of the Revised Code. The refund shall be in the form of a temporary reduction in rates following the final order of the commission, and shall be accomplished in such manner as shall be prescribed by the commission in its final order. The commission shall exercise continuing and exclusive jurisdiction over such refunds."

 [***2] On January 9, 1992, plaintiffs below 2 ("intervening respondents") filed a class action in the Franklin County Common Pleas Court, seeking a declaratory judgment that R.C. 4909.42 is unconstitutional and a permanent injunction preventing CSP from charging the rates proposed in its application. On January 10, 1992, respondent Richard S. Sheward, Judge of the Franklin County Court of Common Pleas, issued a temporary restraining order prohibiting CSP from placing its proposed rates in effect.


2 Plaintiffs below are the city of Columbus; Ronald J. O'Brien, city attorney (in an official and representative capacity); William A. Spratley, Consumers' Counsel (in an official and representative capacity); and Marvin Rothhaar, a residential customer of CSP.

On January 13, 1992, CSP filed this action to prohibit respondent Sheward from exercising jurisdiction over this matter. On January 15, 1992, we granted intervening respondents' motion to intervene in this proceeding, granted CSP's motion for an alternative writ, and sua sponte ordered the temporary restraining order preserved pending further order of this court. On this same date, respondent Sheward and intervening respondents [***3] filed motions to dismiss. On January 21, 1992, the state of Ohio and Attorney General Lee I. Fisher filed a motion to intervene on behalf of relator CSP. Their motion is hereby granted.

The cause is before the court on respondents' motions to dismiss for failure to state a claim upon which relief can be granted.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent judge and intervenor state brought a motion to dismiss relator utility's application for a writ of prohibition to bar the judge from exercising jurisdiction in the action of plaintiffs, city and others, to challenge the constitutionality of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4909.42, under which the utility sought to increase its rates.


OVERVIEW: The utility applied for a rate increase pursuant to Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4909.18 and filed notice of its intent to place the rates in effect pursuant to Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4909.42. The city and others filed a class action for a declaratory judgment that § 4909.42 was unconstitutional and for a permanent injunction preventing the utility from charging the proposed rates. The utility filed an action for a writ of prohibition to prevent the judge from exercising jurisdiction in the case, and the judge and the state sought to dismiss the action. The court held that a writ of prohibition could be granted only if the judge was about to exercise judicial power without authority of law, and if the denial of the writ would result in injury for which no other remedy existed. The court further held that while the Public Utility Commission of Ohio had ratemaking authority, it lacked jurisdiction to determine the constitutional validity of a statute, and the judge's exercise of jurisdiction pertained to the issue of constitutionality and not to the question of the proper rates.


OUTCOME: The court denied the utility's application for a writ of prohibition that sought to prohibit the judge from exercising jurisdiction over the underlying action. Because the court's order preserved the temporary restraining order past the time limit for an extension by the trial court, the trial court was given two days from the date of the decision to extend its order. Until such time, the order was to remain in effect.

Newbury Township Bd. of Township Trustees v. Lomak Petroleum (Ohio)


No. 90-1709


Supreme Court of Ohio


62 Ohio St. 3d 387; 583 N.E.2d 302; 1992 Ohio LEXIS 6; 117 Oil & Gas Rep. 107


October 15, 1991, Submitted  
January 15, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: Certified by the Court of Appeals for Geauga County, No. 88-G-1489.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed in part, reversed in part and cause remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant board of township trustees (board) sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals for Geauga County (Ohio), which granted summary judgment in favor of appellee oil company, and ruled that Newbury Township, Ohio, Zoning Resolution § 801.0 A was invalid because it was preempted by state law.


OVERVIEW: The board promulgated a zoning resolution requiring the oil company to obtain a local permit to operate oil wells in addition to the state permit it had acquired. The oil company alleged that the resolution was not motivated by health and safety concerns and was therefore preempted by Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 1509.39. The board contended that it was a valid health and safety regulation because the wells would be a nuisance in an area zoned for residential purposes. The court of appeals held that the resolution was invalid. On review, the court affirmed in part and reversed in part the decision. The court held that § 1509.39 preempted the power of a township to prohibit drilling in areas which were traditionally appropriate for such activity, unless health and safety standards were being adopted. The court found that the resolution was not an attempt to further health and safety goals because the board attempted to declare the wells nuisances without regard to the fact that some of the areas were farmland and appropriate for the activity. The court reversed the judgment on whether the regulation of distances from wells to occupied buildings created health and safety concerns.


OUTCOME: The court reversed in part and affirmed in part the decision of the court of appeals concerning the township's ordinance.

State v. Collier


No. 90-2053


Supreme Court of Ohio


62 Ohio St. 3d 267; 581 N.E.2d 552; 1991 Ohio LEXIS 2893


September 25, 1991, Submitted  
December 18, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Court of Appeals for Butler County, No. CA89-08-117.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeals for Butler County (Ohio) which reversed defendant's conviction for possession of a controlled substance because the charging statute, Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2925.11, was unconstitutionally void for vagueness.


OVERVIEW: In beginning its analysis of whether Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2925.11 was void for vagueness, the court noted the statute began with a general prohibition, that no person was to knowingly obtain, possess, or use a controlled substance, and that this language was clear and unambiguous. The average person reading that portion would have little doubt as to its meaning. Indeed, the conduct prohibited was set forth with notable lucidity. Likewise, the exception to the general prohibition was readily comprehensible and understandable. The statute simply meant that a person who knowingly obtained, possessed, or used a controlled substance was in violation of the law unless he or she obtained the controlled substance pursuant to a prescription issued by a practitioner and the drug was in the original container in which it was dispensed. The court found that § 2925.11 contained sufficient language to provide the ordinary person with adequate notice and fair warning as to the standard of conduct required. The language of the statute provided guidelines which were constitutionally adequate for citizens and law enforcement personnel. The court held that § 2925.11 was not unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals which reversed defendant's conviction, and the conviction was reinstated.

Hodge v. Middletown Hosp. Ass'n


No. 91-322


Supreme Court of Ohio


62 Ohio St. 3d 236; 581 N.E.2d 529; 1991 Ohio LEXIS 2894


September 25, 1991, Submitted  
December 18, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Court of Appeals for Butler County, No. CA89-09-120.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed and cause remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant claimant sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals for Butler County (Ohio), which affirmed a decision that appellee tortfeasors were jointly and severally liable, that Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2305.27 was constitutional, and that § 2305.27 required reduction of the claimant's malpractice award by the amount he received under Medicare Part A.


OVERVIEW: On appeal of the appellate court's judgment, the claimant argued that the trial court erred in determining that § 2305.27 required reduction of the malpractice award by the amount he received under Medicare Part A. The court held that liability was properly allocated among the tortfeasors upon a finding of the claimant's negligence and that § 2305.27 was constitutional. The court found that reducing the verdict against the tortfeasors by deducting Medicare payments was giving the tortfeasors a benefit paid for out of the wages of the claimant. The court determined Medicare Part A was funded by payments made by beneficiaries and their employers, was actuarially determined, and was described by its enabling statute as insurance and that nothing in § 2305.27 required that the premium payments be voluntary. The court concluded that Medicare Part A benefits fell under the definition of "insurance" in § 2305.27 and, therefore, did not reduce medical malpractice damage awards.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's judgment affirming the decision that a reduction of the claimant's malpractice award by the amount he received under Medicare Part A was required and remanded the cause.

62 Ohio St. 3d 118; 580 N.E.2d 1; 1991 Ohio LEXIS 2636

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Court of Appeals for Hamilton County, No. C-890028.

DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order from the Court of Appeals for Hamilton County (Ohio), which convicted defendant of murder and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged a decision convicting him of murder and sentencing him to death. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that the testimony at trial was sufficient to support the jury's findings of guilt. The probative value of photographs outweighed the danger of material prejudice and the trial court was within its discretion in admitting them. The court also found that it was not an abuse of discretion to admit a videotape which served to illustrate testimony, had clear probative value, and was not cumulative or repetitive. The motive for a murder was evident because a missing VCR, money, and watch made it clear that theft was the motive. Thus, the absence of an instruction on motive did not rise to the level of plain error. Regarding the sentence of death, the court found that the aggravating circumstance outweighed the mitigating factors beyond a reasonable doubt.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for murder and sentence of the death penalty because it was not an abuse of discretion to admit a videotape that had probative value and was not cumulative or repetitive. The death penalty was appropriate when aggravating circumstances outweighed mitigating factors beyond a reasonable doubt.

 

224 of 2888 DOCUMENTS


State ex rel. Petroleum Underground Storage Tank Release Compensation Bd. v. Withrow


No. 91-313


Supreme Court of Ohio


62 Ohio St. 3d 111; 579 N.E.2d 705; 1991 Ohio LEXIS 2522; 11 A.L.R.5th 1010


September 10, 1991, Submitted  
November 13, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: In Mandamus.

This is an original action in mandamus brought by relators, the Petroleum Underground Storage Tank Release Compensation Board ("the board") and Richard C. Murray, Executive Director of the Petroleum Underground Storage Tank Financial Assurance Fund. The board is a creation of the Ohio Legislature pursuant to R.C. 3737.87 et seq. (Sub. H.B. No. 421, effective July 11, 1989), and is authorized thereby to issue revenue bonds to be used to supplement "fees" 1 collected from owners and operators of underground storage tanks ("USTs").


1 The Revised Code uses the term "fees" to refer to the assessments collected by the board. See R.C. 3737.91. However, we will not accept the statutory label, but are required to conduct an inquiry into whether the assessments are, in fact, fees and and not taxes.

The purpose of the statutory plan is to protect Ohio's water resources and reduce pollution by creating a fund to reimburse owners and operators of USTs for the costs of corrective actions in the event of a release of petroleum into the environment and in compensate third parties for bodily injury and/or property damage resulting from such occurrences. In addition to establishing the board to oversee the program, the statutory scheme authorizes an annual assessment, and supplemental assessments if necessary, to pay for implementing, administering, and enforcing the program. The annual assessment also goes to pay claims against the fund, and to pay the principal and interest on revenue bonds issued to capitalize the fund. See Sub. H.B. No. 421; R.C. 3737.91(B).

Leaking USTs present a major environmental hazard, and are recognized as a common cause of groundwater pollution in Ohio. When the United States Environmental Protection Agency ("EPA") promulgated regulations requiring owners and operators of USTs to demonstrate financial responsibility, pursuant to Section 6991b(c)(6) and (d), Title 42, U.S. Code, the EPA gave the individual states the opportunity to aid UST owners and operators in satisfying that requirement. See Sections 280.100 and 280.101, Title 40, C.F.R.

 R.C. 3737.87 et seq. was the Ohio Legislature's effort to assist owners and operators of USTs in demonstrating financial responsibility. The statutory plan set up an Assurance Fund comprised of assessments from owners and operators of USTs, with the proceeds to be kept separate from the general fund of the state treasury. R.C. 3737.91. Additionally, the legislature set up a mechanism whereby the board could issue revenue bonds, payable solely from premiums, charges, and "fees" collected by the board from owners and operators of USTs. R.C. 3737.94(B). Proceeds from the bonds are to be used solely to pay cost incurred in correcting and restoring property damaged by leaking USTs. R.C. 3737.92(A)(3). The parties stipulate that the Assurance Fund meets EPA requirements and has been approved by the EPA.

Respondent Mary Ellen Withrow, as the Treasurer of the state of Ohio, is named custodian of the Assurance Fund by R.C. 3737.91(A). When the board attempted to exercise its statutory authority and issue the bonds, it requested that the Treasurer open an account for receipt and disbursement of proceeds from the bond sale, and also requested that she disburse moneys from the Assurance Fund to be debt service on the bonds. The Treasurer refused, effectively blocking the board's attempt to issue the bonds, because in her opinion the storage tank assessements are taxes, and not fees. The Treasurer contends that the issuance of the bonds would violate the prohibition contained in Section 13, Article VIII of the Ohio Constitution that "* * * moneys raised by taxation shall not be obligated or pledged for the payment of bonds * * *."

The board not seeks a writ of mandamus to require the Treasurer to perform her statutory ministerial duties.



DISPOSITION: Writ allowed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relators, state board and fund chairman, brought an original action in mandamus against respondent state treasurer concerning her refusal to open an account for receipt and disbursement of proceeds from a bond sale and disburse moneys from a state fund to be debt service on the bonds. Respondent contended that the assessments that comprised the fund were taxes and that the issuance of the bonds would violate Ohio Const. art. VIII, § 13.


OVERVIEW: Relator state board attempted to exercise its statutory authority and issue revenue bonds payable from assessments paid into a state fund by owners and operators of underground petroleum storage tanks. Respondent state treasurer refused to open an account for receipt and disbursement of proceeds from the bond sale and disburse moneys from the fund to be debt service on the bonds. Relators, state board and fund chairman, brought an original action in mandamus against respondent. Respondent contended that the assessments that comprised the fund were taxes and that the issuance of the bonds would violate Ohio Const. art. VIII, § 13. The court granted the writ and directed respondent to take the actions requested of her by relators. The court held that the assessments were not taxes for purposes of § 13. The assessments were not the type of moneys raised by taxation that were prohibited from being obligated or pledged for the payment of bonds. The court found that the portion of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. ch. 3737 that granted relator board the authority to issue revenue bonds was not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court granted a writ of mandamus to relators, state board and fund chairman, that directed respondent state treasurer to take the actions requested of her. The portion of the statute that granted relator board the authority to issue revenue bonds was not unconstitutional because the assessments were not the type of moneys raised by taxation that were prohibited from being obligated or pledged for the payment of bonds.

Ohio Hosp. Ass'n v. Ohio Dep't of Human Services


No. 90-1580


Supreme Court of Ohio


62 Ohio St. 3d 97; 579 N.E.2d 695; 1991 Ohio LEXIS 2523


September 17, 1991, Submitted  
November 13, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Court of Appeals for Franklin County, No. 88AP-1034.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state department of human services sought review of the judgment from the Court of Appeals, Franklin County (Ohio) and the determinations by other lower courts that Ohio Admin. Code § 5101:3-2-20 was unconstitutional, and that appellant violated 42 U.S.C.S. § 1396a(a)(30(A) by basing its decision of adopting an administrative rule solely on budgetary concerns.


OVERVIEW: Appellant state department of human services sought review of the lower court's determinations that former Ohio Admin. Code § 5101:3-2-20 was unconstitutional, and that appellant violated 42 U.S.C.S. § 1396a(a)(30)(A). The court affirmed the lower court's judgment vacating as moot that portion of the judgment addressing the constitutionality of former Ohio Admin. Code § 5101:3-2-20 because it found that appellee hospitals were entitled to relief under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1396a(a)(30)(A) and under former Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 5111.02(D), and because no court should have indulged a constitutional issue if the litigant was entitled to relief upon other grounds. The court held that § 5101:3-2-20 violated § 1396a(a)(30)(A) because it was implemented solely for budgetary reasons without due consideration of its effect on the quality of care provided by the Medicaid program. The court stated that the reimbursement of monies withheld pursuant to an invalid administrative rule was equitable relief rather than money damages, and the action was consequently not barred by sovereign immunity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment and vacated as moot the portion of the decision addressing the constitutionality of a former state statute reducing Medicaid reimbursement rates. The court held that appellant state department of human services violated the Medicaid statutes that imposed a duty on state programs to adequately reimburse their Medicaid providers and that appellee hospitals were entitled to relief.

State ex rel. Hinkle v. Franklin County Bd. of Elections


No. 91-1845


Supreme Court of Ohio


62 Ohio St. 3d 145; 580 N.E.2d 767; 1991 Ohio LEXIS 2454


October 21, 1991, Submitted  
October 22, 1991, Decided  
October 25, 1991, Released *


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: * Reporter's Note: The writ of mandamus sought in this cause was allowed on October 22, 1991, "consistent with the opinion to follow." See 62 Ohio St.3d 1444, 579 N.E.2d 489. The "opinion to follow" is announced today.
 [***1] Rehearing Denied November 27, 1991. As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: In Mandamus.



DISPOSITION: Writ allowed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator petitioner sought a writ of mandamus to force respondent election board to consider relator's petition for a local option election.


OVERVIEW: Relator petitioner submitted a petition to respondent election board, asking for a local option election. When respondent refused to validate the petition, on the grounds that it violated Am. Sub. H.B. No. 200, § 7, a law that amended Ohio Rev. Code Ann. 4301.01(B)(19) by making the definition of residence district for the purpose of exercising the local option apply retroactively, relator sought a writ of mandamus. The court granted the writ of mandamus because it found that Am. Sub. H.B. No. 200 violated Ohio Const. art. II, §15(D) by addressing more than one subject. The court found that § 7, a local option liquor law, had nothing in common with the bill's other provisions relating to the state judiciary, therefore the court severed § 7 from the bill and declared it invalid so that the remainder of the bill could be given effect. Because § 7 was invalid, it could not be applied to relator and thus respondents could not reject his petition on the grounds that it did not comply with that section. The court refused to invalidate relator's petition for fraud because that determination was respondent's obligation under Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4305.14(C)(1).


OUTCOME: The court granted relator petitioner's writ of mandamus, but only compelled respondent election board to consider that the petition was not required to comply with a current statute.

 

227 of 2888 DOCUMENTS


Scott v. Bank One Trust Co., N.A.


No. 89-1655


Supreme Court of Ohio


62 Ohio St. 3d 39; 577 N.E.2d 1077; 1991 Ohio LEXIS 2284


February 12, 1991, Submitted  
October 9, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: On Order from the United States District Court, Southern District of Ohio, Eastern Division, Certifying Questions of State Law, No. C-2-89-0040.



DISPOSITION: Decision accordingly.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The action came before the court on an order from the United States District Court, Southern District of Ohio, Eastern Division, certifying the following questions of state law for decision by the court: whether Ohio Sup. Ct. Prac. R. XVI, providing for certification of questions to the supreme court, was constitutional, and whether spendthrift trusts were valid and enforceable under Ohio law?


OVERVIEW: Upon being presented with the question of the constitutionality of the practice of federal courts certifying questions of state law to the state supreme court for interpretation and decision, the court determined that certification ensured that federal courts would properly apply state law. The court found that it was not a constitutional question, in that it was not a question of jurisdiction as granted by the constitution, but of deference to state sovereignty. Thus, determined the court, the rule was constitutional. In determining that the spendthrift trust at issue was enforceable, the court noted that the law was clear in Ohio that such provisions were not enforceable, under the doctrine of stare decisis. However, the court reversed the prior precedent, concluding that enforcing such trusts did not exempt the beneficiary's property from creditors' claims because it was not property that the creditors ever had a right to attach, the beneficiary not having a right to it himself when in a position of insolvency. Moreover, the general assembly had not outlawed spendthrift trusts.


OUTCOME: The court held that the practice rule was constitutional, and found that the spendthrift clause in the relevant trust was enforceable under Ohio law, thus overruling prior precedent.

Christensen v. Board of Comm'rs on Grievances & Discipline of Supreme Court


No. 90-1932


Supreme Court of Ohio


61 Ohio St. 3d 534; 575 N.E.2d 790; 1991 Ohio LEXIS 2115


April 23, 1991, Submitted  
August 28, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] In Prohibition.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator attorney sought a writ of prohibition against respondent Board of Commissioners on Grievances and Discipline of the Supreme Court of Ohio to test the constitutionality of an ethical canon.


OVERVIEW: The attorney sought a writ of prohibition to challenge the constitutionality of an ethical canon. The court noted one of the requirements for a writ of prohibition to lie was that the officer against whom the writ was sought was about to exercise judicial or quasi-judicial power. The Disciplinary Counsel's powers were quasi-prosecutorial, not quasi-judicial, and he was thus not subject to a writ of prohibition. Although the Board's powers were quasi-judicial, there was no case pending before the Board and it was thus not "about" to exercise quasi-judicial power. Even if this issue was conceded to the attorney, prohibition was still not a proper remedy because prohibition was not available to determine the constitutionality of a statute or ordinance where a relator had, as did the attorney, an adequate remedy at law by way of appeal. If the attorney were to be charged with misconduct for violating the canon and the Board found misconduct and recommended a disciplinary sanction, the court would then have decided the issue. This disciplinary procedure was the equivalent of appeal, and it was an adequate remedy at law.


OUTCOME: The court denied the writ.

 

229 of 2888 DOCUMENTS


Brady v. Safety-Kleen Corp.


No. 90-166


Supreme Court of Ohio


61 Ohio St. 3d 624; 576 N.E.2d 722; 1991 Ohio LEXIS 2118


March 20, 1991, Submitted  
August 27, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: On Order from the United States District Court, Southern District of Ohio, Eastern Division, Certifying Questions of State Law, No. C2-87-1548.



DISPOSITION: Judgment accordingly.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The United States District Court for the Southern District of Ohio certified to the court the question of whether Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4121.80, governing actions that alleged intentional torts committed by employers against employees, was unconstitutional in whole or in part under the Ohio Constitution.


OVERVIEW: In response to judicial rulings by the state's highest court, the Ohio legislature enacted Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4121.80 to govern actions brought by employees alleging that their employers committed intentional torts against them. Specifically, § 4121.80 prohibited suits against employers for intentional torts. In the lower court, various portions of § 4121.80 were assailed as constitutionally infirm on several grounds. The court found that § 4121.80 was unconstitutional in toto because the legislature did not have the authority to prohibit such suits against employers under its powers found in Ohio Const. art. II, §§ 34, 35. The court found that those constitutional provisions allowed the legislature to limit suits against employers for injuries that arose out of the employment relationship. However, intentional torts inflicted upon employees by their employers, the court found, were outside the scope of the employment relationship. Therefore, the court held that the powers granted to the legislature under Ohio Const. art. II, §§ 34, 35 did not confer the authority to bar suits for intentional torts committed in the workplace.


OUTCOME: The court answered the certified question in the affirmative, finding that the challenged statute was unconstitutional in toto.

Cincinnati v. Ohio Council 8, AFSCME


No. 90-544


Supreme Court of Ohio


61 Ohio St. 3d 658; 576 N.E.2d 745; 1991 Ohio LEXIS 2123


February 6, 1991, Submitted  
August 27, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Court of Appeals for Hamilton County, No. C-880710.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed and cause remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city employees sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeals for Hamilton County (Ohio), which was rendered in favor of appellee city. The issue involved was whether the collective bargaining agreement under the Public Employees' Collective Bargaining Act, Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4117, had prevailed over the municipal home rule charter, Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4117.08(A).


OVERVIEW: Appellant city employees had challenged the lower court's judgment that was rendered in favor of appellee city. The lower appeals court had found the bargaining not listed in the municipal home rule charter, Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4117.08(A), and was outside the scope of the Public Employees' Collective Bargaining Act, Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4117. On appeal, the court disagreed with the lower court's findings and reversed the judgment rendered for appellee. Under Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4117.10(A), the collective bargaining agreement had prevailed over a conflicting municipal home rule charter, which applied equally to contract provisions encompassing mandatory subjects of bargaining and those encompassing permissive subjects of bargaining. However, the court could not determine from the state of the record whether the "PEOPLE" checkoff provision was valid in whole or in part for the term of any particular collective bargaining agreement. The court remanded for further determination on the use of "PEOPLE" funds in the collective bargaining agreement.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's judgment, which was rendered in favor of appellee city, because under the language of the Public Employees' Collective Bargaining Act the collective bargaining agreement had prevailed over the conflicting provision in the municipal home rule charter.

Morris v. Savoy


No. 89-1807


Supreme Court of Ohio


61 Ohio St. 3d 684; 576 N.E.2d 765; 1991 Ohio LEXIS 2117


February 20, 1991, Submitted  
August 27, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: On Order from the United States District Court, Northern District of Ohio, Eastern Division, Certifying Questions of State Law, No. C88-3649-A.



DISPOSITION: Judgment accordingly.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The United States District Court for the Northern District of Ohio certified three questions to the court addressing the constitutionality of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2307.43, setting a $ 200,000 cap on general damages that may be awarded for medical malpractice, and Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2305.27, limits on the collateral source rule and its application to future payments to a claimant.


OVERVIEW: In reviewing the certified questions, the court held that Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2307.43, by setting a $ 200,000 cap on general damages that could be awarded for medical malpractice, was unconstitutional. Regardless of the intent of the statute, there was no evidence that the damage cap had been a factor in medical malpractice insurance rate setting, that is, there was no reasonable relationship to the statute and the legislative intent. The court found it irrational and arbitrary to impose the cost of the intended benefit to the general public solely upon a class consisting of those most severely injured by medical malpractice. Having found the statute unconstitutional on due process grounds, the court stopped short of finding an equal protection defect despite disparate treatment within the class, because the "any conceivable set of facts" test supported a rational-basis argument for the distinctions made for that statute. Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2305.27, regarding limits on the collateral source rule, was constitutional and § 2305.27 was intended to reach certain future payments regarding worker's compensation that were capable of being calculated with reasonable certainty.


OUTCOME: A $ 200,000 cap on general damages that could be awarded for medical malpractice was declared unconstitutional. Statutory limits on the collateral source rule were constitutional and were intended to reach certain workers' compensation future payments capable of being calculated with reasonable certainty.

 

232 of 2888 DOCUMENTS


State v. Claytor


No. 89-499


Supreme Court of Ohio


61 Ohio St. 3d 234; 574 N.E.2d 472; 1991 Ohio LEXIS 1920


April 23, 1991, Submitted  
July 31, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Court of Appeals for Cuyahoga County, No. 53342.
State v. Claytor, 1988 Ohio App. LEXIS 5347 (Ohio Ct. App., Cuyahoga County, Dec. 19, 1988)



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed in part, reversed in part and cause remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Court of Appeals for Cuyahoga County (Ohio), which upheld convictions for aggravated murder with specifications based on an escape conviction and evidence of prior calculation and design. The imposition of the death sentence was upheld.


OVERVIEW: Defendant shot and killed two security guards who approached him and asked him for identification. After he killed the first guard and wounded the second, defendant pursued the wounded man as he attempted to flee and finally killed him with a bullet in the face. Defendant suffered from chronic paranoid schizophrenia and had stopped taking his medication. An arrest warrant had been issued for defendant. The court overturned the death sentence. First, the court held that defendant could not be guilty of escape because he was not in custody at the time of the shooting. A person was considered under "detention" within the meaning of the escape statute, Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2921.34(A), when the person had been arrested. Here, there was no evidence that the guards had intended to arrest defendant. Second, defendant's act in pursuing the wounded guard showed prior calculation and design. However, a life sentence was warranted because the court concluded that the murders were the product of defendant's mental disease such that the court could not find that the aggravating circumstances of the crime were shown to outweigh the mitigating factors by proof beyond a reasonable doubt.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and reversed in part the judgment of the court of appeals. Defendant's sentence of death was reversed and the matter remanded for resentencing on two counts of aggravated murder based on the escape conviction and for the imposition of a life sentence on another death-penalty-eligible count alleging prior calculation and design.

Shover v. Cordis Corp.


No. 90-332


Supreme Court of Ohio


61 Ohio St. 3d 213; 574 N.E.2d 457; 1991 Ohio LEXIS 1923; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P12,955


January 22, 1991, Submitted  
July 31, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied September 18, 1991. As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Court of Appeals for Montgomery County, No. 11636.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant son, individually and as personal representative of his deceased mother, challenged a judgment from the Court of Appeals for Montgomery County (Ohio), which affirmed a trial court decision that a wrongful death action against appellee manufacturer of a defective heart pacemaker surgically implanted in his mother was time-barred by Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2125.02(D).


OVERVIEW: Appellant son, individually and as personal representative of his deceased mother, challenged a trial court judgment holding that a wrongful death claim was time-barred by Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2125.02(D). The mother was never notified of the recall of a heart pacemaker surgically implanted in her. It was undisputed that her pacemaker was registered with appellee manufacturer. After the statute of limitations had run, the son discovered from a newspaper article that certain former officials of the manufacturer had been indicted for allegedly conspiring to cover-up pacemaker defects. The son argued that either the fraud or the discovery rule tolled the statute of limitations. The appellate court disagreed and affirmed the judgment because an action for wrongful death was a statutorily created right and compliance with the period of limitation was a condition precedent to the right to maintain the action. The appellate court held that fraud tolled a statute of limitations only where fraud was the gist of the action. While noting that an action for fraud could still be filed, the appellate court also held that the discovery rule did not apply to wrongful death actions.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed because the wrongful death action was properly dismissed on the basis of the statute of limitations.

State v. Bonnell

OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of aggravated murder and sentenced to death. Finally, the court held that the aggravating circumstances outweighed the evidence presented in mitigation beyond a reasonable doubt and that the imposition of the death penalty on defendant was neither excessive nor disproportionate.

Westlake v. Mascot Petroleum Co.

Supreme Court of Ohio

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals, Cuyahoga County (Ohio) that the city was without authority to extinguish the privilege of appellee service station to sale alcoholic beverages.

State v. Watson


No. 89-1079


Supreme Court of Ohio


61 Ohio St. 3d 1; 572 N.E.2d 97; 1991 Ohio LEXIS 1339


March 6, 1991, Submitted  
June 19, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Court of Appeals for Butler County, No. CA88-02-014.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed in part, reversed in part and cause remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Court of Appeals for Butler County (Ohio), which affirmed his convictions of aggravated murder, aggravated robbery, a gun specification, and imposition of the death penalty.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of a capital crime and sentenced to the death penalty. The court affirmed the convictions for aggravated murder, aggravated robbery, and the gun specification, but reversed as to imposition of the death sentence. Upon independent review of the evidence pursuant to Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2929.05, the court concluded that the death sentence was not appropriate. The court held that although defendant was convicted of the crimes beyond a reasonable doubt, residual doubt remained. Defendant's counsel called no witnesses to testify to such doubt, which may have influenced the jury's death sentence recommendation. Four disinterested eyewitnesses testified under oath that defendant was not the man they saw running from the crime scene. There was a question as to the credibility of the prosecution's witnesses, two of whom saw the perpetrator only briefly, and one of whom had a motive to protect a different suspect. Circumstantial evidence pointed to someone else as the perpetrator. Finally, the jury may have felt that any residual doubt would be confirmed or denied by the judge in imposing the sentence. The case was remanded for imposition of a life sentence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction of aggravated murder, aggravated robbery, and the gun specification, but reversed as to imposition of the death sentence. Although defendant was convicted of the crimes beyond a reasonable doubt, residual doubt remained and was inadequately presented to the jury. The court remanded to the trial court for imposition of a life sentence.

State ex rel. Brookpark Entertainment, Inc. v. Cuyahoga County Bd. of Elections


No. 90-2255


Supreme Court of Ohio


60 Ohio St. 3d 44; 573 N.E.2d 596; 1991 Ohio LEXIS 1169


January 22, 1991, Submitted  
May 29, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied July 3, 1991. As Corrected.


PRIOR HISTORY: IN PROHIBITION.

IN MANDAMUS.

Relator, Brookpark Entertainment, Inc., d.b.a. Crazy Horse Saloon ("Brookpark"), operates a nightclub that sells liquor on the authority of permits issued by respondent, Ohio Department of Liquor Control Director John Hall. On October 2, 1989, the Department of Liquor Control issued a notice of hearing that cited Brookpark for two violations of liquor control laws. The notice identified acts that occurred on August 7, 1989 and set October 24, 1989 as the date for a hearing before the Liquor Control Commission. The hearing went forward as scheduled, and, in an order mailed November 16, 1989, the commission found that Brookpark had committed one of the violations charged.

On or about August 23, 1990, six petitions and a copy of the commission's order were filed, pursuant to R.C. 4301.331, with respondent Cuyahoga County Board of Elections to initiate the local option election process. R.C. 4301.331 allows permit holders to protest such petitions, but Brookpark did not file a protest. Thus, in accordance with R.C. Chapter 4301, the following question was printed on the ballot for the general election to  [***2] be held on November 6, 1990:

"Shall the sale of spirituous liquor, mixed beverages, wine & beer by Brookpark Entertainment, Inc., d.b.a. Crazy Horse Saloon at 16600 Brookpark Road, Cleveland, Ohio 44135, be permitted in this precinct?"

On or about October 23, 1990, Brookpark sought declaratory and injunctive relief in federal district court to prevent this exercise of the local option privilege. Brookpark asserted the unconstitutionality of Ohio laws permitting revocation of liquor permits by local option. On the day of the election, the district court dismissed Brookpark's action for lack of jurisdiction, finding that the case could be resolved under state law and, thus, that it did not present a federal question. In doing so, however, the court concluded: (1) that the August 23, 1990 petitions were timely under R.C. 4301.331 because they had been filed within one year after October 1, 1989, which, according to the court, was the date that Brookpark's violation occurred; and (2) that the November 6, 1990 election was untimely under R.C. 4301.321 because it was not being held within one year after October 1, 1989. The court also rejected Brookpark's constitutional arguments.

The [***3] question challenging Brookpark's liquor permits was submitted to the voters at the general election. A majority of votes opposed liquor sales by Brookpark in the relevant precinct.

Several days after the election, Brookpark asked the board of elections not to certify the results of the local option election. To justify this request, Brookpark cited the district court's conclusion that the election was untimely. The board apparently did not respond, and Brookpark filed the instant action for extraordinary relief on November 16, 1990.

Brookpark's complaint seeks a writ of prohibition to prevent respondent Secretary of State and the board of elections from canvassing the local option election returns, certifying the election results, and notifying the Director of Liquor Control about these results. Brookpark also wants a writ of prohibition to prevent the director from "cancelling and picking up" Brookpark's liquor permits as R.C. 4301.362 requires after a "no" vote. In the alternative, Brookpark seeks a writ of mandamus ordering the board and Secretary of State to impound the uncertified election results and ordering the director to disregard any notice he may receive about these [***4] results.



DISPOSITION: Motion granted and writs denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator nightclub operator filed a complaint seeking a writ of prohibition to prevent respondents Secretary of State and board of elections from canvassing certain local option election returns, certifying the results and notifying the Director of Liquor Control, or in the alternative seeking a writ of mandamus ordering respondents to impound uncertified election results. The Secretary and board moved for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: A company which operated a nightclub was cited for two violations of liquor control laws. A hearing was held and notice that the operator was found to have committed one of the violations was mailed. Sometime thereafter, the local option election process was initiated. The operator failed to protest the petitions used to initiate the process. Prior to the election, the operator's request for an injunction was dismissed by the federal court, which found it had no jurisdiction but which did determine that the petitions were timely but the election was not. The election was held and a majority of votes opposed liquor sales by the operator in the relevant precinct. When the board refused its request not to certify the election results, the operator filed its complaint seeking a writ of prohibition or in the alternative a writ of mandamus. The court held that: (1) a writ of prohibition did not lie because the actions of the Secretary and the board did not constitute an exercise of quasi-judicial authority; (2) the prior determination that the election was void was not conclusive; and (3) mandamus did not lie because the acts at issue were statutory duties, and not discretionary.


OUTCOME: The court denied the operator's writs for prohibition and mandamus and granted the motion for summary judgment filed by the Secretary and the board.

 

238 of 2888 DOCUMENTS


State v. Malinovsky


No. 90-317


Supreme Court of Ohio


60 Ohio St. 3d 20; 573 N.E.2d 22; 1991 Ohio LEXIS 1115


February 5, 1991, Submitted  
May 15, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Lorain County, No. 89CA004592.

On March 9, 1989, the Grand Jury of Lorain County returned a four-count indictment against John Malinovsky, the Chief of Police for the city of Lorain. The indictment charged Malinovsky with extortion, perjury, obstructing official business, and interfering with civil rights. The state alleged that Malinovsky ordered a police officer under his command, Sergeant Wayne Long, to intimidate two fire inspectors into overlooking fire code violations at a bingo parlor run by Malinovsky's daughter.

The state's case was based on the testimony of the two fire inspectors. From the outset, the state intended to have the fire inspectors testify as to what Long said to them, which testimony directly implicated Malinovsky. The state also intended to enter into evidence a tape recording of a telephone conversation between Long and Roger A. Thomas, one of the fire inspectors, which implicated Malinovsky in a like manner.

Recognizing potential hearsay problems with this evidence and the critical nature of same to its case, the state attempted to secure a ruling on the admissibility of the evidence through pretrial [***2] motions. On May 12, 1989, the state filed a "Notice of Intention to Use Evidence" in an effort to elicit a motion pursuant to Crim. R. 12 from Malinovsky. On May 30, 1989, the state filed a "Motion to Compel Defense to File Objections" concerning the admissibility of the telephone conversation recording. This motion was overruled and the case proceeded to trial without resolving any questions concerning the admissibility of either the recording or the testimony crucial to the state's case.

At trial, the state's fears were realized. When the state asked Anthony M. Cuevas, the other fire inspector, to recount what Long said that Malinovsky had said, the defense objected and the court sustained the objection. The state argued that the testimony was not hearsay because it was a statement made by a co-conspirator in furtherance of a conspiracy pursuant to Evid. R. 801(D)(2)(e). However, the trial court apparently found that the state had not met its burden under the rule requiring independent proof of the conspiracy.

The court then recessed, during which time the state filed notice that it was taking a Crim. R. 12(J) appeal from this adverse ruling by the trial court. Although failing [***3] initially to do so, by the end of the recess the state had certified that this appeal was not taken for the purpose of delay and that the court's evidentiary ruling had destroyed the state's case. The trial court ordered the state to continue with its case, despite the appeal. After a faltering attempt to continue, the state announced that it could not go forward without the excluded testimony. The court then dismissed the case for failure to prosecute.

The Court of Appeals for Lorain County dismissed the appeal, holding that Crim. R. 12(J) does not provide for a mid-trial appeal of an evidentiary ruling. The appellate court also held that the Double Jeopardy Clause barred future prosecution.

The cause is now before this court upon the allowance of a motion for leave to appeal.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed and cause remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State challenged a decision of the Court of Appeals for Lorain County (Ohio), which dismissed the State's appeal, holding that Ohio R. Crim. P. 12(J) did not provide for a mid-trial appeal of an evidentiary ruling. The appellate court also held that the Double Jeopardy Clause barred future prosecution of defendant. Defendant was charged with extortion, perjury, obstructing official business, and interfering with civil rights.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a police chief, allegedly ordered an officer under his command to intimidate two fire inspectors into overlooking fire code violations. The State planned to have the inspectors testify as to what the officer said to them, which testimony directly implicated defendant. Recognizing the potential hearsay problems with the evidence, the State tried to secure a ruling on the admissibility of the evidence through pretrial motions, however defendant did not respond and the case proceeded to trial. At trial, defense's objection to the State's evidence was sustained. The State could not move forward without this evidence and informed the trial court that it was taking an Ohio R. Crim. P. 12(J) appeal from the adverse evidentiary ruling. The State certified that the appeal was not taken for the purpose of delay and that the trial court's ruling had destroyed its case. The trial court ordered the State to continue and dismissed the case for failure to prosecute when the State could not go forward. The appellate court dismissed the State's appeal. On appeal, the court reversed and held that Ohio R. Crim. P. 12(J) allowed for expedited appeals of evidentiary rulings during trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals and remanded the cause to the court of appeals for consideration of the State's appeal of the evidentiary ruling.
