Klepper v. Ohio Bd. of Regents


No. 90-977


Supreme Court of Ohio


59 Ohio St. 3d 131; 570 N.E.2d 1124; 1991 Ohio LEXIS 1044


March 6, 1991, Submitted  
May 1, 1991, Decided


NOTICE:

 [***1] .


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL and CROSS-APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Franklin County, No. 89AP-277.

Prior to 1987, Michael M. Klepper, appellee and cross-appellant, was enrolled as an undergraduate student at the Ohio State University ("OSU"), an appellant and cross-appellee. While attending OSU, appellee, an Ohio resident, was required to pay the in-state tuition rate established for Ohio residents and he financed his education, in part, with Ohio Instructional Grants. 1


1 See R.C. 3333.12.

In 1986, the General Assembly enacted R.C. 3345.32. R.C. 3345.32(C) requires that a "state university or college," 2 such as OSU, 3 charge all male students born after December 31, 1959, any tuition surcharge applicable to students who are not Ohio residents if the student fails to file a "statement of selective service status" 4 with the state university or college. A statement of selective service status certifies that the student is either registered with the selective service system in accordance with the Military Selective Service Act, 5 or that he is not required to register. 6 R.C.  [***2] 3345.32(D) prohibits the Ohio Student Loan Commission from granting or guaranteeing a loan for any male student born after December 31, 1959, who fails to file the statement with the commission and, pursuant to R.C. 3345.32(E), the student is also ineligible for, inter alia, instructional grants unless he has filed the statement with appellant and cross-appellee Ohio Board of Regents. These statutory mandates were first implemented at OSU beginning in the winter quarter of 1987.


2 R.C. 3345.32(A)(1) provides as follows:

"'State university or college' means the institutions described in section 3345.27 of the Revised Code, the northeastern Ohio universities college of medicine, and the medical college of Ohio at Toledo."


3 See R.C. 3345.32(A)(1), 3345.27(A) and 3345.011.


4 R.C. 3345.32(A)(3) provides:

"'Statement of selective service status' means a statement certifying one of the following:

"(a) That the individual filing the statement has registered with the selective service system in accordance with the 'Military Selective Service Act,' 62 Stat. 604, 50 U.S.C. App. 453, as amended;

"(b) That the individual filing the statement is not required to register with the selective service for one of the following reasons:

"(i) He is under eighteen or over twenty-six years of age;

"(ii) He is on active duty with the armed forces of the United States other than for training in a reserve or national guard unit;

"(iii) He is a nonimmigrant alien lawfully in the United States in accordance with section 101(a)(15) of the 'Immigration and Nationality Act,' U.S.C. 1101, as amended;

"(iv) He is not a citizen of the United States and is a permanent resident of the trust territory of the Pacific Islands or the Northern Mariana Islands[.]"

 [***3] 

5 See App. 453, Title 50, U.S. Code.


6 See R.C. 3345.32(B).

Appellee was born after December 31, 1959 and is required by law to register with the selective service system. However, based upon his opposition to a military draft, appellee did not register with selective service and, therefore, he was unable to honestly file a "statement of selective service status" referenced in R.C. 3345.32. As a result, in January 1987, the beginning of the winter quarter at OSU, appellee was charged an out-of-state tuition surcharge and was denied instructional grants based upon his failure to file a statement of selective service status. Consequently, appellee claims he was unable to continue his education at OSU.

On October 26, 1987, appellee filed a complaint in the Court of Common Pleas of Franklin County against appellants and cross-appellees, the Ohio Board of Regents, OSU, and the Ohio Attorney General. In his complaint, appellee sought to have R.C. 3345.32 declared unconstitutional and, further, sought to enjoin appellants from enforcing the statute.

Subsequently, appellants moved for judgment on the pleadings pursuant to Civ. R. 12(C). 7 The trial court granted appellants'  [***4] motion and held that R.C. 3345.32 does not contravene the Ohio Constitution.


7 Civ. R. 12(C) provides as follows:

"After pleadings are closed but within such time as not to delay the trial, any party may move for judgment on the pleadings."

On appeal, the court of appeals determined that R.C. 3345.32(C) violates Section 2, Article I, and Section 32, Article II of the Ohio Constitution. Accordingly, the court of appeals reversed, in part, the judgment of the trial court. However, the court of appeals affirmed the judgment of the trial court with regard to the constitutionality of R.C. 3345.32(D) and (E).

The cause is now before this court pursuant to the allowance of a motion and cross-motion to certify the record.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a board of regents and a state university, sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals for Franklin County (Ohio), which determined that Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 3345.32(C) violated Ohio Const. art. I, § 2. Appellee student sought review of the decision by the appellate court that Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 3345.32(D) and (E) were constitutional in the student's suit challenging the constitutionality of the statutes.


OVERVIEW: The student was enrolled in the state university and paid in-state tuition. He relied in part on grants and failed to register for the draft. Pursuant to Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 3345.32(C), the state university was obligated to charge him out-of-state tuition and pursuant to Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 3345.32(D) and (E), he was no longer eligible for financial aid. The student claimed that the statutes prevented him from attending college. On appeal, the court affirmed in part and reversed in part the appellate court's decision and found that Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 3345.32 (C), (D), and (E) did not violate Ohio Const. art. I, § 2. In determining that the statutes were rationally related to a legitimate governmental interest, the court found that the State had a stake in the maintenance of a military force. The court found that the statutes did not create improper classifications because non-residents who attended state schools and residents who attended private institutions did not receive state-subsidized tuition and could be properly excluded from the classification created by the statutes because they were not similarly situated to those who fell within the statutory classification.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the appellate court that found that statute requiring appellants to charge the student out-of-state tuition due to his failure to register for the draft unconstitutional and affirmed the appellate court's decision that found the statutes revoking financial aid to the student constitutional.

 

240 of 2888 DOCUMENTS


ComTech Systems, Inc. v. Limbach


No. 90-661


Supreme Court of Ohio


59 Ohio St. 3d 96; 570 N.E.2d 1089; 1991 Ohio LEXIS 957


January 16, 1991, Submitted  
April 24, 1991, Decided


NOTICE:

 [***1] .


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from the Board of Tax Appeals, Nos. 87-B-339 and 87-D-340.

ComTech Systems, Inc., appellant, provides consulting services for its clients. It studies clients' systems analysis requirements, issues feasibility studies, and evaluates different information alternatives for clients. It provides conceptual detail designs, writes and codes programs, and designs, modifies, and debugs computer software programs. ComTech does not sell hardware or off-the-shelf software, process others' information on its computers, keypunch for its clients, or sell access to its computers.

ComTech began collecting sales tax from its clients on July 1, 1983, the effective date of Am. Sub. H.B. No. 291. On January 28, 1986, it filed three refund claims for taxes collected from July through December 1983, 1984, and January through October 1985. It attached authorizations from its clients, in which the clients authorized ComTech to file the claims and agreed to await reimbursement from ComTech. See Columbus Southern Lumber Co. v. Peck (1953), 159 Ohio St. 564, 566-567, 50 O.O. 457, 458, 113 N.E. 2d 1, 3.

 [***2] The Tax Commissioner, appellee, refunded the tax collected after January 10, 1985, the effective date of Sub. S.B. No. 112. She interpreted this Act to relieve ComTech of the duty to collect the tax after the Act's effective date. However, she denied refund of amounts collected before this date.

On appeal, the Board of Tax Appeals ("BTA") affirmed the commissioner's orders. As to ComTech's constitutional challenges, the BTA found that it had no authority to declare the statute unconstitutional. The BTA also rejected ComTech's arguments that Sub. S.B. No. 112 should apply retroactively and that ComTech's services were personal services and excepted under Emery Industries, Inc. v. Limbach (1989), 43 Ohio St. 3d 134, 539 N.E. 2d 608. Instead, the BTA found that ComTech's clients' overriding purpose was to receive the software.

This cause is before this court upon an appeal as of right.



DISPOSITION: Decision affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant software company collected sales taxes from its customers and applied for refunds from appellee tax commissioner. The tax commissioner interpreted a new act as relieving the software company of the duty to collect the tax after the act's effective date. However, she denied refund of amounts collected before this date. The Ohio Board of Tax Appeals affirmed the commissioner's orders. The software company appealed.


OVERVIEW: The software company argued that under Ohio Rev. Code § 5739.01(B)(5), contrary to the board's finding, its services were personal or professional services and exempt. The commissioner responded that the § 5739.01(B)(5) personal services exemption and case law interpreting it did not apply to automatic data processing and computer services because this transaction had its own definition for these services. The court agreed. The court also found that the statute was not a manifestly gross and fraudulent violation of the one-subject rule. The statute was an appropriations bill and dealt with the operations of the state government. It might contain a new object of taxation because the tax funds government operations described elsewhere in the Act. The commissioner argued that the court had no jurisdiction over either constitutional challenge because the software company did not include these errors in its notice of appeal to the board. The board correctly noted that the court could decide the one-subject question without extrinsic evidence. The court need only examine the Act to decide that question. Consequently, the software company properly raised that challenge.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the board of tax appeals' decision.

 

241 of 2888 DOCUMENTS


State v. Wiles


No. 88-1265


Supreme Court of Ohio


59 Ohio St. 3d 71; 571 N.E.2d 97; 1991 Ohio LEXIS 960


October 16, 1990, Submitted  
April 24, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied May 29, 1991, Reported at 1991 Ohio LEXIS 3097. As Amended.
Rehearing denied by State v. Wiles, 60 Ohio St. 3d 713, 573 N.E.2d 673, 1991 Ohio LEXIS 3097 (1991)
Writ of certiorari denied Wiles v. Ohio, 506 U.S. 832, 113 S. Ct. 99, 121 L. Ed. 2d 59, 1992 U.S. LEXIS 5786 (1992)
Motion granted by State v. Wiles, 2011 Ohio 2861, 2011 Ohio LEXIS 1536 (Ohio, June 14, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Portage County, No. 1675.

Charles and Carol Klima are owners of Shakespeare Acres, a horse farm in Rootstown, Ohio, where they have resided since 1969. On June 22, 1970, Mark, their only child, was born. Beginning in 1982, the Klimas employed defendant-appellant Mark W. Wiles as a part-time laborer on the farm. In late 1982 or early 1983, Wiles was discovered by Mrs. Klima departing from her home. When asked about his presence in the house, appellant informed Mrs. Klima that he was cold and had entered the home in order to get warm. He was thereafter instructed to turn up the heat in the tack room and use it for that purpose in the future. On another occasion, appellant was found in the garage of the house slumped below an open exterior window. On neither occasion had appellant been given permission to enter the residence.

On the morning of January 19, 1983, appellant reported for work at Shakespeare Acres. At approximately 1:30 p.m., Carol Klima returned to the residence after tending to [***2] the horses. When she entered the kitchen she felt a draft but was uncertain from where it originated. After eating lunch, she proceeded to her bedroom whereupon she discovered an open window and the screen thereto on the ground below. Moreover, the doors to the bedroom closet and the drawers to the dresser had been opened. Subsequent investigation revealed that approximately $ 200 in coin and currency were missing from the residence. Only Carol Klima and appellant were present on the grounds of the farm at the time of the thefts. Despite the fact that he was owed money for work he had performed for the Klimas, appellant never returned to the farm to collect his paycheck.

On August 5, 1985, Mark Klima remarked to his mother that he had seen appellant in the main barn of the estate. Mark Klima mentioned that he had spoken with appellant and that appellant asked Mark whether he recognized him. Mark Klima reportedly stated: "Yes, you're Mark Wiles." When appellant stated that he wished to speak to Carol Klima, her son directed appellant to the residence. While appellant initially proceeded to the house, when the family dog began to bark he turned around and left.

At approximately [***3] 4:30 or 5:00 a.m. on August 7, 1985, appellant arrived at the Klima farm. While obscured in an adjacent field, appellant watched for any activity at the home. Carol, Charles and Mark Klima, Anne Marie O'Brien and Susie and Laura Kerper were present in the Klima residence. Anne Marie and the Kerper girls stayed on the farm to assist in the maintenance of the horses and to receive horseback riding lessons. At approximately 6:00 a.m., Charles Klima left for work. Early that morning, Ms. O'Brien also departed for her place of employment. The remaining members of the household rose around 8:00 a.m. and had breakfast. Thereafter, Susie and Laura left the residence to feed the horses. At approximately 10:15 a.m., Carol Klima also left the residence. Prior to departing, she instructed her son, who was in his bedroom, to close the windows in the house if it began to rain. The doors to the house were left unlocked. Mark Klima apparently left the house shortly thereafter. After appellant observed the Klimas, the Kerpers and O'Brien leave, he entered the house and locked the door. He thereupon rummaged through the bedroom closet and dresser of Carol and Charles Klima and a desk in [***4] the living room. As appellant continued searching a china cabinet in the living room for valuables, he was confronted by Mark Klima who had reentered the house. In an ensuing scuffle, appellant stabbed Mark Klima repeatedly with a kitchen knife. Thereafter, appellant departed the residence with approximately $ 260 and returned to his home.

Meanwhile, after leaving the residence, Carol Klima proceeded to an outdoor exercising area and began to provide horseback riding instruction to Judy Campbell, who had arrived for a lesson. When it began raining, the lesson was moved inside a barn. In the course of the instruction, Susie Kerper approached Carol Klima and informed her that her attempt to retrieve some raincoats from the house was unsuccessful because the house was locked. A short while later the rain ceased and the instruction continued outside. At this time, Susie and Laura Kerper were instructed to return to the house and prepare for an upcoming horse show. They were instructed where to find a key to the house. Shortly thereafter, Laura Kerper returned from the house and informed Carol Klima that Mark was unconscious on the floor covered in blood. Mrs. Klima ran to the [***5] house, called medics and attempted to administer artificial respiration to her son. In the course of her attempt to revive Mark, Carol Klima discovered a kitchen knife buried in his back. When the medics arrived, they pulled up Mark's shirt revealing multiple stab wounds in his back. It was later determined by the coroner that two of the twenty-four total wounds were lethal and another two were "possibly lethal." Mark was transported to the emergency room where he was pronounced dead.

At approximately 4:00 p.m. on August 7, 1985, Chief Detective Duane Kaley and Detective Donald Doak of the Portage County Sheriff's office and Officer Larry Hanks of the Akron Police Department visited the house appellant shared with Gary Benden and Valerie Marshall. The police officers encountered Gary Benden outside the residence and asked to speak with appellant. Benden replied that appellant was inside the residence and, at the request of the officers, returned to the home to notify appellant of their presence. Appellant responded by requesting Benden to tell the officers that he was not home. When appellant was informed that the officers were already aware that he was inside the residence,  [***6] appellant initially attempted to hide in the basement. At the urging of Benden, he eventually departed the house and approached the police officers. After being apprised of his rights, Wiles spoke with Chief Detective Kaley. During the course of this conversation, appellant denied that he had been to the Klima residence earlier in the day. Appellant remained outside with the officers for approximately forty-five minutes to one hour. At the conclusion of their discussion with appellant, the police officers departed. Shortly thereafter, appellant paid Benden $ 150 for rent and fled Akron.

On August 12, 1985, appellant contacted the Savannah, Georgia, police department, announced that he was wanted for murder in Ohio and wished to surrender to authorities. At approximately 10:25 a.m., Officer Joe Johnson was dispatched to 35010 Ogeechee Road, whereupon he was approached by appellant who stated that he was the person for whom the officer was looking.

Appellant thereafter identified himself. Once his identity was confirmed by the dispatcher, Officer Johnson resumed his conversation with appellant, who stated again that he wished to surrender to face charges for murder. Following [***7] this conversation, Officer Johnson requested proof of identification from appellant, who produced his driver's license. The information contained therein was relayed to the police dispatcher who confirmed that a warrant for appellant's arrest was outstanding. Appellant was informed of his rights, handcuffed and placed in the police cruiser for transportation to the station. No conversation occurred between Officer Johnson and appellant during the trip.

At approximately 11:48 a.m. on August 12, 1985, appellant was taken to a conference room at the police station and informed of his rights by Detective J. E. Brinson. Utilizing a form listing these rights, Detective Brinson placed a check mark next to each enumerated right as he read it to appellant and as appellant responded that he understood that right. Appellant then was asked again if he understood his rights and if, with those rights in mind, he still wished to answer questions. Appellant signified his affirmative response by placing check marks and his initials in the appropriate blanks, and then signing the form.

After initially denying any involvement, appellant thereafter described to Detective Brinson and Detective Everett [***8] Ragan his commission of the homicide and his activities before, during and after the act. He subsequently repeated his recollections in the presence of a stenographer who later prepared a transcript of the statement. This statement was reviewed by appellant, who acknowledged its accuracy by affixing his signature thereto.

At approximately 7:40 p.m. on August 12, 1985, Portage County Detectives Kaley and Doak arrived in Savannah, where they were transported to the police station by Savannah Police Detective Bob Scott. Once at the station, the three officers proceeded to a police interview room in which appellant was waiting. Prior to any questioning, appellant was again advised of his rights.

Moreover, appellant was presented with a form apprising him of his rights and he again indicated in writing that he understood them but nevertheless wished to speak with the detectives without presence of counsel. Appellant thereupon repeated the substance of his earlier statements implicating himself in the crime. The conversation between the detectives and appellant was taped and subsequently transcribed.

Appellant thereafter waived extradition and was returned to Ohio. On August 16,  [***9] 1985, appellant was indicted by the Portage County Grand Jury on one count of aggravated murder and two counts of aggravated burglary. Count One, aggravated murder, contained the following two specifications: (1) appellant was the principal offender in the commission of the murder while committing aggravated burglary in violation of R.C. 2929.04(A)(7), and (2) appellant committed the murder on August 7, 1985 for the purpose of escaping detection, apprehension, trial or punishment for another crime. Count Two, aggravated burglary, contained a specification that appellant had been previously convicted of an aggravated felony. Count Three charged appellant with the commission of aggravated burglary on or about January 19, 1983.

On August 23, 1985, appellant filed a motion to sever Count Three of the indictment from the remaining counts. On September 27, 1985, appellant filed with the Portage County Common Pleas Court a motion to suppress any oral or written statements made by him. Contemporaneously therewith, a motion to increase the burden of proof to a "beyond all doubt" standard was filed by appellant. A hearing on the motion to suppress was held on October 23, 1985 wherein the [***10] motion was amended to encompass statements about which the defense had become aware since its filing.

On December 2, 1985, appellant filed a motion for waiver of jury trial. A three-judge panel was appointed by the Chief Justice on December 19, 1985. At a pretrial conference on December 30, 1985, the court inquired of the prosecutor whether discovery had been completed. In response, the prosecuting attorney replied:

"There are -- apparently there were some statements made by the defendant in jail within the last week or two which we're going to provide to them today."

Later in the colloquy between the prosecutor and the court, the following exchange took place:

"THE COURT: It's then complete and you will not offer anything into evidence that has not been given to them as of today?

"MR. PLOUGH: As far as I know, your Honor, unless we accidently [sic] overlooked something.

"THE COURT: No, we're going to go along, we're going to say that as of today you tell me that you have given them discovery.

"MR. PLOUGH: That is correct, your Honor.

"THE COURT: I don't want you to find something as of tomorrow, because you won't be permitted to use it. That is why I'm asking what you've  [***11] provided.

"MR. PLOUGH: As far as I know, your Honor, we have provided them everything. They have seen everything. * * *"

On January 2, 1986, the state filed a supplemental disclosure statement pursuant to Crim. R. 16 identifying evidence previously requested by appellant and a notice of intent to use such evidence at trial. Among the witnesses disclosed was Portage County Corrections Officer Keith Jirousek. Also on January 2, 1986, the trial court denied appellant's motion to suppress his pretrial statements, his motion to increase the burden of proof beyond all doubt and his motion to sever Count Three of the indictment.

On January 6, 1986, trial commenced before the three-judge panel of the Portage County Common Pleas Court. Carol Klima was called to testify on behalf of the state. In the course of her testimony, Mrs. Klima stated that two days before his death, her son had related to her how he had spoken to appellant and, in response to appellant's question, was able to identify appellant as Mark Wiles. No objection was interposed as to this testimony.

On January 8, 1986, the state sought to have Keith Jirousek testify as a prosecution witness. Appellant objected, claiming [***12] that the failure of the state to provide the name of Jirousek in response to defense requests prior to the December 30, 1985 deadline imposed by the trial court for completion of discovery barred the use of such testimony. Following deliberation by the panel, the objection was overruled and Officer Jirousek was permitted to take the stand. He testified that appellant, during his incarceration, remarked as follows:

"'I want transferred out of this fucking jail before I fucking kill someone else.'"

Appellant objected to this testimony on the basis of relevancy. The objection was overruled. Officer Jirousek further testified that appellant also stated:

"'How come you're treating me like a kid or that? Is it because I killed one of your boys?'"

No objection was made to this testimony. After the state had completed its case, appellant advanced a motion to dismiss Count Three of the indictment (i.e., the January 19, 1983 aggravated burglary charge) on the basis of insufficient evidence. This motion was granted by the court.

At the close of evidence, counsel for the respective parties proceeded to closing argument. In the course of his argument to the bench, the prosecuting attorney [***13] made the following comments:

"I know that this has been a rather lengthy trial for a trial to Court and I ask you to bear with me for just a few more minutes, not for myself, but for Mark Klima, a 15 year old boy. A boy, a son that every parent would dream about having. A four-point student and an accomplished writer. A shy and quiet boy. A boy who liked computers, who hated violence. To sum it up, just an ordinary kid.

"A child whose life was snuffed out by an ogre. Ogre. Just like the book he was reading, a man-eating monster, a hideous, brutish person who turned him from this (indicating exhibit), into this."

Following submission of the case to the court on January 9, 1986, appellant was found guilty of Counts One and Two of the indictment and the specifications relative thereto.

On January 27, 1986, the penalty phase of the present action commenced. Following the submission of mitigating evidence, the court, on January 29, 1986, rendered the following determinations:

"The Court in accordance with Section 2929.03, Subsection F, makes the following specific findings:

"A. We find that the Defendant has established the following mitigating factors: One, the youth of the offender,  [***14] specifically 22 years of age: two, the confessions of the offender after he surrendered to police was [sic] mostly truthful and sincere.

"We find that the defendant has not established as mitigating factors the following, paragraphs 1, 2, 3, 5, 6, as set forth in Revised Code Section 2929.04, paragraph B.

"The Court finds that it has been established beyond a reasonable doubt that the aggravating circumstances was [sic] that the defendant was the principal offender, and that he killed Mark Klima in the commission of an aggravated burglary of the Klima residence. The defendant killed Mark Klima for the purpose of escaping -- escaping detection or apprehension or trial or punishment for the aggravated burglary of the Klima dwelling.

"The Court has considered in accordance with 2929.04, Paragraph B, one, the nature and circumstances of the offense; two, the history, character, background of the offender; three, school records and conduct; four, the testimony of officials and records from the Ohio State Reformatory; five, church affiliations; six, drug and alcohol abuse; seven, evidence of remorse; eight, psychological evaluations and opinions; nine, cooperation of the defendant [***15] with law enforcement officials and confessions; ten, and all the factors specified in Subsection B(1) through B(7).

"The Court finds beyond a reasonable doubt that the aggravating circumstances of the offense far outweigh the mitigating factors for the following reasons:

"One, we find the defendant was the principal offender; two, we find that the defendant entered the private home of the victim for the purpose of committing aggravated burglary; three, we find the defendant also planned the burglary and had planned the burglary for several days prior to making an unlawful entry and previously had made an attempt to commit the offense; four, the defendant stabbed the 15 year old victim repeatedly and brutally with the purpose to kill; five, the defendant killed the victim for the purpose of escaping detection and apprehension for the aggravated burglary; six, the defendant gave no aid or assistance to the victim, fled the residence with money stolen from the Klima residence; seven, the victim was only 15 years of age and in his own home at the time of the stabbing and killing and; eight, the defendant, while relatively youthful, had been previously convicted of aggravated burglary,  [***16] refused drug and alcohol treatment, displayed defiance of authority and participated in other criminal activity for which he was not apprehended or convicted.

"The defendant had been incarcerated in Mansfield Reformatory for approximately a year and a half.

"Nine, the defendant fled the jurisdiction using monies stolen in the burglary of the Klima residence; ten, the Court finds that while the defendant was only convicted of one previous felony, the nature of the felony makes it a significant criminal conviction."

The Eleventh District Court of Appeals conducted a mandatory independent review of the death sentence pursuant to R.C. 2929.05. While the appellate court concluded that the trial court had erred in not merging the two specifications relative to Count One of the indictment, it concluded that the error could be remedied by merger of the specifications prior to conducting its own independent weighing of aggravating and mitigating factors. Despite such merger, the court nevertheless concluded that the single aggravating circumstance outweighed the mitigating circumstances set forth by appellant. Accordingly, the court of appeals affirmed the conviction and sentence of death.

 [***17] The cause is now before this court upon an appeal as of right.
State v. Wiles, 1988 Ohio App. LEXIS 2131 (Ohio Ct. App., Portage County, June 3, 1988)



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Eleventh District Court of Appeals (Ohio), which affirmed his convictions for aggravated murder and aggravated burglary and the defendant's sentence of death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found guilty of aggravated murder and aggravated burglary and sentenced to death. The court ruled that the trial court's judgment and sentence were proper. The court held: (1) joinder of an earlier burglary charge with the current offenses was not error because the evidence relative to the former played no role in defendant's convictions on the latter; (2) defendant failed to show that the state's failure to disclose the identity of a witness, who later testified about statements made by the defendant during his confinement, in response to his discovery demand constituted reversible error; (3) the statements made by defendant to the police were voluntary in nature; (4) the statements defendant made to an officer that he sought to suppress were not the product of a custodial interrogation; (5) there was sufficient evidence for the jury to conclude that defendant killed one of his victims for the purpose of escaping detection for the burglary; and (6) the extreme violence employed in the commission of the crime and its perpetration in the home of the victim evidenced that the aggravating circumstance outweighed the mitigating factors beyond a reasonable doubt.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

242 of 2888 DOCUMENTS


State v. Spirko


No. 89-722


Supreme Court of Ohio


59 Ohio St. 3d 1; 570 N.E.2d 229; 1991 Ohio LEXIS 922


October 23, 1990, Submitted  
April 10, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied May 15, 1991. As Amended. Second Amendment. Motion for Delayed Reconsideration Denied November 9, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Ohio LEXIS 2429.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Van Wert County, No. 15-84-22.

Prior to August 1982, Betty Jane Mottinger had been employed as the postmaster in the village of Elgin, Ohio, for approximately five years. Sometime after 8:00 a.m. on August 9, 1982, Mottinger reported to work. Two eyewitnesses reported that they observed a man outside the post office at approximately 8:30 a.m. that morning. Opal Seibert, who lived across the street from the post office, testified that she noticed a strange man standing beside a brown car that she did not recognize. Subsequently, Seibert described the man she saw to a police artist. Upon viewing several photographic arrays, Seibert picked out the picture of Delaney Gibson as the person she saw outside the post office. Gibson was the best friend and former cellmate of defendant-appellant, John George Spirko.

Mark Lewis, a truck driver, testified that he saw a man near a brown car as he was driving past the post office on the date and time in question. Lewis also described the [***2] man he saw to a police artist, and was also shown photographic arrays. From one such array, Lewis picked out a photograph of Spirko and stated that he was seventy percent certain that the picture he chose was of the man he saw outside the Elgin post office. While Lewis had initially chosen the photo of Sonny Baumgardner as a person he thought had similar features to the person he had seen outside the post office, he later decided that Baumgardner was not the man he had seen on the morning of August 9, 1982.

Shortly after 8:30 a.m. on August 9, 1982, it was discovered that Mottinger was missing from the post office, as was her purse, some postal bait money orders, and over $ 700 aggregate in cash and postage stamps. Inspectors from the United States Postal Service were contacted by local officials and an investigation ensued, which included the lifting of fingerprints from the safe and office areas of the post office.

On September 18, 1982, Mottinger's body was discovered by a person searching for butterflies in a field located in Hancock County. The victim's body was wrapped in a canvaslike material, covered with paint spots of various colors, that was secured by a rope and duct [***3] tape. It was later determined that Mottinger's death was caused by approximately fourteen to eighteen stab wounds to her chest and abdomen.

In late October 1982, the defendant, who was then incarcerated in the Lucas County Jail on two pending charges of felonious assault, indicated that he wished to speak to the postal authorities. On October 31, 1982, postal inspector Steve Cline contacted defendant at the jail. At that time, defendant stated that he had knowledge concerning the murder, and wished to exchange his information for the elimination of the remainder of the jail time that he was serving. Several days later, another postal inspector interviewed defendant, and the defendant again indicated that he wished to make a deal with the postal authorities whereby he would provide information concerning the Mottinger murder in exchange for, inter alia, freedom for his girlfriend, LuAnn Smith, who was serving time in jail, and for protection for his family.

Continued contacts between defendant and the postal inspectors resulted in a plea bargain agreement between the prosecutor, defendant and the postal authorities. In accordance with the plea bargain, defendant was inducted [***4] into the Federal Witness Protection Program.

On November 29, 1982, postal inspector Paul Hartman interviewed defendant in jail, and defendant stated that a reddish-blond person told him at a party that the Mottinger murder had been committed by three white males. Defendant further related to Hartman that three white males went to the Elgin post office to claim a parcel containing heroin, and that a scuffle ensued which resulted in the abduction of Mottinger.

Several days later, Hartman again interviewed defendant, and defendant told Hartman that he had been commissioned by someone named Vito to recover the parcel of heroin. Defendant stated that he and one of the persons involved in the murder drove from Toledo to the murder scene where he saw Mottinger's body and noticed that the victim had been stabbed approximately fifteen times. Defendant also told Hartman that he had delivered the heroin to "the dope man," and that this person wanted one of the alleged murderers, who was nicknamed Rooster, to be killed. Defendant stated that Rooster was killed by another of the murderers and buried in a marsh at an unknown location.

On December 8, 1982, Hartman conducted yet another interview [***5] with defendant, and was told that a person named Swartz had informed defendant that Mottinger was dead. Defendant stated that Swartz told him that Rooster wanted the victim to perform oral sex and that Rooster stabbed the victim after she bit him on the penis. Defendant stated he and Swartz went to the house where the murder took place and saw Rooster and a person named Dirty Dan with blood on their clothes. Defendant stated he was told that these individuals had already dumped the victim's body.

During an interview conducted by Hartman with defendant on December 9, 1982, defendant stated that Rooster told him about the murder and that the only thing that Rooster said bothered him was the "whoosh" sound the knife had made when he stabbed the victim. Defendant then related to Hartman that Rooster was killed in a Florida swamp.

On December 13, 1982, another interview was conducted by Hartman, and the defendant stated that he and Swartz were at the house where the murder took place and that he was lying on a couch watching television when he saw the victim running downstairs screaming and crying while Rooster was pursuing her. Defendant stated that the victim then ran out of the [***6] house and he saw Rooster and Dirty Dan grab the victim and stab her.

Later that same day, Hartman conducted another interview with defendant and the defendant stated that three men -- a biker, a man named Dino, and Rooster -- took turns raping the victim. The defendant stated that he, Dino, Rooster, the biker, Dirty Dan and the victim walked outside the house and that the victim tried to run away when Dirty Dan displayed a knife. Defendant stated that he tackled the victim, held her down, and that Rooster then ran up and started stabbing her.

On December 15, 1982, another interview took place, during which defendant said he had seen Rooster stabbing the victim as he and Dino were walking outside around the house.

In yet another interview conducted by Hartman with the defendant, it was stated by defendant that Delaney Gibson told defendant that Gibson and his cohorts had erred in robbing the post office since it did not have any money in it. According to defendant, Gibson told defendant that he and his accomplices raped the victim, and that she jumped out of the assailants' car and started to run. After grabbing the victim, Gibson told defendant that he and his accomplices "took"  [***7] her to the ground, stabbed her, and later dumped the body.

When asked by his attorney at trial why he had told Hartman so many different stories, defendant stated: "He [Hartman] wouldn't settle for nothin' else. I would tell him one story and be back the next day, he would come back for another story, and the more I told the more deeper I got into it, you know, and finally he told me one time, he said, he said 'either you did it,' or he says, 'or you know who did it.' I don't know if those were his exact words but it was something to that effect."

In a letter written to his girlfriend, LuAnn Smith, defendant stated: "* * * [T]here are some things that I told him [Paul Hartman] that only the persons who did this shit know, there are no if and ands about that * * *."

In a letter defendant sent to Leon Connors, a former cellmate of the defendant, he wrote: "[I]t seems like everything you said to me has come to pass, but [I] am still going to take it to trial[.] * * * [I] don't know how [I] will react when they set me in the chair."

On September 13, 1983, defendant was indicted by the Van Wert County Grand Jury for the aggravated murder of Mottinger. Count I of the indictment charged [***8] that defendant committed kidnapping in violation of R.C. 2905.01(A)(2). Count II charged aggravated murder in the purposeful killing of Mottinger with prior calculation and design in violation of " R.C. 2903.01(B) [sic]." Two death penalty specifications were included alleging that the murder was committed for the purpose of escaping detection, apprehension, trial and punishment for the kidnapping, as set forth in R.C. 2929.04(A)(3); and that prior to the offense at bar, the offender had been convicted of an offense, an essential element of which was the purposeful killing of or attempt to kill another, as set forth in R.C. 2929.04(A)(5).

On September 27, 1983, the trial court appointed the Ohio Public Defender Commission to represent defendant, who entered his plea of not guilty to both counts.

The state complied with defendant's request for a bill of particulars, stating as to Count II that the defendant committed the murder "for the purpose of eliminating * * * [the victim] as a witness to her kidnapping," which essentially alleged the R.C. 2929.04(A)(8) death penalty specification.

On February 22, 1984, defendant filed a renewed motion to dismiss the indictment on the basis [***9] that it could not be determined which specification had actually been alleged in the indictment and bill of particulars. Subsequently, the trial court allowed the state to orally amend its indictment to charge a violation of R.C. 2903.01(A), and denied defendant's motion to dismiss.

Jury selection began on July 30, 1984 and a jury trial was commenced on August 6, 1984. Numerous witnesses were called to testify, including Connors. Connors testified that defendant had admitted to him that Gibson and another unidentified person from Florida were with defendant when he killed Mottinger. On redirect examination of Connors, the following colloquy took place:

"Q Did John Spirko tell you that he killed the postmistress or didn't he?

"A Yes, he said, he said, he said, 'I killed her.' He said, 'I killed her.' He said, he made a statement point blank, 'I killed her.'"

Andre Ruffin, another former cellmate of the defendant, testified that defendant told him that he and a friend robbed the post office, and that they took the postmaster with them because she could identify them. Ruffin stated that defendant told him that they took the victim to a friend's house "* * * and made her perform  [***10] all types of acts. And she bit one of their penuses [sic]." Ruffin further testified that defendant told him "[t]hey put her in the back seat and drove her and spotted an abandoned house. They took her up in the abandoned house, and he said they went . . . just like goosh her. He waved like a gash, then he said it was a knife, and that was done in the abandoned house."

In response to several questions, Ruffin testified as follows:

"Q Did he [defendant] indicate whether or not he was involved in the stabbing?

"A Yes, he indicated that him and his friend was involved in the stabbing.

"Q Did he indicate anything to you about how he felt about the stabbing of the woman?

"A Yes, he did, he said it isn't nothing to kill anyone. He said it's nothing to kill a person."

On August 22, 1984, the jury returned a verdict of guilty on both charges and on the R.C. 2929.04(A)(3) death specification. The trial court entered judgment upon the verdict.

At defendant's option under R.C. 2929.022, his guilt of the R.C. 2929.04(A)(5) death specification was tried to the court in the jury's absence. The court consequently ruled that defendant was guilty of the R.C. 2929.04(A)(5) death specification [***11] beyond a reasonable doubt. Thereafter, the court informed the jury of its finding of guilt.

During the mitigation phase, the defendant acknowledged that he refused to allow his attorneys to call any witnesses to testify on his behalf. Defendant offered his own statement which essentially was that while he was not guilty of killing Mottinger, he wanted to be sentenced to death. The court subsequently admitted into evidence a psychiatrist's report which contained a psychiatric evaluation of defendant.

Subsequently, the jury found that the aggravating circumstances out-weighed the mitigating factors beyond a reasonable doubt, and recommended that defendant be sentenced to death. Thereafter, the court ordered that defendant be sentenced to a term of imprisonment on the first count, and to death on the second count.

Upon appeal, the court of appeals affirmed the trial court's judgments of conviction and sentence.

The cause is now before this court upon an appeal as of right.
State v. Spirko, 1989 Ohio App. LEXIS 731 (Ohio Ct. App., Van Wert County, Mar. 6, 1989)



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the decision of the Court of Appeals for Van Wert County (Ohio) which affirmed his conviction for aggravated murder with a recommendation of death.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of aggravated murder and sentenced to die. Appellant argued that his sentence had to be vacated because he experienced numerous instances of prosecutorial misconduct that induced the jury to convict and sentence him to death according to factors that were irrelevant and highly prejudicial. Upon a careful review of the record, the court found that most of the examples cited by appellant occurred when the prosecutor was commenting on evidence that had already been admitted without objection. Therefore, appellant failed to cite even one example of error under this proposition of law that deprived him of a fair trial. Appellant also argued that the trial court erred by denying his motion for a new trial. Appellant's motion was not filed within 14 days of the verdict as required by Ohio R. Crim. P. 33. This deadline did not apply to newly discovered evidence. Even assuming that appellant's argument was based on newly discovered evidence, the court found his argument devoid of merit. The court commented that given the circumstances, the imposition of death was proper in this case. The court affirmed the death sentence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the court of appeals which affirmed the sentencing of appellant to death. The court held that appellant was not a victim of any type of prosecutorial misconduct and that under the circumstances, the imposition of death was proper.

State ex rel. Celebrezze v. Hughes


No. 89-2219


Supreme Court of Ohio


58 Ohio St. 3d 273; 569 N.E.2d 1059; 1991 Ohio LEXIS 918


December 18, 1990, Submitted  
April 10, 1991, Decided


NOTICE:

 [***1] .


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Greene County, No. 88-CA-62.

On September 16, 1987, appellant, the Ohio Attorney General, filed an amended civil complaint in the Court of Common Pleas of Greene County against Kathleen L. Hughes, individually and d.b.a. Fairborn Sales and Lease, and Terrance A. Hughes, alleging violations of R.C. 1345.01 et seq. (the Ohio Consumer Sales Practices Act), R.C. 4549.41 et seq. (the Ohio Odometer Rollback and Disclosure Act), and Subchapter IV of the Motor Vehicle Information and Cost Savings Act (Section 1981 et seq., Title 15, U.S. Code). The appellant amended his complaint again on December 21, 1987, by adding Todd W. Klein, individually and d.b.a. Regency Motors, and appellee Jerry W. Klein, as defendants to the action. The second amended complaint included a new count under Section 1986, Title 15, U.S. Code, alleging that Terrance Hughes and the Kleins had conspired to violate Sections 1984 and 1988 of the Motor Vehicle Information and Cost Savings Act.

The gravamen of appellant's claim was that defendant Todd W. Klein,  [***2] as owner and agent of Regency Motors, would purchase used automobiles and roll back, alter or otherwise tamper with the automobiles' odometers. The complaint further alleged that defendants would transfer these automobiles to consumers without disclosing the automobiles' actual mileage. In addition, appellant alleges that defendants bribed an investigator with the Ohio Bureau of Motor Vehicles so that these fraudulent activities could continue undetected.

On August 28, 1987, appellee Jerry Klein pled guilty to federal violations of Sections 1341 and 2, Title 18, U.S. Code, and Section 371, Title 18, U.S. Code, relative to an odometer rollback conspiracy he operated with a Kentucky automobile dealership. Pursuant to the terms of the plea agreement, appellee agreed to fully and truthfully cooperate with a federal investigation of the conspiracy. In exchange, the appellee was granted "use" immunity for his testimony and evidence derived therefrom.

Appellant's aforementioned amended complaint sought to recover civil penalties from defendants pursuant to both federal and Ohio law. On September 23, 1988, the Court of Common Pleas of Greene County granted appellant's motion for summary [***3] judgment against all defendants. The trial court held appellee, Terrance A. Hughes and Todd W. Klein, individually and d.b.a. Regency Motors, jointly and severally liable in the amount of $ 25,000 for violations of the Ohio Consumer Sales and Practices Act, $ 1,000 per vehicle ($ 67,000 total) for violations of the Ohio Odometer Rollback and Disclosure Act, and $ 1,500 per vehicle ($ 105,000 total) as restitution to each consumer who purchased a vehicle listed in the complaint, with certain exceptions.

On appeal by appellee, the court of appeals found that the civil penalties authorized by R.C. 1345.07(D) and 4549.48(B) were punitive in nature and reversed that portion of the trial court's judgment which imposed these civil penalties. The court, however, affirmed that portion of the claim which sought restitution for the victims of the odometer rollback scheme.

This cause is now before the court pursuant to the allowance of a motion to certify the record.



DISPOSITION: Judgment accordingly.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state attorney general challenged the judgment of the Court of Appeals for Greene County (Ohio), which reversed the trial court's summary judgment, and the imposing of civil penalties under Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 1345.07(D) and Ohio Rev. Code § 4549.48(B), against appellee car dealer, finding that the same were punitive in nature.


OVERVIEW: Appellant state attorney general brought actions against appellant car dealer, alleging violations of the Ohio Consumer Sales Practices Act, Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 1345.01 et seq., the Ohio Odometer Rollback and Disclosure Act, Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4549.41 et seq., and Subchapter IV of the Motor Vehicle Information and Cost Savings Act, 15 U.S.C.S. § 1981 et seq., and 15 U.S.C.S. § 1986. Appellant alleged that appellee rolled back odometers and sold vehicles without disclosing that the mileage was not accurate. The trial court imposed civil penalties for such violations. The appellate court reversed, finding the imposition of the penalties to have been punitive. On further appeal, the court reversed and remanded the matter, holding that the statutory language in the case was clear and unambiguous; the general assembly intended in Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 1345.07(D) and Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4549.48(B) to impose civil penalties and to allow penalties to be imposed without regard to the procedural protections and restrictions available in criminal prosecutions. The court held that for those reasons, the penalties by their labeling and by serving remedial purposes were civil in nature.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court, which had found that the imposition of civil penalties was punitive, because the penalties authorized by the Ohio Consumer Sales Practices Act and the Ohio Odometer Rollback and Disclosure Act by their labeling and by serving remedial purposes were civil in nature. The matter was remanded to the court of common pleas.

State v. Casalicchio


No. 89-1865


Supreme Court of Ohio


58 Ohio St. 3d 178; 569 N.E.2d 916; 1991 Ohio LEXIS 905


November 20, 1990, Submitted  
April 3, 1991, Decided


NOTICE:

 [***1] .


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied May 15, 1991. As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL and CROSS-APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Cuyahoga County, No. 55655.

On the evening of August 25, 1987, Joseph Casalicchio was driving his 1974 Chevrolet Corvette north on Interstate 77 outside Cleveland. He was travelling at a high rate of speed when a patrolman for the village of Cuyahoga Heights stopped him. The police officer arrested Casalicchio for reckless operation and took him into custody. The patrolman then performed a routine inventory search of Casalicchio's automobile. The inventory revealed what appeared to be seven marijuana cigarettes, a quantity of hashish, a quantity of cocaine, and a plastic tube used for inhaling cocaine.

On October 22, 1987, Casalicchio pleaded no contest to two felony offenses, possession of a controlled substance, cocaine, in violation of R.C. 2925.11, and possession of a criminal tool, the inhaler, in violation of R.C. 2923.24. Casalicchio's sentence was announced in court on November 20, 1987. Three days later, on November 23, 1987, the state petitioned the trial court for [***2] forfeiture of Casalicchio's automobile pursuant to R.C. 2933.43. A hearing on the petition for forfeiture was held on January 21, 1988, and an order of forfeiture was handed down on April 7, 1988.

Casalicchio appealed the order of forfeiture to the Court of Appeals for Cuyahoga County. The appellate court reversed the order of forfeiture, holding that because the defendant's automobile was not used in the commission of the offenses for which he was convicted, it was not subject to forfeiture.

The state now appeals, asserting that the forfeiture statutes reach Casalicchio's automobile. Casalicchio cross-appeals, challenging not only the reach of the forfeiture statutes but also the procedure by which the state applied them to him.

The cause is now before this court upon the allowance of a motion for leave to appeal.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pleaded no contest to two felony drug offenses. The trial court granted the petition the State filed under Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2933.43 for the forfeiture of defendant's automobile. The Court of Appeals for Cuyahoga County (Ohio) reversed the order, holding that the automobile was not subject to forfeiture because it was not used in the commission of the underlying offenses. The State appealed, and defendant cross-appealed.


OVERVIEW: The underlying felonies were possession of a controlled substance, cocaine, and possession of a criminal tool, an inhaler. Defendant questioned whether his automobile was constitutionally subject to forfeiture when it bore no substantial relationship to the underlying crimes for which he was convicted. Defendant also argued that Ohio Rev. Code §§ 2933.42 and 2933.43 were void for vagueness under the Fourteenth Amendment. The court held that (1) the automobile was contraband pursuant to § 2933.42 because the cocaine and the cocaine inhaler constituted contraband involving felonies and because they were possessed and transported in the automobile; (2) the statutes were not void for vagueness; (3) both statutes were constitutional in their clarity and scope; and (4) forfeiture of property pursuant to § 2933.43 constituted a separate criminal penalty in addition to the penalty defendant faced for conviction of the underlying felony. Thus, the court held that the forfeiture in the instant case violated the double jeopardy provisions of the federal and state constitutions because the State was precluded from seeking a new criminal penalty after defendant had been sentenced.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed on other grounds.

State ex rel. Zupancic v. Limbach


No. 89-734


Supreme Court of Ohio


58 Ohio St. 3d 130; 568 N.E.2d 1206; 1991 Ohio LEXIS 703


November 13, 1990, Submitted  
March 27, 1991, Decided


NOTICE:

 [***1] .


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: IN MANDAMUS.

Relators, Edward Zupancic as the Lake County Auditor, Robert Gardner, Mildred Teuscher and John Platz as the Lake County Board of Commissioners, Steven C. LaTourette as the Lake County Prosecuting Attorney, and the Perry Township Board of Trustees have brought the instant action in mandamus in order to require respondent, Joanne Limbach, as Tax Commissioner of Ohio, to apportion public utility property values to taxing districts according to the apportionment formula that existed prior to Am. Sub. S.B. No. 449 (effective November 28, 1988), i.e., former R.C. 5727.15(D). Specifically, relators claim that Am. Sub. S.B. No. 449, to the extent that it enacted R.C. 5727.15(D), now (C), is unconstitutional in that the statute is of a general nature, but it does not have uniform operation as required under the Ohio Constitution. Furthermore, relators allege that the statute is arbitrary and unreasonable and thus violates the Equal Protection Clause of the Ohio Constitution. Respondent asserts that a mandamus action is inappropriate under the facts of [***2] this case and, even assuming arguendo that the requested relief is deemed appropriate, the current apportionment formula is constitutional.

Respondent is responsible for certifying to county auditors her assessment of taxable public utility property apportioned to each taxing district within their respective counties on or before the first Monday in October, pursuant to R.C. 5727.23. The Perry Nuclear Power Plant is an electric power plant with an initial cost of plant production equipment exceeding $ 1 billion, and all of its potentially taxable production equipment subject to the aforementioned apportionment law is located within Lake County, Ohio. Accordingly, respondent is obligated to certify the value of the Perry Nuclear Power Plant and apportion the taxable value of the property among various taxing districts. See R.C. 5727.23 and 5727.15.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relators, county administrators, filed a petition for writ of mandamus in the Supreme Court of Ohio to order respondent tax commissioner to apportion public utility property values to taxing districts according to the apportionment formula that existed in former Ohio Rev. Code § 5727.15 (D). The administrators claimed that § 5727.15 (c) was unconstitutional because it did not have uniform operation as required under the Ohio Constitution.


OVERVIEW: The court found that mandamus was proper because other remedies at law, such as declaratory judgment were not adequate. The administrators contended that the distributive features of the statute were not uniform, and that the statute lacked a rational basis or legitimate state interest. The court disagreed. Although the statute may have applied to one particular electric power plant with an initial cost exceeding $ 1 billion, there was nothing within the Act itself to prevent its prospective operation upon any electric power plant similarly situated throughout the state. Furthermore, the General Assembly's classification of electric power plants with initial production equipment costs exceeding $ 1 billion was reasonable in light of the enormous cost of building a modern generating facility. The increased revenue that would befall a situs district, absent the statute, supported the legislative attempt to distribute the revenue windfall among other taxing districts in which there were transmission and distribution lines for the power generated by the production equipment.


OUTCOME: The court denied the administrators' petition for a writ of mandamus.

 

246 of 2888 DOCUMENTS


State v. Durr


No. 90-291


Supreme Court of Ohio


58 Ohio St. 3d 86; 568 N.E.2d 674; 1991 Ohio LEXIS 616


November 20, 1990, Submitted  
March 20, 1991, Decided


NOTICE:

 [***1] .


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied May 1, 1991. As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Cuyahoga County, No. 57140.

On January 31, 1988, at approximately 10:50 p.m., Norma Jean O'Nan and her husband returned to their home in Elyria and discovered the front door unlocked, the lights and television on, and their sixteen-year-old daughter, Angel Vincent, missing. Only twenty minutes earlier, Mrs. O'Nan had spoken with her daughter by telephone to learn that Angel's girlfriend, Deborah Mullins, was at her home and that Deborah's boyfriend, appellant Darryl Durr, was expected to arrive later in the evening. That was the last chance Mrs. O'Nan would have to speak to her daughter alive.

Mrs. O'Nan testified that Angel was wearing a hot pink sweater, a light pink and white checkered blouse, hot pink pants, and white tennis shoes when she and her husband left Angel home alone on the evening of January 31, 1988. After notifying the Elyria Police of Angel's disappearance, Mrs. O'Nan searched her home to determine if any of Angel's belongings were missing. Although Angel's pink pants were found,  [***2] Mrs. O'Nan's search revealed the following items missing: an old lavender blanket with a hole in the center, a pair of black acid-washed denim jeans, Angel's pink and white checkered blouse, light blue eyeglasses that Angel wore only in her home, a jean jacket that Angel had borrowed from a friend, an Avon necklace with an "A" charm attached, a small chain bracelet, an Avon slip-on bracelet, an inexpensive rhinestone ring and a dog chain that hung from her mirror. Mrs. O'Nan also discovered Angel's handbag stuffed under her bed.

Three or four days later, Mrs. O'Nan confronted Deborah Mullins and the appellant regarding the disappearance of her daughter, and was told by the appellant that "you know how kids are, she probably ran away."

On April 30, 1988, three boys noticed a foul odor coming from two orange traffic barrels while playing in Brookside Park. The barrels had been placed open end to open end, and were underneath a railroad tie. Upon separating the barrels, the boys discovered a severely decomposed female body that had been wrapped in a dirty old blanket. A portion of a leg was visible through a large hole in the blanket.

A deputy coroner testified that the only clothing [***3] found on the victim was a pink sweater and a pair of white tennis shoes. The pink sweater had been pushed up well above the victim's breast area. An initial external examination determined the body to be that of a young white female, who was in an advanced state of decomposition. The body was heavily infested with maggots and the body's eyes and ears had been lost. There was also prominent evidence of animal activity about the inguinal and vulval regions of the body, and in and about the thighs. According to the deputy coroner, the decomposition was consistent with three months' exposure.

After examining the body, the deputy coroner concluded that the cause of death was homicidal violence. Since the body was so badly decomposed, the deputy coroner could not determine whether ligature marks, scrapes or tears indicating strangulation were present. There was no damage noted to the internal cartilaginous structures of the neck. The deputy coroner declined, however, to rule out strangulation as a cause of death since damage to these structures is not always present in young strangulation victims due to the flexibility of these structures. In addition, because the body was so severely [***4] infested with bacteria, testing for the presence of acid phosphates and spermatozoa was inconclusive.

In September 1988, after appellant was arrested for two unrelated rapes, Deborah Mullins revealed her knowledge of Angel's disappearance to the Cleveland Police Department. As the result of her information, an ankle X-ray obtained from Elyria Memorial Hospital, and dental records, the body discovered in Brookside Park was determined to be that of Angel Vincent.

At trial, Deborah Mullins testified that on the evening Angel disappeared Deborah had asked the appellant to drive to the house of one of Angel's friends to retrieve a package of cigarettes for Angel. Appellant agreed and left. Shortly thereafter, appellant returned to Deborah's house and, instead of entering through the front door, began throwing stones at her upstairs bedroom window and blew his car horn for her to come out. Deborah and her baby, who had been fathered by the appellant, left the house and entered the appellant's car where the appellant brandished a knife toward both of them.

As the appellant was driving, Deborah heard noises from the back seat and after turning around discovered Angel bound on the rear [***5] floorboard.

According to Deborah's testimony, Angel was wearing black acid-washed denim jeans, a jean jacket, and tennis shoes when she was last seen in the back of appellant's car.

When Deborah asked the appellant why Angel was bound in his car, the appellant responded that he intended to "waste" her because "she would tell." He never revealed just what Angel was going to tell.

After threatening the life of both Deborah and his baby, the appellant let Deborah out of his car. He returned to her home three or four hours later. Upon returning, appellant told Deborah that he had "wasted" Angel and that she should pack her things because they were leaving.

Appellant drove Deborah and their baby to his wife's, Janice Durr's, Cleveland apartment. After dropping Deborah and the baby off, the appellant left with a duffle bag containing two shovels.

When appellant returned, he was wet and covered with snow. Upon entering the room, appellant placed a ring and bracelet that belonged to Angel on a coffee table. As he was falling asleep, appellant told Deborah that he had strangled Angel with a dog chain until she "pissed, pooped and shit and made a few gurgling sounds," took her body to [***6] a park, wrapped it in a blanket, placed it between two construction cones, and left her by some railroad tracks.

Later that day or the next day, appellant burned a bag of clothing in the basement of Janice Durr's apartment building and asked Deborah to model the black acid-washed jeans that Angel had worn on the evening of her abduction.

The appellant then drove Deborah, Janice Durr and his children to the west side of Cleveland where he burned another bag of items, and while driving from Cleveland toward Elyria, the appellant threw Angel's jean jacket out the car window.

After arriving at Deborah's home in Elyria, Deborah's mother informed her that Mrs. O'Nan had come over and inquired about Deborah's knowledge of Angel's disappearance. Deborah testified that appellant threatened her and their baby's life and instructed her to tell Mrs. O'Nan that Angel had been talking about running away. Deborah also testified that the appellant took her and their baby to Edgewater Park where the appellant threw Angel's glasses over a cliff into the lake. A month or so later, while driving past the Cleveland Zoo, appellant pointed to a location near a bridge and said, "Over there." When Deborah [***7] questioned his statement, the appellant replied, "You know what I am talking about."

Following a jury trial appellant was convicted of one count of aggravated murder, R.C. 2903.01, with specifications, pursuant to R.C. 2929.04(A)(7), that the murder was committed while the appellant was committing, attempting to commit, or fleeing after committing aggravated robbery, rape, and kidnapping, (2) kidnapping, R.C. 2905.01, with a violence specification, (3) aggravated robbery, R.C. 2911.01, with a violence specification, and (4) rape, R.C. 2907.02, with a violence specification.

The cause is now before this court upon an appeal as of right.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Court of Appeals for Cuyahoga County (Ohio), which convicted defendant of aggravated murder, with specifications, that the murder was committed while defendant was committing, attempting to commit, or fleeing after committing aggravated robbery, rape, and kidnapping, all with a violence specification, and sentenced to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of aggravated murder, kidnapping, robbery, and rape of the victim. Defendant was sentenced to death. He appealed, alleging numerous errors, including that the trial court unreasonably restricted his questioning during voir dire, admitted gruesome photographs of the victim that were prejudicial because they were cumulative and repetitive, and defendant challenged his sentence of death. On appeal, the court affirmed and ruled that a review of the entire voir dire revealed that the trial court limited defense counsel's statements to the jury in an attempt to prohibit counsel from lecturing or making legal arguments to them that were overly broad and outside the scope of voir dire. Although the photographs that were admitted at trial were shocking, their probative value outweighed the danger of unfair prejudice. The court concluded that the death sentence was appropriate because the aggravating circumstances that defendant murdered the victim after first kidnapping, robbing and raping her were proved beyond a reasonable doubt and outweighed the mitigating factors consisting of testimony of two witnesses and defendant' unsworn statement.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence of death.

 

247 of 2888 DOCUMENTS


State v. Suchomski


No. 89-2131


Supreme Court of Ohio


58 Ohio St. 3d 74; 567 N.E.2d 1304; 1991 Ohio LEXIS 612


December 12, 1990, Submitted  
March 13, 1991, Decided


NOTICE:

 [***1] .


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied April 24, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Lake County, No. 89-L-14-052.

On June 20, 1988, Thomas C. Suchomski, appellee, was indicted for one count of domestic violence, a violation of R.C. 2919.25. The indictment charged appellee with knowingly causing or attempting to cause physical harm, a fourth degree felony, since appellee had previously been convicted of a domestic violence offense, to Matthew Suchomski, appellee's son.

On August 9, 1988, appellee filed a motion to dismiss the indictment, claiming that R.C. 2919.25 is unconstitutionally vague and overbroad. Appellee contended that charging him under this statute could bring about the punishment of a parent who uses corporal punishment to discipline a child. Appellee's motion was granted and the indictment was dismissed. On appeal, the court of appeals affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

The cause is now before this court pursuant to the allowance of a motion for leave to appeal.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant government sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeals for Lake County (Ohio), which granted appellee father's motion to dismiss the indictment charging him with one count of domestic violence pursuant to Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2919.25.


OVERVIEW: The indictment charged the father with knowingly causing or attempting to cause physical harm to his son. The father had previously been convicted of a domestic violence offense. The father claimed that the indictment was unconstitutionally vague and overbroad. He also contended that charging him under § 2919.25 could bring about the punishment of a parent who used corporal punishment to discipline a child. On appeal, the court found that the father's conduct as charged in the indictment clearly constituted a violation of § 2919.25 and that it apprised him of each and every element of the offense. The court held that the indictment was not defective. The court did not find a conflict between § 2919.25 and Ohio Rev. Code Ann. §2919.22. The court determined that nothing in Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2919.25 prevented a parent from properly disciplining his child but rather that it only prohibited physical harm or injury to the child. The court concluded that the only facts before it were those contained in the government's memorandum in opposition to the father's motion and that the facts contained therein were sufficient to meet all the elements of a charge for domestic violence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court dismissing the indictment charging the father for domestic violence and reinstated it.

In re Election of November 6, 1990 for Office of Attorney Gen.


No. 90-2544


Supreme Court of Ohio


58 Ohio St. 3d 103; 569 N.E.2d 447; 1991 Ohio LEXIS 613


February 20, 1991, Submitted  
March 11, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: ON PETITION TO CONTEST ELECTION.

On December 28, 1990, contestor, Paul E. Pfeifer, filed a petition to contest the general election of November 6, 1990 for the office of Attorney General of Ohio. He lost that election to contestee, Lee I. Fisher, by a margin of less than one-quarter of one percent -- 1,679,464 votes to 1,680,698 votes. A mandatory recount of ballots took place as required by R.C. 3515.011.

Contestor's petition alleges six grounds for relief:

(1) That automatic tabulating equipment, specifically, optical scanning machines, used in Mahoning County, was in such disrepair that different machines counted the same ballots differently;

(2) That during the recount in Mahoning County and elsewhere, stacks of ballots were moved from one optical scanning machine to another until a count was obtained that agreed with earlier tabulations; and that in Summit County the automatic tabulating machine results produced more votes than there were names of voters recorded in the poll books;

(3) That in Mahoning County certain "overvote" ballots -- ballots on which  [***2] both ovals had been darkened, indicating a vote for both candidates, and an "X" had been placed over one of the ovals -- were counted differently in the recount for a common pleas judgeship race than they were in the race for Attorney General; that the "X's" appeared to have been made by the same hand; and that all but one of the forty to fifty overvotes were counted for contestee;

(4) That at the recount in Mahoning County, "remake" ballots -- ballots marked by election officials from ballots that had been "torn and tattered" and could not be counted by machine -- considerably outnumbered the torn and tattered ballots from which they purportedly derived; that attempts at reconciliation were unsuccessful; that during the recount the deputy director of the Mahoning County Board of Elections was observed in the room where the remake ballots were kept with a "short, stubby, 'sawed-off' pencil concealed within the palm of his hand"; that thereafter a number of additional remake ballots were found; and that it was then determined that the remake count was complete, even though there was never a proper reconciliation of the numbers of remake and torn and tattered ballots;

(5) That ballots [***3] were stored in improper containers at the Mahoning County Board of Elections in violation of R.C. 3505.31; that "H.B. 237" ballots -- ballots cast at the board of elections pursuant to the requirements of R.C. 3503.11, amended by Am. Sub. H.B. No. 237 of the 118th General Assembly, effective July 27, 1990 -- were not integrated with other ballots, despite a direct order of this court; that ballots were scattered about on the floor of the storage room; and that numerous ballots and entire precinct envelopes were determined to be missing during the recount; and

(6) That contestor's and contestee's names were not rotated in position on the ballots from precinct to precinct as required by Section 2a, Article V, Ohio Constitution, and R.C. 3505.03, but that contestee's name appeared first on four-sevenths of the ballots.

Contestor alleges that these "irregularities, willful misconduct, fraud, and electronic and mechanical inaccuracies" cost him the election. He requests the court to declare him the winner, or, if the precise effect of the irregularities, misconduct, and inaccuracies cannot be determined, to declare that no one was elected.

Contestee denied the material allegations of  [***4] the complaint. On January 8, 1991, we joined the Secretary of State as a party and ordered him to order boards of elections to preserve certain evidence.

On February 1, 1991, contestee filed a motion to dismiss the complaint in part at the close of contestor's evidence. We overruled the motion on February 15, 1991.

The parties have submitted evidence and briefs; oral argument has been held and the case is now before the court for a determination on the merits.



DISPOSITION: Judgment accordingly.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Before the court was contestor's petition to challenge the general election for the office of Attorney General of Ohio, which he lost to contestee by a slim margin.


OVERVIEW: A general election was held for the office of Attorney General of Ohio. Contestor lost the election to contestee, and challenged the election results alleging that irregularities, willful misconduct, fraud, and electronic and mechanical inaccuracies cost him the election. Contestor requested that the court declare him the winner, or, if the precise effect of the irregularities, misconduct, and inaccuracies could not have been determined, to declare that no one was elected. The court held that were the allegations of contestor's petition supported by the required weight of the evidence, they would have been required to set aside the election for Attorney General. However, the court's thorough examination of the evidence and arguments of the parties caused the court to conclude that contestor had not met his burden of proof to overturn the election. Therefore, the court exercised judgment for the contestee.


OUTCOME: The court declared contestee the winner of the general election for the office of Attorney General of Ohio.
