State ex rel. Tyler v. Alexander


No. 90-394


Supreme Court of Ohio


52 Ohio St. 3d 84; 555 N.E.2d 966; 1990 Ohio LEXIS 264


April 17, 1990, Submitted  
June 27, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Madison County, No. 89CA-10-020.

Appellee, George D. Alexander, is the Warden of the London Correctional Institution, where appellant, David B. Tyler, is incarcerated. Tyler claims that the Adult Parole Authority (which is not a party to this action) revoked his parole on unconstitutional grounds and, alternatively, that the authority abused its discretion by making Tyler wait three years before he next becomes eligible for parole.

Tyler filed a complaint in mandamus, containing these allegations, in the Court of Appeals for Madison County. The warden moved to dismiss the complaint, alleging, inter alia, that he was not a proper party to the action. Tyler then filed a motion to amend the complaint so as to substitute the Adult Parole Authority for the warden as respondent. The court of appeals denied Tyler's motion to amend and granted the warden's motion to dismiss on December 29, 1989.

Tyler, representing himself, filed a notice of appeal in the court of appeals. Tyler claims that he entrusted his notice of appeal to the prison mail room on January 27, 1990. However, the file stamp indicates that the notice was filed with the court [***2] of appeals on February 5, 1990. Tyler then filed his notice of appeal in this court on March 6, 1990.

The cause is now before us upon an appeal as of right.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate challenged a judgment of the Court of Appeals for Madison County (Ohio), which dismissed his action in mandamus. Appellee was a warden.


OVERVIEW: The inmate's parole was revoked, and he filed a complaint in mandamus against the warden. The appellate court dismissed the action on December 29, 1989. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court. The court found that the inmate claimed that he entrusted his notice of appeal to the prison mail room on January 27, 1990, but that the file stamp indicated that the notice was filed with the appellate court on February 5, 1990. The court declined to consider the notice of appeal filed when the inmate turned it over to the prison authorities for mailing. Finding that the notice of appeal was not timely filed, pursuant to Ohio Sup. Ct. Prac. R. 1, § 1(A), the court held that the action was properly dismissed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

Gambill v. Bonded Oil Co.


No. 89-703


Supreme Court of Ohio


52 Ohio St. 3d 90; 556 N.E.2d 177; 1990 Ohio LEXIS 262


April 4, 1990, Submitted  
June 27, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Champaign County, No. 88-CA-08.

Plaintiff-appellee, Marjorie E. Gambill, and her husband, Richard Gambill, instituted this action against defendant-appellant, Bonded Oil Company 1 ("Bonded"), on November 16, 1987 in the court of common pleas. In their complaint, plaintiffs alleged, inter alia, that Marjorie was intentionally injured by her employer, Bonded, on November 15, 1985. Richard Gambill also claimed damages for loss of consortium.


1 Culp Distributors, Inc. was also named as a party defendant in plaintiffs' complaint. The case against Culp apparently remains pending, since the trial court noted in its journal entry dated January 12, 1988, that service of process was not attempted on Culp.

Subsequently, Bonded filed a motion to dismiss the complaint on the grounds that the action was time barred by the new one-year limit of R.C. 4121.80(A), and that it was immune from liability on the husband's loss-of-consortium claim based on Bevis v. Armco Steel Corp. (1951), 156 Ohio St. 295, 46 O.O. 172, 102 N.E. 2d 444. The trial court treated the motion to dismiss as a motion for summary judgment, and granted summary judgment [***2] in favor of Bonded based on the statute of limitations set forth at R.C. 4121.80(A).

Upon appeal, the court of appeals reversed and remanded. The appellate court observed that the plaintiffs' cause of action arose prior to the effective date of R.C. 4121.80 (August 22, 1986), and that Hunter v. Shenango Furnace Co. (1988), 38 Ohio St. 3d 235, 537 N.E. 2d 871 was applicable to the instant action. Therefore, the court of appeals held that since plaintiffs' complaint did not clearly allege a battery or other specific intentional tort, the complaint was timely filed pursuant to the statute of limitations set forth at R.C. 2305.10.

In addition, the appellate court found that Bevis, supra, was not dispositive of the plaintiff-husband's loss-of-consortium claim. The court of appeals opined that more recent cases have indicated that spouses may recover for loss of services as part of an intentional tort action against an employer. See Blankenship v. Cincinnati Milacron Chemicals, Inc. (1982), 69 Ohio St. 2d 608, at 614-615, 23 O.O. 3d 504, at 508-509, 433 N.E. 2d 572, at 577, and Jones v. VIP Development Co. (1984), 15 Ohio St. 3d 90, at 99, 15 OBR 246, at  [***3] 254, 472 N.E. 2d 1046, at 1055.

The cause is now before this court pursuant to the allowance of a motion to certify the record. 2


2 The motion was denied with regard to the issue concerning the plaintiff-husband's loss-of-consortium claim.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action an intentional tort causing plaintiff employee's injury and for loss of consortium claimed by plaintiff husband, defendant employer appealed the judgment of the Court of Appeals for Champaign County (Ohio), which reversed the decision of the trial court that granted summary judgment in favor of the employer. The cause was before the court pursuant to the allowance of the employer's motion to certify the record.


OVERVIEW: The employee and her husband instituted an action against the employer, alleging that the employee was intentionally injured by the employer. The husband also claimed damages for loss of consortium. The employer filed a motion to dismiss the complaint on the grounds that the action was time barred by the new one-year limit of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4121.80(A) and that it was immune from liability on the husband's loss-of-consortium claim. The trial court treated the motion to dismiss as a motion for summary judgment and granted summary judgment in favor of the employer. The court of appeals reversed and remanded. The cause came before court pursuant to the allowance of the employer's motion to certify the record. The court affirmed because the circumstances did not clearly indicate a battery or other intentional tort specifically enumerated in the Revised Code, and the cause of action arose prior to the effective date of § 4121.80; therefore, the action was governed by the two-year statute of limitations codified in Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2305.10.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals.

State ex rel. Hensley v. Nowak


No. 89-186


Supreme Court of Ohio


52 Ohio St. 3d 98; 556 N.E.2d 171; 1990 Ohio LEXIS 266


April 17, 1990, Submitted  
June 27, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] IN MANDAMUS and/or PROHIBITION.

Relators are the holders of liquor permits issued by the Ohio Department of Liquor Control. Respondents are the department, its director, Karen Nowak, and the Ohio Liquor Control Commission. The complaint alleges that agents of the department have conducted warrantless administrative searches of relators' permit premises, pursuant to a regulation that was adopted by unconstitutional procedures and that is insufficient to justify such searches under State v. VFW Post 3562 (1988), 37 Ohio St. 3d 310, 525 N.E. 2d 773. 1 Relators allege that they will be subjected to more warrantless searches by agents of the department, and that the commission is about to "impose forfeitures or suspend or revoke the liquor permits of permit holders" based on evidence seized during such warrantless searches.


1 In VFW Post 3562, we held that warrantless administrative searches of liquor permit premises were unconstitutional unless authorized by a statute or regulation limiting the time, place and scope of the search.

Relators request that a writ of mandamus issue directing respondents to "comply with this Court's holding in State v. VFW Post  [***2] 3562," which presumably means to desist from conducting warrantless administrative searches until a statute, conforming to the requirements set forth in that case, is enacted. In the alternative, relators ask for a writ of prohibition to prevent respondents from conducting such searches until such a statute is enacted.



DISPOSITION: Writs denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator liquor permit holders requested that a writ of mandamus issue directing respondents, Ohio Department of Liquor Control, its director, and Ohio Liquor Control Commission, to desist from conducting warrantless administrative searches until a statute regarding such searches was enacted. In the alternative, the permit holders asked for a writ of prohibition to prevent respondents from conducting such searches until such a statute was enacted.


OVERVIEW: The permit holders alleged that Department agents conducted warrantless administrative searches of their premises, pursuant to a regulation that was adopted by unconstitutional procedures. They further alleged that Department agents would have subjected them to more warrantless searches and that the Commission intended to impose forfeitures or suspend or revoke their permits based on evidence seized during warrantless searches. Because the requested relief would have restricted, rather than compelled, respondents, the cause of action sounded in injunction rather than mandamus. The court had no jurisdiction to grant injunctive relief. The court held that a writ of prohibition could not have been issued to prohibit the allegedly forthcoming searches and seizures regardless of their legality unless they constituted the exercise of quasi-judicial power. Quasi-judicial power was the power to hear and to determine controversies between the public and individuals that required a hearing resembling a judicial trial. An administrative officer who searched liquor permit premises was not exercising quasi-judicial power. Therefore, prohibition was not available to forbid searches and seizures.


OUTCOME: The court denied the permit holders' requests for writs of prohibition and mandamus.

Jones v. Franklin County Sheriff


No. 89-125


Supreme Court of Ohio


52 Ohio St. 3d 40; 555 N.E.2d 940; 1990 Ohio LEXIS 256


January 24, 1990, Submitted  
June 20, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Franklin County, No. 88AP-533.

On January 9, 1986, Diane Jones, a Deputy Sheriff 1 in the Franklin County Sheriff's Department, involved herself in the pursuit of a woman whom Jones's sister suspected of stealing her purse. Jones participated in a series of events that included a car chase and the search of a private residence, without any apparent authorization. At no time during the pursuit and search did Jones report the incident to the police or otherwise try to dissuade the other participants from continuing their efforts to retrieve the purse on their own.

The events began when Jones's sister, Valerie Matfield, was visited at home by Tracy Smith, also known as Tracy Gresham. While Matfield was in another room, Smith stole Matfield's purse and left the apartment. Upon discovering her purse was missing, Matfield phoned the Columbus Police Department and reported the theft.

When Matfield was told that Smith was in a nearby car, she got into a car driven by an acquaintance, Warren Callaway, and gave chase. Meanwhile, Matfield's neighbor, Lilly White, informed Deputy Jones of the theft of the purse as Jones arrived, off duty and out  [***2] of uniform, at her sister's home. Jones and White joined the chase in a car driven by White.

When stopped at an intersection, Matfield left her friend's car and walked up to the car occupied by Smith. Matfield grabbed Smith's arm and helped her from the back seat of the car. Meanwhile, White and Jones arrived at the intersection. Smith confessed to the theft and agreed to take Matfield to where the purse was hidden. This information was gained through Matfield's interrogation of Smith at the intersection which, according to Smith, was intimidating. Jones reached her sister and Smith after Smith was out of the car. From the nature of this scene, Jones must have known that Matfield was taking the law into her own hands. Jones still did nothing to discourage Matfield's actions. Again, she continued to participate, following her sister and Smith to the home of Vivian Brihm to recover the purse.

Matfield, White, Callaway, Jones, and Smith arrived at Brihm's house in the early evening. Brihm and her children later testified that after the five arrived, Brihm objected when Matfield, White, and Smith went upstairs for the purse. According to Brihm, they persisted in searching upstairs [***3] despite Brihm's protests and pulled clothes out of closets. Jones remained downstairs during this improper search of the Brihm house, once again doing nothing to stop it. Instead, Jones facilitated the search by virtue of her entry into the house. During the search, Smith left the house through an upstairs window.

Jones, Matfield, and White then left to search for Smith. They later were told that Smith was at 808 East Fulton Street, Smith's residence. The information came from the same person who had earlier correctly identified Smith's location in the automobile. After unsuccessfully trying to gain entry into the Fulton Street house, Matfield finally called the Columbus Police Department. Officer Richard Ey responded to the call, obtained entry into the home, found Smith, recovered Matfield's purse, and arrested Smith.

This incident resulted in two separate investigations into Jones's conduct. One was a criminal investigation regarding allegations of kidnapping and assault. The other was a Franklin County Sheriff's Department Internal Affairs Division ("IAD") investigation which reviewed the actions of Deputy Jones to determine whether she had violated any sheriff's department [***4] regulations. On January 31, 1986 Sergeants Michael Flynn and John Hammond of the IAD attempted to interview Deputy Jones about the incident. Through her counsel, Jones informed the two sheriff's sergeants that she would not answer questions put to her during the IAD sessions. The sergeants then advised Jones that no evidence or information gathered in the IAD proceeding would be used against her in any subsequent criminal proceeding. The sergeants further advised Jones regarding the relevant provisions of the department's collective bargaining agreement that required answering such questions, gave her a direct order on the record to answer specific questions regarding the January 9, 1986 events, and warned her that refusal to answer would constitute insubordination.

Based upon the sergeant's findings, the sheriff's department held a predisciplinary hearing on February 11, 1986. In accordance with R.C. 124.34, a removal order filed February 12, 1986 notified Jones that she was "* * * Removed from the position of Deputy Sheriff 1" for "* * * [discourteous] treatment [of] the public, neglect of duty, failure of good behavior, misfeasance, malfeasance." Jones had been disciplined [***5] previously on six separate occasions. She had received four reprimands and two suspensions for, among other things, failing to comply with a superior's direct order. 1


1 Jones was suspended in August 1982 for failure to comply with a direct order of her supervisor and received a written reprimand in December 1982, a written reprimand September 1984, reprimands for tardiness in March and May 1985, and a three-day suspension in July 1985.

Jones appealed her removal to the State Personnel Board of Review (SPBR). A hearing convened on December 8, 1986 before Administrative Law Judge Richard J. Levine (the "ALJ"), who took two days of testimony.

In his January 21, 1987 report and recommendation, the ALJ found that Jones had been actively involved in what the ALJ described as vigilante activity and that her conduct reflected poorly on the department. He determined that her failure to call for police assistance evidenced so callous a disregard for procedure that she in essence took the law into her own hands. The ALJ further found that Jones had been insubordinate, and had violated Reg. 102.55, when she refused to answer questions under a direct order to respond after she had been [***6] informed that her answers could not be used in a subsequent criminal proceeding. The ALJ recommended affirmance of the order removing Jones for insubordination, neglect of duty, and conduct unbecoming an officer, specifically citing a violation of Reg. 102.40.1.

The SPBR issued its final order on March 27, 1986. The members of the board indicated that they had reviewed the ALJ's report and recommendation, and they accepted its factual conclusions. Nevertheless, the order stated that "* * * the unusual circumstances surrounding this incident somewhat negate the impropriety of * * * [Jones's] conduct." Further, the board found that Jones's refusal to answer IAD questions could not technically be considered a violation of department policy since the final step, actually asking questions after Jones refused to answer, was never taken. The board therefore ordered Jones's reinstatement coupled with a written reprimand.

The Franklin County Sheriff appealed to the Court of Common Pleas of Franklin County. Upon its review of the entire record, the court found the board's decision to be inconsistent with the evidence presented to the ALJ. The cour
Jones v. Franklin County Sheriff, 1988 Ohio App. LEXIS 4743 (Ohio Ct. App., Franklin County, Nov. 29, 1988)



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sheriff sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeals for Franklin County (Ohio) that reversed a ruling of a trial court and reinstated appellee deputy to her position in the sheriff's department. The sheriff dismissed the deputy and an administrative law judge recommended affirmance of the dismissal. The State Personnel Board of Review (SPBR) ordered reinstatement. The trial court reversed and affirmed the deputy's removal.


OVERVIEW: The court found that the deputy involved herself in the pursuit of a woman whom her sister suspected of having stolen her purse. The deputy participated in a car chase and the search of a private residence, without authorization and never reported the incident to police. During an investigation of the incident, officers of the Internal Affairs Division (IAD) attempted to interview the deputy but she refused to answer any questions even after she was informed that her answers could not be used against her in a criminal prosecution. She was then discharged for conduct unbecoming a deputy sheriff and for insubordination. On appeal, the court agreed with the trial court that the SPBR's decision to reinstate the deputy was inconsistent with the evidence, which indisputably revealed that the deputy's vigilante activities could not bring anything but disrepute upon the sheriff's department. Furthermore, the court held that because the deputy was assured that her constitutional guarantees remained intact, she was required to respond to specific questions dealing with job performance and that her refusal to do so constituted insubordination.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower appellate court and reinstated the ruling of the trial court that affirmed the removal of the deputy from the sheriff's department.

State v. Lott


No. 89-846


Supreme Court of Ohio


51 Ohio St. 3d 160; 555 N.E.2d 293; 1990 Ohio LEXIS 244


March 27, 1990, Submitted  
June 6, 1990, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied July 25, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Cuyahoga County, No. 54537.

In August 1983, a burglar broke into John McGrath's East Cleveland home three separate times. After each break-in McGrath, the murder victim, then seventy-nine years old, called the East Cleveland Police Department. On August 24, 1983, detectives placed a clean drinking glass upside down over a quarter on McGrath's dining room table. McGrath called the police in September after another break-in. As proven by fingerprints, a burglar had moved the glass and taken the quarter. Three years later, police identified the appellant, Gregory Lott, by his fingerprints as having burgled McGrath's home on September 7, 1983 and stolen that quarter.

On July 12, 1986, McGrath, driving his 1982 Ford Escort, stopped and cashed a check for $ 21 at a savings and loan. Patricia Hill, the head teller, described McGrath as chipper, and in fine spirits and good health that Saturday morning.

Two days later, around 9:15 a.m. on Monday morning, July 14, a Cleveland housewife, Diedrea Coleman, noticed that same Ford Escort parked in her neighborhood. The driver, a young male, remained sitting in the car [***2] almost two hours, though he moved it once. Her suspicions aroused, Coleman walked by the car, looked closely at the driver, noted the license number, and called the police twice that morning from a pay phone a block away. The car left around 11:20 a.m. and came back thirty minutes later. Coleman watched the driver walk over to the yard of her elderly neighbors, the Turks. Coleman later noticed the driver running from the Turks' house carrying a brown bag under his arm. Shortly afterwards, she and Mr. Turk found Mrs. Turk bruised and shaking with her blouse undone. Coleman, a trained artist, drew a sketch of the driver for the police. After the police showed her various photos several times, Coleman on July 28, 1986 identified Lott, from a photo array, as the suspicious Ford Escort driver.

City of Cleveland detectives traced the Escort's license number, discovered it belonged to McGrath, and stopped by McGrath's home. Unable to get a response, they asked the East Cleveland police to check on McGrath's welfare. On July 15, uniformed officers stopped by McGrath's house. Also unable to get a response, they entered through an unsecured kitchen door. Inside, they found the house [***3] in shambles and ransacked, with drawers pulled open and their contents dumped on the floor.

Police found McGrath lying on the floor in a downstairs bedroom. Conscious and moaning, McGrath complained of pain and suffering from burns. It appeared from the scene that McGrath had been doused with flammable lamp oil and then set on fire. His face also had dried blood on it. Paramedics rushed McGrath to a hospital.

Treating physicians described the burns as causing a greenish ooze and having the odor of rotting flesh. When first seen by treating physicians, McGrath was severely dehydrated to a degree consistent with having received burns more than twelve hours old. The second degree burns, estimated as covering twelve percent to eighteen percent of his body, extended over his right back, right side, lower and upper left side, both arms and both knees. His initial treating physician, Dr. Bruce Oppenheimer, noted a bruised left eye, apparently caused by a blunt impact injury resulting in swelling and bleeding. That physician believed, with reasonable medical certainty, that because of McGrath's age, and judging by the appearance and odor of the burns, he would not survive these injuries.

 [***4] McGrath died in the hospital of acute bilateral bronchopneumonia on July 23, 1986. Dr. Jay P. Logeman, one of McGrath's treating physicians, noted that the pneumonia was caused by the burn injuries, the lack of early medical treatment for those burns, and the patient's age. The deputy coroner noted crusted abrasions on McGrath's wrist, knee and ankle. He ruled the death a homicide.

McGrath's home revealed compelling circumstantial evidence of what occurred. When found, McGrath wore a blue shirt which covered his burns, but was not scorched itself. Dried fluids were present on the shirt, and a strong odor was emanating from the burns. The trousers he wore were burned. Within a foot of McGrath's body, police found a telephone cord that had a plug on one end and was frayed on the other. On a nearby cabinet, police found a quart bottle of flammable lamp oil with the cap missing. The cap was later found on the bed. Only one third of the oil remained. Charred drapes and a sheet were found on the floor, but police did not see other evidence of a fire.

A burglar had forcibly entered McGrath's home by removing a rear basement window from its frame, leaving a gaping hole. The burglar [***5] had forcibly pried loose an entire panel on the door leading from the basement to the kitchen, splintering the panel into pieces. Evidence of theft or attempted theft abounded, with drawers in the bedroom and kitchen open and their contents dumped. The bed's mattress was askew on the box springs. Police recovered McGrath's Ford Escort in the early morning of July 16.

Various items of evidence linked Lott to this crime. A shoeprint in the dust in McGrath's bedroom generally matched the pattern found on Lott's tennis shoes. Police recovered those shoes from the trunk of Lott's automobile when they arrested him. Police found Lott's fingerprints on a church contribution envelope in McGrath's home. McGrath had received the envelope less than a month before. McGrath did not attend church on Sunday, July 13, though he was very regular in attendance and contributions. Police also found Lott's fingerprint on a dresser in McGrath's bedroom. Lott had driven McGrath's stolen automobile more than twenty-eight hours before police found McGrath. Police arrested Lott on July 30, 1986 based on Coleman's identification.

Lott was indicted on three different occasions. On August 8, 1986 the [***6] grand jury indicted him on two counts of aggravated burglary, two counts of aggravated robbery, felonious assault, two counts of kidnapping, aggravated murder with a felony murder specification, and aggravated arson. On August 12, 1986 the grand jury indicted Lott on four counts of aggravated burglary, felonious assault, four counts of aggravated robbery, kidnapping, gross sexual imposition, and petty theft. Finally, on October 17, 1986, he was re-indicted for aggravated arson. The indictments alleged crimes against six victims, including McGrath, and contained prior felony and violence specifications. After arraignment on each indictment, Lott pled not guilty. Because Lott was indigent, the court appointed counsel to represent him. On June 23, 1987, Lott waived a jury trial. Additionally, at the request of the state, the court severed all counts except those relating to McGrath. The court then ordered the trial to proceed, without Lott's objection, for all offenses against McGrath: aggravated murder; two counts of aggravated burglary, one for the 1983 and one for the 1986 break-in; aggravated robbery; kidnapping; aggravated arson; and petty theft for the 1983 break-in.

On [***7] July 13, 1987, a panel of judges tried Lott for all the offenses relating to McGrath. On July 16, 1987, at the close of the prosecution's case, the court dismissed the kidnapping charge. The prosecution also was permitted to amend the aggravated murder charge and the felony murder specification in order to delete the references to kidnapping. On July 17, the court panel found Lott guilty of the aggravated murder of McGrath, two counts of aggravated burglary, aggravated robbery, aggravated arson, and petty theft. Lott was also found guilty of the death specification of murder during robbery and burglary, and of specifications alleging a prior felony conviction in all charges but the petty theft and one aggravated burglary charge. Following a sentencing hearing, the trial panel determined, beyond a reasonable doubt, that the aggravating circumstance outweighed any mitigating factors. Thereafter, the trial court sentenced Lott to death for the aggravated murder, fined him $ 41,000 and imposed maximum terms of consecutive imprisonment for the other offenses. On March 27, 1989, the court of appeals affirmed the convictions and death sentence, modified the sentence for one of the [***8] aggravated burglary convictions, and disapproved the fines due to Lott's indigency.

The cause is now before this court upon an appeal as of right.
State v. Lott, 1989 Ohio App. LEXIS 969 (Ohio Ct. App., Cuyahoga County, Mar. 16, 1989)



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a decision of the Court of Appeals for Cuyahoga County (Ohio), which affirmed his conviction for murder and sentence of death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of aggravated murder and sentenced to death. Both the conviction and the sentence were affirmed. Defendant appealed, claiming, inter alia, that he was deprived of due process through the joinder of previous aggravated burglary and petty theft offenses that he claimed were unrelated and that prejudiced his cause. Defendant also claimed ineffective assistance of counsel because his attorney did not move for a new trial or move to reopen the trial after new allegedly exculpatory evidence was discovered during the sentencing phase of the trial. The court affirmed, holding that joinder of the previous burglary and petty theft charges was proper because defendant burgled the same house and stole from the same victim, and it was beyond credibility that the panel of judges would be confused by the offenses or would improperly cumulate evidence of the various crimes. The court held there was no merit to the ineffective counsel assertion because any objection by defense counsel to the sentencing panel's receipt of the presentence investigation report would have been for naught because it had the right to receive the report.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction and sentence because joinder of previous burglary and theft charges against the same victim was proper and did not deprive defendant of due process. There was no merit to the assertion of ineffective counsel because any objection to the sentencing panel's receipt of the presentence investigation report would have been for naught, because the panel had the right to receive it.

Doyle v. Ohio Bureau of Motor Vehicles


No. 89-497


Supreme Court of Ohio


51 Ohio St. 3d 46; 554 N.E.2d 97; 1990 Ohio LEXIS 209


March 13, 1990, Submitted  
May 9, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Butler County, No. CA88-05-060.

Appellee, Clyde D. Doyle, was convicted of driving under the influence of alcohol on three separate occasions: December 27, 1983, April 25, 1985, and November 11, 1986. The appellant, Ohio Bureau of Motor Vehicles, notified appellee that because his three DWI convictions were within the immediately preceding three years, his operator's license was being suspended pursuant to R.C. 4507.08(A) and Ohio Adm. Code 4501:1-1-16(B)(1). An administrative hearing on the matter was held at appellee's request. The hearing examiner recommended that the suspension remain in effect, and appellee filed his objections to the examiner's recommendation. The Registrar of the Bureau of Motor Vehicles ("Registrar") overruled the objections and affirmed the suspension of appellee's operator's license.

Appellee sought judicial review of this decision by the Ohio Bureau of Motor Vehicles, and appealed to the common pleas court. On April 5, 1988, the trial court reversed the bureau's decision and held that Ohio Adm. Code 4501:1-1-16(B)(1) was unconstitutional. The court of appeals affirmed the decision of the trial court and also held [***2] that Ohio Adm. Code 4501:1-1-16(B)(1) was unconstitutional. Both courts relied on Gatts v. State (1984), 13 Ohio App. 3d 380, 13 OBR 463, 469 N.E. 2d 562, for the proposition that Ohio Adm. Code 4501:1-1-16(B)(1) violates the due course of law provision of Section 16, Article I of the Ohio Constitution by establishing a conclusive presumption as to the status of the license holder.

This cause is now before this court pursuant to the allowance of a motion to certify the record.
Doyle v. State, 1989 Ohio App. LEXIS 190 (Ohio Ct. App., Clermont County, Jan. 23, 1989)



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed and cause remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: An administrative hearing on appellee driver's license suspension was held at appellee's request. The hearing examiner recommended that the suspension remain in effect and the Registrar of the Bureau of Motor Vehicles affirmed. The Court of Appeals for Butler County, Ohio, affirmed the trial court's ruling that the code under which appellee's license was suspended was unconstitutional. Appellant Ohio Bureau of Motor Vehicles sought review.


OVERVIEW: Appellee's driver's license was suspended pursuant to Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4507.08(A) and Ohio Admin. Code § 4501:1-1-16(B)(1), which defined an "alcoholic" as any person who was convicted three or more times within the immediately preceding three-year period of driving under the influence of intoxicating liquor. The court reversed the lower courts' finding that Ohio Admin. Code § 4501:1-1-16(B)(1) was unconstitutional because the code provided a reasonable definition of one type of alcoholic for the limited purpose of enforcing and administering Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4507.08.The court held that the sole purpose of the post-suspension hearing at issue was to determine whether appellee was in fact convicted three times for DWI in the three immediately preceding years. No purpose would be served by permitting evidence to be presented as to whether the individual was or was not an alcoholic each time appellant proceeded to suspend a license pursuant to Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4507.08. The rule did not violate the due course of law provision of the Ohio Constitution.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and held that the administrative code properly defined "alcoholic" so as to carry out the legislative purpose of the rules promulgated to define who was not entitled to a driver's license and was thus not unconstitutional. The cause was remanded to the trial court.

State ex rel. Beacon Journal Pub. Co. v. Ohio Dep't of Health


No. 89-56


Supreme Court of Ohio


51 Ohio St. 3d 1; 553 N.E.2d 1345; 1990 Ohio LEXIS 197; 18 Media L. Rep. 1454


November 7, 1989, Submitted  
May 2, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL and CROSS-APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Franklin County, No. 86AP-1027.

The Akron Beacon Journal Publishing Company ("Beacon Journal"), appellant and cross-appellee, sought to obtain copies of written inspection reports prepared by investigators from the Ohio Department of Health ("ODH"), concerning their visits to the Dee Maret Nursing Home in Akron. When ODH refused to release the records, Beacon Journal in November 1986 brought a mandamus action in the Court of Appeals for Franklin County against ODH and its Director of Health, Dr. Thomas J. Halpin, appellees and cross-appellants, seeking the release of seven reports pursuant to R.C. 149.43.

In February 1988, the appellate-court referee recommended that the records not be released because they were exempt from disclosure under R.C. 149.43(A)(2)(c) as specific investigatory work product, and under R.C. 149.43(A)(4) as trial preparation records. Beacon Journal objected to these findings, and the court of appeals agreed, granting the writ on September 6, 1988, based largely on our decision in State, ex rel. Natl. Broadcasting Co., v. Cleveland (1988), 38 Ohio St. 3d 79, 526 N.E. 2d 786.

 [***2] On November 22, 1988, the court of appeals denied Beacon Journal's motion for the award of attorney fees. ODH did not appeal the granting of the writ of mandamus, but Beacon Journal appeals the denial of attorney fees. ODH and Halpin filed a cross-appeal asserting that the court of appeals' retroactive application of R.C. 149.43(C) to this case violated Section 28, Article II of the Ohio Constitution. The issue of attorney fees is now before this court upon an appeal as of right.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant publishing company sought review of an order from the Court of Appeals (Ohio), which denied appellant's request for attorney fees in a case against appellee Department of Health regarding appellee's failure to disclosure documents under Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 149.43.


OVERVIEW: Appellant publishing company challenged a decision denying attorney fees relating to appellant's case against appellee Department of Health regarding appellee's failure to disclosure documents under Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 149.43. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that attorney fees were costs, statutes relating to costs were remedial, and, therefore, the attorney fees provision of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 149.43(C) did not violate the constitutional prohibition against retroactive legislation. The court of appeals in this case did not abuse its discretion by denying appellant attorney fees based on its finding that appellee acted in good faith and with a reasonable legal basis for its refusal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed an order denying appellant publishing company attorney fees in a case against appellee Department of Health seeking disclosure of documents. The court held that attorney fee provisions were remedial and did not violate the constitutional prohibition against retroactive legislation.

State ex rel. Baker v. Troutman


No. 89-2044


Supreme Court of Ohio


50 Ohio St. 3d 270; 553 N.E.2d 1053; 1990 Ohio LEXIS 186


February 6, 1990, Submitted  
April 25, 1990, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reporter's Note: Writs of habeas corpus and mandamus were issued in this case in 48 Ohio St. 3d 701, 549 N.E. 2d 524.


PRIOR HISTORY: IN HABEAS CORPUS and MANDAMUS.

ON REQUEST FOR CLARIFICATION.



DISPOSITION: Writs allowed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relators petitioned for writs of habeas corpus and mandamus that requested clarification of a prior order and sought relators' release on bond and a peremptory write of mandamus to require respondents, a sheriff, and others, to nullify a miscellaneous order of the Court of Common Pleas of Summit County (Ohio) as violative of Oh. Const. art. I, § 9.


OVERVIEW: The miscellaneous order of the court of common pleas conditioned the right to bail on an accused's or surety's consent to forfeit the bail for fines and costs, which respondents did not explain or justify in terms of ensuring appearance. Moreover, it provided implicitly for forfeiture upon conviction even though the obligation to appear was fully satisfied. The court viewed its operation as excessive bail under Oh. Const. art. I, § 9 because it placed limiting conditions on bail that were unrelated to appearance of the accused. The court rejected respondents' arguments that the relators had not action in habeas corpus, and ruled that habeas corpus was the proper method of securing relief for excessive pretrial bail under Oh. Const. art. I, § 9, and that the miscellaneous order violated the prohibition against excessive bail. The court stated that the purpose of bail was to ensure the appearance of a defendant at all stages of the criminal proceedings and that conditions that did not relate to appearance were necessarily excessive.


OUTCOME: The court allowed the writs and granted the relief sought by the relators.

 

279 of 2888 DOCUMENTS


State v. Wickline


No. 89-310


Supreme Court of Ohio


50 Ohio St. 3d 114; 552 N.E.2d 913; 1990 Ohio LEXIS 165


February 21, 1990, Submitted  
April 11, 1990, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied May 16, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Franklin County, No. 87AP-46.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a decision of the Court of Appeals for Franklin County (Ohio), which affirmed his conviction and death sentence for murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murdering two people upon the testimony of a witness who was with defendant during the killings but never indicted as an accomplice. On appeal, the court held that the State's failure to provide defendant with alleged exculpatory records prior to trial did not amount to a Brady violation requiring a new trial because the evidence was presented at the trial and the disclosure of the materials before trial would not have affected the trial's outcome. The court rejected defendant's contention that he was convicted solely on the uncorroborated testimony of an accomplice in violation of Ohio Rev. Code § 2923.03(D) because the State's witness was never indicted and thus, she was not an "accomplice" within the meaning of the statute. Further, the court held that because the State's witness was not indicted, she was not a "codefendant" under Ohio R. Crim. P. 16(B)(1)(a), which provided for the pretrial discovery of a codefendant's statements. The court held that sufficient evidence, in particular the eye-witness testimony of the State's witness, was sufficient to sustain defendant's convictions and death sentence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals.

 

280 of 2888 DOCUMENTS


State ex rel. Carpenter v. Indus. Com. of Ohio


No. 89-1606


SUPREME COURT OF OHIO


50 Ohio St. 3d 85; 552 N.E.2d 645; 1990 Ohio LEXIS 159


February 6, 1990, Submitted  
April 11, 1990, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied May 23, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] IN MANDAMUS.



DISPOSITION: Cause dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator claimant brought an intentional tort claim against his employer in his third amended complaint, and a jury set damages. The claimant petitioned for a writ of mandamus with regard to damages and sought to avoid the application of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4121.80. Respondent, the Industrial Commission of Ohio moved to dismiss the petition for failure to state a claim.


OVERVIEW: The claimant filed a third amended complaint against the employer alleging an intentional tort, and a jury set damages. He filed the amended complaint more than one year after the effective date of § 4121.80, which provided for the Commission's exclusive jurisdiction over actions alleging damages based upon an intentional tort of an employer. Section 4121.80(D) gave the Commission exclusive jurisdiction to determine damages. The claimant sought to avoid that jurisdiction and argued that caselaw prohibited using the statute retroactively to destroy a claimant's ability to have a jury calculate damages. The court held that the claimant's precedent did not apply because the operative date for judging retroactivity was the date the action was filed, which was after the statute's effective date. Thus, there was no basis for avoiding the application of § 4121.80. Accordingly, § 4121.80(D) applied and the claimant was bound by the Commission's exclusive authority over the amount of damages.


OUTCOME: The court sustained the motions to dismiss.

 

281 of 2888 DOCUMENTS


Hoops v. United Tel. Co.


No. 88-1871


Supreme Court of Ohio


50 Ohio St. 3d 97; 553 N.E.2d 252; 1990 Ohio LEXIS 157; 52 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 1135; 56 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P40,748


November 22, 1989, Submitted  
April 11, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Wood County, No. WD-87-62.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court of Appeals for Wood County (Ohio) found that Ohio Const. art. I, § 5 did not guarantee plaintiff employee a right to a jury trial in his age discrimination action brought against defendant employer under Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4101.17. The employee appealed.


OVERVIEW: The employee filed an action for equitable relief for age discrimination under Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4101.17 against his employer. The employee argued that Ohio Const. art. I, § 5 guaranteed him a right to a jury trial of his claim. The court found, however, that Ohio Const. art. I, § 5 only provided a right to a jury trial in those cases where such a right existed at common law, prior to the adoption of Ohio Const. art. I, § 5. Although, § 4101.17 did not exist at common law, the employee argued that a right to a jury trial existed at common law for claims sounding in intentional tort and breach of contract. Thus, he argued, that because § 4101.17 provided a remedy for an intentional tort and a breach of contract, he must have a right to a jury trial. The court found, however, that, by his complaint, the employee's action was based only on a violation of § 4101.17, which did not expressly provide for a right to a jury trial and which was enacted after the adoption of Ohio Const. art. I, § 5. Thus, the court found that the employee did not have a right to a jury trial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court.

 

282 of 2888 DOCUMENTS


Record Pub. Co. v. Kainrad


Nos. 88-2042 and 88-2105


Supreme Court of Ohio


49 Ohio St. 3d 296; 551 N.E.2d 1286; 1990 Ohio LEXIS 124


January 10, 1990, Submitted  
March 21, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALS from the Court of Appeals for Portage County, No. 1740.
Record Pub. Co. v. Kainrad, 1988 Ohio App. LEXIS 3936 (Ohio Ct. App., Portage County, Sept. 30, 1988)



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed as modified.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated cases, appellants, a weekly legal newspaper and a daily general newspaper, challenged a judgment of the Court of Appeals for Portage County (Ohio), which held that an order of appellee trial court, amending a court rule by requiring publication of all legal notices in the weekly legal newspaper, unless counsel requested otherwise, was invalid because it violated Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 7.12.


OVERVIEW: The trial court designated the weekly legal newspaper to publish court calendars and other notices required to be published. The weekly newspaper was a weekly publication with a circulation of 251 and did not publish news of "passing events." The daily general newspaper had a circulation of about 25,000 and published news of "passing events." The court held that the trial court's order was invalid because the weekly legal newspaper was not a newspaper of general circulation under § 7.12 and, as such, the amendment to the rules requiring publication in the weekly legal news, unless counsel requested otherwise, was an impermissible limitation under § 7.12. The court further held that the trial court did not have the authority to designate a specific publication to be the exclusive carrier of all notices that were required to be published by law. However, the court held that the trial court's mandate as to the publication of its own trial court calendar in the weekly legal news was a valid exercise of the trial court's inherent authority under Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2701.09 because there was no daily law journal published in the county of Ohio where the trial court was located.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment to provide that the weekly legal newspaper was not a paper of general circulation and, as modified, affirmed the judgment.

South Euclid v. Richardson


No. 88-1885


Supreme Court of Ohio


49 Ohio St. 3d 147; 551 N.E.2d 606; 1990 Ohio LEXIS 105


November 22, 1989, Submitted  
March 7, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALS from the Court of Appeals for Cuyahoga County, Nos. 54247 and 54248.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a consolidated action, appellant city challenged the judgment of the Court of Appeals for Cuyahoga County (Ohio), which affirmed the dismissal of criminal charges against appellee business owners under a city ordinance which made it a misdemeanor for any person to own, operate, maintain or manage a brothel.


OVERVIEW: The charges against the business owners were brought under a city ordinance prohibiting brothels. In dismissing the charges against them, the municipal court agreed that although the term "brothel" was defined in the ordinance, the ordinance was unduly vague and overbroad and thus void. The court of appeals affirmed the dismissal. Upon review, the court adopted the opinion of the court of appeals. Though the ordinance prohibited maintaining or operating a place "for the purposes of engaging in group sex, sexual conduct and sexual acts, as herein defined," no definitions were included. The court found that while the term "group sex" could be understandable to individuals of reasonable intelligence, the ordinance's failure to define either "sexual conduct" or "sexual acts" left citizens without notice of what activity either encompassed. Thus, the ordinance was unduly vague. The court also found that if the definition of "sexual conduct" in Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2907.01(A) was incorporated into the ordinance, it would have prohibited sexual activities between a married, heterosexual couple. Thus, the ordinance was overly broad.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment holding the city's ordinance prohibiting brothels to be void.

 

284 of 2888 DOCUMENTS


American Home Products Corp. v. Limbach


Nos. 88-1252 and 88-1318


Supreme Court of Ohio


49 Ohio St. 3d 158; 551 N.E.2d 201; 1990 Ohio LEXIS 100


November 7, 1989, Submitted  
March 7, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALS from the Board of Tax Appeals, Nos. 87-J-95, 87-B-96, 87-A-97, 87-F-98 and 87-A-99.



DISPOSITION: Decision affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporation challenged the judgment from the Board of Tax Appeals which upheld the apportionment and tax of disputed investment income. Appellee tax commissioner challenged the judgment which placed on her the burden to prove that a nexus existed.


OVERVIEW: Ohio levied a franchise tax on foreign corporations for the privilege of doing business in Ohio and levied the tax on the net worth or net income basis. An apportionment formula attempted to attribute to Ohio the extent of the corporation's business activity in Ohio and to fairly tax the foreign corporation. The corporation argued that the Due Process Clause prohibited Ohio from taxing investment income because the investment activity producing the income took place entirely outside Ohio and because the payors were discrete business entities. The tax commissioner responded that the income was available as working capital and apportioning the income to Ohio did not violate the Due Process Clause. The court held that Ohio could not constitutionally apportion and then tax the disputed investment income. The court found that the corporation's manufacturing, warehousing, and selling in Ohio established a minimal connection with the state but the corporation's independence from the payors of the investment income broke the nexus for the investment income. On the tax commissioner's cross-appeal the court found that the tax commissioner had not rebutted the evidence breaking the nexus.


OUTCOME: The court reversed that part of the judgment which upheld the apportionment and tax of the corporation's disputed investment income. The court affirmed that part of the judgment which placed on the tax commissioner the burden to prove that a nexus existed.

State Farm Mut. Ins. Co. v. Blevins


No. 88-1664


Supreme Court of Ohio


49 Ohio St. 3d 165; 551 N.E.2d 955; 1990 Ohio LEXIS 103


November 7, 1989, Submitted  
 March 7, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Warren County, No. CA87-12-103.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant insured sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals for Warren County (Ohio) holding that arbitrators hearing a personal injury automobile accident case exceeded their power when they made an award directing appellee insurer to pay punitive damages to the insured.


OVERVIEW: The insured received an award of punitive damages arising from bodily injury from an arbitration board under the uninsured motorist provision of his insurance policy arising from an automobile accident. On review, the appellate court found the award inappropriate. On appeal, the court noted an arbitrator's powers are limited by the bounds of the agreement from which he draws his authority. In the case at bar the court found the uninsured motorist provision of the relevant policy promised to pay damages for bodily injury. The court held that provision clearly limited the insurer's liability to compensatory damages for personal injury. It was not a contractual undertaking to insure against punitive damages. The court held that the arbitrators exceeded their power when they made an award for punitive damages.


OUTCOME: The court held that in the absence of specific contractual language, coverage for punitive or exemplary damages would not be presumed under a provision for uninsured motorist coverage. Therefore, the court affirmed the lower court's decision.

Sedar v. Knowlton Constr. Co.


No. 88-1569


Supreme Court of Ohio


49 Ohio St. 3d 193; 551 N.E.2d 938; 1990 Ohio LEXIS 99


October 25, 1989, Submitted  
March 7, 1990, Decided 3


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Cuyahoga County, No. 54931.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant injured individual sought review of an order from the Court of Appeals for Cuyahoga County (Ohio), which upheld the constitutionality of a statute of repose for tort actions against architects, construction contractors, and others performing design and construction services for improvements to real property. The repose statute was found in Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2305.131.


OVERVIEW: Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2305.131 barred tort actions against architects, construction contractors and others who performed services related to the design and construction of improvements to real property, where such action was brought more than 10 years following the completion of the services. In addition to his due process and equal protection assertions against the statute, the injured individual asserted that a discovery rule should be incorporated into the statute for injuries caused by static conditions in a building. The court held that: (1) the discovery rule had no application in the construction case setting, (2) the statute did not violate due process because the legislature's choice of a 10 year cut-off for construction related tort actions was neither unreasonable nor arbitrary, and the statute satisfied a proper public purpose of preventing stale litigation and its attendant risks, (3) the statute did not violate the "open court" and "right to remedy" provisions of the Ohio Constitution, and (4) a rational basis existed for the legislative classifications in the statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order finding that the construction statute of repose was constitutional.

Menefee v. Queen City Metro


No. 88-2095


Supreme Court of Ohio


49 Ohio St. 3d 27; 550 N.E.2d 181; 1990 Ohio LEXIS 50


December 5, 1989, Submitted  
February 14, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Hamilton County, No. C-870641.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed and cause remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of a judgment of the Court of Appeals for Hamilton County (Ohio), which reversed a judgment in favor of the city in an action filed by appellee insurers regarding the constitutionality of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2744.05. The statute was promulgated to limit the damages recoverable against political subdivisions and disallowed subrogation claims.


OVERVIEW: The insurers contended that Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2744.05(B) denied equal protection of the law to insurance companies that asserted claims against political subdivisions under subrogation provisions of an insurance contract because the statute did not permit subrogation claims against a political subdivision. The court of appeals found that it was violative of equal protection and reversed the trial court's judgment. On appeal, the court, in reversing the decision of the court of appeals, held that the statute was not unconstitutional. The statutory classification did not involve a suspect class or a fundamental right, and it did not violate the Equal Protection Clause of the Ohio or United States Constitutions if it bore a rational relationship to a legitimate governmental interest. The purpose of the statute was to conserve the fiscal resources of political subdivisions by limiting their tort liability, and it permitted injured persons who had not source of reimbursement for their damages to recover for a tort committed by the political subdivision. The court concluded that the state had a valid interest in preserving the financial soundness of its political subdivisions.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals and remanded the cause to the trial court for disposition in accordance with the court's opinion.

Ohio State Univ. Hosp. v. Kinkaid


No. 88-1744


Supreme Court of Ohio


48 Ohio St. 3d 78; 549 N.E.2d 517; 1990 Ohio LEXIS 35



January 31, 1990, Decided; November 14, 1989, Submitted; As Amended; Rehearing denied February 28, 1990


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Franklin County. No. 88-AP-261.

On December 8, 1986, the Ohio State University Hospital, appellant, filed a complaint in the Court of Common Pleas of Franklin County naming appellee, Georgene Kinkaid, as the defendant. The complaint alleged that medical services, rendered to appellee's deceased husband, Dean J. Kinkaid, and unpaid in the amount of $ 7,099.20, were the obligation of appellee pursuant to R.C. 3103.03.

The decedent was admitted to Ohio State University Hospital on May 30, 1984 and remained there until his death on July 7, 1984. Dean Kinkaid died leaving no estate. As a result, appellant sought payment from appellee of the remaining amount due for the medical services rendered the decedent.

On January 22, 1988, appellee filed a motion for summary judgment, claiming she was not liable for the amount due. Appellant admitted that the appellant contracted with appellee for the services rendered. The trial court granted summary judgment to appellee. On August 3, 1988, the court of appeals affirmed the decision of the trial court.

The cause is now before this court upon the allowance of a motion to certify the record.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed  [***2] and cause remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant hospital sought review of an order of the Court of Appeals for Franklin County (Ohio), which affirmed the trial court's order that granted summary judgment to appellee widow in the hospital's suit to collect from the widow medical bills incurred in the last illness of the widow's deceased husband.


OVERVIEW: The hospital filed suit against the widow to recover payment for medical services incurred by her deceased husband during his final illness. The husband's estate did not contain sufficient assets to pay the bill. The widow filed a motion for summary judgment, contending that she had not personally contracted for the services, had not pledged her own credit, and had not refused to obtain the needed services for her husband. The trial court granted summary judgment to the widow, and the hospital appealed. The court held that where a husband was unable to provide for his own support pursuant to Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 3103.03, a wife had to aid in the support of her husband to the extent that she was able. The determination as to a wife's ability to aid in the support of her husband was a matter to be decided within the sound discretion of the trial court. Medical expenses were necessaries and, as such, were included as part of any definition of support under § 3103.03.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the grant of summary judgment to the widow in the hospital's suit to recover payment of medical services rendered to the widow's deceased husband.

State v. Corbin


NO. 75627


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, EIGHTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, CUYAHOGA COUNTY


1999 Ohio App. LEXIS 6386



December 30, 1999, Date of Announcement of Decision


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] Character of Proceeding: Civil appeal from Court of Common Pleas. Case No. CR-16867.



DISPOSITION: Judgment: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the order of the Cuyahoga County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio), which denied his petition for post-conviction relief seeking review of his 1975 felonious assault conviction.


OVERVIEW: Defendant petitioned the trial court for review of his 1975 felonious assault conviction. Since defendant was convicted in 1975, Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2953(A)(2) and Am. Sub. S.B. 4 § 3 applied. Therefore, he was required to file his petition by September 21, 1996. Since he did not file until December 5, 1997, his petition was untimely. The trial court did not have jurisdiction to hear defendant's petition, since he was unable to show that he was unavoidably prevented from discovering that his trial counsel failed to timely file his appeal, that plaintiff committed misconduct at trial, or that the victim committed perjury, since these facts were easily discoverable by defendant at trial. Nor was defendant able to show by clear and convincing evidence that but for these errors he would not have been convicted. Therefore, defendant's petition was properly dismissed.


OUTCOME: Order affirmed, because the trial court lacked jurisdiction to hear defendant's untimely post-conviction petition since defendant failed to show that he was unavoidably prevented from discovering the errors made at trial, and that but for these errors he would not have been convicted.

Smith v. Liberty Twp. Bd. of Trustees


Case No. 99CA-H-05-023


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DELAWARE COUNTY


1999 Ohio App. LEXIS 6437



December 30, 1999, Date of Judgment Entry


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] CHARACTER OF PROCEEDING: Civil Appeal from the Delaware County Court of Common Pleas. Case 98-CV-F-10-344.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, township board of trustees, appealed from the Delaware County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio), which reversed a decision of the Delaware County Board of Commissioners and granted appellees' petition for annexation to the City of Delaware.


OVERVIEW: Appellees petitioned for annexation. The Delaware County Board of Commissioners denied appellees' petition based on a finding that the annexation territory was unreasonably large and that annexation would not serve the general good of the territory sought to be annexed. Trial court reversed and granted appellees' petition. Appellants, township board of trustees, claimed the trial court erred when it concluded that the territory was sufficiently adjacent to the city of Delaware. Nothing about the size or shape of the territory prevented its annexation. Based on territory's character, shape and size, there was evidence to support determination that annexation territory was not unreasonably large. There was no evidence that annexation of the territory would disrupt the remainder of the townships. Trial court did not error in holding that the decision that annexation would not serve the general good of the territory was unsupported by substantial, reliable and probative evidence.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed. There was a preponderance of evidence that the territory sought to be annexed was adjacent to city and that annexation complied with concept of municipality as a unified body. Decision of Board of Commissioners was not supported by preponderance of reliable, probative and substantial evidence.

State v. Miller


Case No. 1999 AP 02 0010


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, TUSCARAWAS COUNTY


1999 Ohio App. LEXIS 6543



December 30, 1999, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] CHARACTER OF PROCEEDING: Criminal Appeal from Tuscarawas County Court of Common Pleas. Case 1996CR12 0335.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a Tuscarawas County Common Pleas Court (Ohio) judgment correcting his original sentence to provide for three years' incarceration after revocation of his community control release. He argued the sentence was invalid for failure to comply with Ohio Rev. Code § 2929.19(B)(5) at original sentencing and violated double jeopardy.


OVERVIEW: At defendant's sentencing after guilty plea to robbery, the trial court explained all possible sentences and sentenced him to release under community control, explaining that revocation would result in a prison sentence. The sentencing court did not explicitly impose a three-year sentence before ordering community control, so defendant argued after revocation that this sentence violated applicable statute because it was not specifically mentioned at the hearing pursuant to Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2929.19(B)(3), and that it violated the United States and Ohio Constitutions' double jeopardy by modifying his sentence to impose a longer one. The court held that the full explanation of possible sentences at the sentencing hearing satisfied statutory requirements, and defendant's sentence fell within the range explained at that time. The consequences of revocation of community control release were part of defendant's original sentence, so that sentence's modification to provide a term of incarceration was not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the modified sentence, because trial court informed defendant about potential sentences upon revocation of community control at the time of original guilty plea and sentence. There was no constitutional violation because the original sentence provided for imposition of a prison sentence upon violation of community control terms.

State v. Bretz


Case No. CA-98-001


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, HOLMES COUNTY


1999 Ohio App. LEXIS 6443



December 30, 1999, Date of Judgment Entry


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] CHARACTER OF PROCEEDING: Criminal Appeal from the Holmes County Court of Common Pleas. Case No. 96-CR-048.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant filed a criminal appeal from a judgment of the Holmes County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio), which found appellant incompetent to stand trial and not restorable to competency, and committed appellant to psychiatric hospitalization and retained jurisdiction over the commitment for the remainder of appellant's life.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, indicted for serious sexual offenses, pled not guilty by reason of insanity. The trial court found appellant was repeatedly sexually abused children, threatening violence to avoid detection, and suffered from mental illness. The court committed him to psychiatric hospitalization, retaining jurisdiction over appellant for the maximum prison term for felonious sexual penetration by force which was life. Appellant appealed. The court affirmed, holding that the trial court was allowed to continue appellant's treatment which did not constitutionally amount to punishment. The duration of appellant's compelled hospitalization was limited by the maximum imprisonment for the crime for which he was charged and unless appellant was found competent or no longer subject to psychiatric hospitalization, the trial court could maintain jurisdiction over his commitment for life. Due process rights were not violated since the nature and duration of appellant's commitment were reasonably related to the purpose for which he was committed.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed because the nature and duration of appellant's commitment were reasonably related to the purpose for which he was committed, due process rights were not violated. Appellant's commitment was limited to the maximum imprisonment for the crime for which he was charged, and the trial court properly retained jurisdiction.

In re Griffin


CASE NO. CA99-01-020


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, TWELFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, BUTLER COUNTY


1999 Ohio App. LEXIS 6278



December 27, 1999, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [*1] Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother challenged the decision of the Butler County Court of Common Pleas, Juvenile Division (Ohio), which granted permanent custody of her minor daughter to the Butler County Children Services Board.


OVERVIEW: Appellant's daughter was placed in the permanent custody of the Butler County Children Services Board (BCCSB). Appellant challenged, arguing that Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2151.414 violated her right to equal protection because it allowed the child of a disabled person to be taken by the state without accommodations being made to meet the parent's disability. Maass v. Maass


CASE NO. 98 CA 190


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, SEVENTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, MAHONING COUNTY


1999 Ohio App. LEXIS 6431



December 27, 1999, Decided  
December 27, 1999, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] CHARACTER OF PROCEEDINGS: Civil Appeal from Mahoning County Common Pleas Court, Case No. 95 CV 390.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant brought this appeal from the Mahoning County Common Pleas Court (Ohio), which awarded prejudgment interest in favor of appellee based on appellant's failure to make a good faith effort to settle the parties' negligence suit.


OVERVIEW: Appellee was injured when appellant was attempting to throw a flaming pan out of a house. Appellee filed a negligence suit against appellant. An arbitrator's report found in favor of appellee. The case unsuccessfully went through mediation, and a jury eventually found in favor of appellee. Appellant claimed the lower court erred in granting prejudgment interest to appellee. The case was a negligence action and was governed by Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 1343.03(C). Appellant argued that he did not fail to make a good faith effort to settle the case because he objectively believed that he was not liable. Appellant never once offered to settle, even after an arbitrator found in favor of appellee. Determination that appellant failed to make a good faith effort to settle was not unreasonable, arbitrary or unconscionable so as to constitute an abuse of discretion.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed. Appellant's failure to make any efforts to settle after being put on notice by adverse arbitration ruling justified lower court's imposition of prejudgment interest. Determination that appellant failed to make a good faith effort to settle was not unreasonable, arbitrary or unconscionable.

 

298 of 2888 DOCUMENTS


State v. Buoscio


CASE NO. 98-CA-7


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, SEVENTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, MAHONING COUNTY


1999 Ohio App. LEXIS 6436



December 27, 1999, Decided  
December 27, 1999, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] CHARACTER OF PROCEEDINGS: Criminal Appeal from Mahoning County Court of Common Pleas. Case No. CR-22759.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged judgment from the Mahoning County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio), denying appellant's second petition for postconviction relief as untimely.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, 30 years after his conviction, petitioned for relief. More than one year after it was denied he filed a second petition. The court held application of the 180-day statute of limitations under Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2953(A)(2), as amended, was not unconstitutional as applied. The legislature intended the statute to apply retroactively, and the statute, as amended, provided appellant one year from its enactment within which to file his petition. This was a reasonable time in which appellant could have asserted his right to petition the lower court. The court held Ohio Rev. Code Ann § 2953.21(A)(2) was not retroactive in violation of Ohio Const. art. II § 28 which proscribed the Ohio General Assembly from passing retroactive laws.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed; appellant's petition for post-conviction relief was untimely since more than 30 years passed from the date of conviction to the first filing of petition for relief and he filed his second petition more than one year after the effective date of amended Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2953.21.

State v. Burnett


APPEAL NO. C-981003


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, HAMILTON COUNTY


1999 Ohio App. LEXIS 6216



December 23, 1999, Date of Judgment Entry on Appeal


NOTICE:

 [*1] THESE ARE NOT OFFICIAL HEADNOTES AND OR SYLLABI AND ARE NEITHER APPROVED IN ADVANCE NOR ENDORSED BY THE COURT. PLEASE REVIEW THE CASE IN FULL.


PRIOR HISTORY: Criminal Appeal From: Hamilton County Municipal Court. TRIAL NO. 98CRB-23819.



DISPOSITION: Judgment Appealed From Is: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal defendant appealed his conviction by the Hamilton County Municipal Court (Ohio) for criminal trespass.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested for a drug-related offense in an area that had been designated by ordinance as a "Drug-Exclusion Zone." Defendant was issued a "Notice of Exclusion" that prohibited him from entering the area for 90 days. Following defendant's conviction for the drug offense, he was issued a second notice prohibiting him from entering the area for one-year and violation would subject defendant to prosecution for criminal trespass. Defendant was observed in the area, arrested and convicted of criminal trespass. Defendant appealed alleging the one-year exclusion violated his right of peaceable assembly, right of intrastate travel, and usurped the authority of the state legislature. Affirming, the court held the ordinance was not overbreadth as it had a legitimate application and did not encroach substantially on his right to assemble. Further, there was no constitutional right to intrastate travel and the ordinance was not a criminal penalty, rather, a civil sanction.


OUTCOME: Affirmed; the ordinance was not overbreadth as it had a legitimate application and did not encroach substantially on his right to assemble. Further, there was no constitutional right to intrastate travel and the ordinance was not a criminal penalty, rather, a civil sanction.

State v. Wiley


Court of Appeals No. L-98-1212


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, SIXTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, LUCAS COUNTY


1999 Ohio App. LEXIS 6232



December 23, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] Trial Court No. TRC-9705892.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of Sylvania Municipal Court (Ohio) which found appellant guilty of failure to keep reasonable control of her vehicle, a violation of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4511.202, and of driving under the influence of drugs of abuse, a violation of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4511.19(A)(1).


OVERVIEW: Because appellant did not identify instance where inaudible portions of transcript resulted in prejudice, and did not attempt to use procedures provided to add to or modify the record, her claims of prejudice were waived. The trooper's observations of two flat tires, rubber missing from one tire, smoke coming from under vehicle, slow rate of speed, no lights, scrapes and dents in the body of vehicle, and appellant driving her heavily damaged vehicle in the median of the highway, justified his stop. Appellant's slow responses and lack of balance, inability to remain still, slurred speech and lethargy, 10 pills missing from recently filled prescription that listed one pill as the appropriate dose, appellant's damaged car and the location of her car when he found her, formed probable cause for arresting appellant for driving under the influence of drugs of abuse and for failure to maintain reasonable control of a vehicle.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed, because the condition of the car and its slow rate of speed, justified the officer's stop; and the appellant's slurred speech and lethargy, formed probable cause for her arrest..

 

301 of 2888 DOCUMENTS


State v. Baksi


CASE NO. 98-T-0123


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, ELEVENTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, TRUMBULL COUNTY


1999 Ohio App. LEXIS 6271



December 23, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] CHARACTER OF PROCEEDINGS: Criminal Appeal from the Court of Common Pleas. Case No. 98 CR 236.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his conviction by the Court of Common Pleas, Trumbull County (Ohio), for involuntary manslaughter pursuant to Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2903.04(A), corrupting another with drugs pursuant to Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2925.02(A)(3)(C)(1), and trafficking in heroin pursuant to Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2925.03(A) and (C)(6)(a).


OVERVIEW: Appellant inmate provided his cellmate with a syringe of heroin. The dosage was fatal to the cellmate. Appellant was convicted of involuntary manslaughter, corrupting another with drugs, and trafficking in heroin. Appellant challenged his conviction, arguing appellee state was barred from criminally prosecuting him because he had already been punished by prison authorities for the same offense when he was placed in administrative segregation. Appellant also argued he was subjected to multiple punishments for the same conduct when he was convicted of both involuntary manslaughter and corrupting another with drugs. Affirming, the court held imposed prison administrative sanctions did not raise double jeopardy protections barring a prosecution for the statutory offense arising from the same course of conduct for which the disciplinary measures were taken. Further, involuntary manslaughter and corrupting another with drugs were not allied offenses because each offense required proof of an element that the other did not.


OUTCOME: Affirmed; imposed prison administrative sanctions did not raise double jeopardy protections barring prosecution for statutory offense of involuntary manslaughter because such sanctions were not punishment. Further, involuntary manslaughter and corrupting another with drugs were not allied offenses because each offense required proof of an element that the other did not.
